1.1.6.5.3.1.1.6.3.1. Con: An enhanced moral reputation could allow countries to have greater influence in international negotiation, thanks to their moral high ground and the respect they gain from other countries.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.6.3.1.1. Con: Accepting many refugees could also alienate countries who oppose the general idea of allowing in refugees - for example, Hungary and Poland - and thus reduce a country's international influence.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.6.3.1.1.1. Con: Countries who oppose taking in refugees have no reason to oppose other countries taking them in. In fact, other countries taking in refugees benefits them by reducing their burden to take refugees.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.6.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.6.1.3.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7. Con: Allowing a huge number of refugees into a country is a safety risk.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1. Pro: Letting in a lot of unknown people into a country is a bad idea. All that a country can follow is a paper trail, and that makes it difficult to screen the individuals coming in effectively.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1. Pro: It is virtually impossible to vet refugees thoroughly. This leaves the door open for terrorists to migrate to a country posing as refugees.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.1. Pro: Local intelligence agencies find it [really hard](http://www.dailymail.co.uk/news/article-3283587/FBI-admits-s-no-way-screen-Syrian-refugees-Obama-administration-plans-accept-US.html) to thoroughly check the backgrounds on refugees. This leaves a lot of room for unknown people to enter the country who may possibly even be terrorists.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.2. Con: Accepting Syrian refugees is [good for US security](http://www.huffingtonpost.com/david-bier/five-reasons-refugees-are_b_9534010.html): It undermines ISIS' propaganda and might bring valuable intelligence.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.3. Con: Countries can choose to only admit refugees that can be vetted thoroughly.  Often refugees do have reliable documentation and can be meaningfully vetted.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.4. Con: Even if individuals cannot be screened effectively, if the overall risk of terrorism is low, this is not an increased safety risk.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.4.1. Pro: At least in the case of the United States, refugees have been found to pose [no](https://www.cato.org/publications/policy-analysis/terrorism-immigration-risk-analysis) safety risk with respect to terrorism.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.4.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.2.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.4.1.2. Pro: According to [historical research](https://www.cato.org/publications/policy-analysis/terrorism-immigration-risk-analysis), the chance of an American being murdered in a terrorist attack caused by a refugee is 1 in 3.64 billion per year.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.4.1.3. Pro: The magnitude of crime and terrorism committed by refugees specifically \(rather than migrants or ethnic minorities in general\) is tiny. There is considerable media bias, compounded by psychological bias \(xenophobia\) that makes the threat [seem worse](https://qz.com/898207/the-psychology-of-why-americans-are-more-scared-of-terrorism-than-guns-though-guns-are-3210-times-likelier-to-kill-them/) than it is.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.4.2. Pro: Fears of terrorism are typically [irrational](https://qz.com/898207/the-psychology-of-why-americans-are-more-scared-of-terrorism-than-guns-though-guns-are-3210-times-likelier-to-kill-them/), and are not supported by any evidence.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.2. Con: Much of the research on the [causal relationship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immigration_and_crime) between immigration and crime has been limited due to weak instruments for determining causality. This means it is very uncertain whether or not refugees pose a safety risk.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.2.1. Con: When some migrant groups are known to have higher crime rates it is not so relevant for the pro/con of "letting them in" that we do not know precisely *why* they have higher crime rates.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.2.2. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.2.1.1.2.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.2.3. Pro: As refugees are demographically similar to at least some groups of immigrants, research on immigration and crime should carry over to a significant extent.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.2.3.1. Con: As non-refugee immigrants are generally people who arrive somewhat prepared to their new country, their living conditions are likely to be a lot better than a refugee who is without social and cultural connections, as well as any form of starting capital.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.2.3.2. Con: Even if refugees are demographically similar to some subsets of immigrants, this does not mean that the research on immigration and crime also applies to refugees. Perhaps some demographic groups of immigrants do increase crime but their effect is outweighed by the positive effect of other immigrants, and refugees are demographically similar to the crime-increasing group.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.3. Pro: Some refugees hold highly illiberal views / beliefs. If we simply accept any refugee who seeks entry, some will believe that the penalty for homosexuality should be death. Others will believe that apostates should be killed. Still others will believe that women do not deserve the same rights as men. People with beliefs such as these just cannot be safely allowed into a western culture that abhors these illiberal views.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.3.1. Con: The personal views and opinions of refugees should not matter as long as their behavior corresponds to the laws and norms of the societies that take them in.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.3.1.1. Con: High-income countries should try to create and preserve a socially liberal consensus, in order to ensure tolerance and equality.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.3.1.2. Con: Personal views are commonly seen as one of the motivators of behavior.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.3.2. Con: There are many native citizens in high-income countries that hold illiberal views that are anti-LGBTQ+, that women aren't equal to men, and racist beliefs, yet those people aren't kicked out from their country as preventative measures.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.3.2.1. Con: Nevertheless, it is problematic to add ever more people in high-income countries with illiberal views.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4. Pro: Many refugees are terrorists.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.1. Con: Even if this were true, the threat to life posed by not allowing refugees into a country far outweighs that posed by terrorism. In 2016 alone, [over 5,000 refugees died](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/dec/23/record-migrant-death-toll-two-boats-capsize-italy-un-refugee) attempting to make it to Europe, a figure which on its own far exceeds terrorism deaths in Europe [since 2000](https://www.washingtonpost.com/graphics/world/a-history-of-terrorism-in-europe/).
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.1.1. Con: Just because some refugees die on the way to Europe doesn't make those who arrive any less dangerous \(if one assumes that they are actually dangerous\).
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.1.1.1. Con: The citizens of European countries should be more concerned with domestic threats.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.1.1.1.1. Pro: The majority of terrorists in Western countries are [second generation immigrants](https://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/europe/2005-07-01/europes-angry-muslims), which implies that it is not the refugees that are the problem but the countries' poor immigrant integration policies that leave the next generation feeling isolated from their parents' adopted culture.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.1.1.1.1.1. Con: You can not blame a country for not cleaning up a mess you created.  Immigrants are less exposed what is happening. Immigrant children from western countries did not need programs to make them like the west. You are talking about mind control and brainwashing the children of Muslims to make them like the host country.  Every immigrant child must chose one country of the other. The gap between the west and islam is too great. Living in the west for them is torture.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.1.1.1.1.1.1. Con: Immigrant integration policies do not serve the purpose of "brainwashing" immigrants so that they are more like their Western counterparts, but rather are intended to provide services so that immigrants can find decent housing, jobs, and feel welcome in their new communities.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.2. Con: Taking in refugees does not necessarily entail having open borders. High income states could take in refugees whilst still having robust border security checks capable of catching any terrorists.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.2.1. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.3. Pro: The US issued an [executive order](https://www.whitehouse.gov/the-press-office/2017/01/27/executive-order-protecting-nation-foreign-terrorist-entry-united-states) to protect itself from terrorists entering the country. Among the target groups banned from immigration are refugees.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.3.1. Con: Fear that refugees are terrorists does not mean that refugees actually are terrorists.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.3.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.4.2.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.3.2. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.1.1.1.1.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.4. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.4.1.1.1.1.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.5. Pro: A significant number of Muslims worldwide - according to [estimates](http://www.pewforum.org/2013/04/30/the-worlds-muslims-religion-politics-society-app-a/) about 11% - believe that violence in the name of Islam is often or sometimes justified.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.5.1. Con: Only 1% of US Muslims, and 3% of Muslims world-wide [say](http://www.pewforum.org/2013/04/30/the-worlds-muslims-religion-politics-society-app-a/) suicide bombings or other violence against civilian targets are often justified.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.5.2. Pro: According to a [Gallup](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muslim_attitudes_toward_terrorism#Polls) poll in 2011, over 18% of Muslims said military attacks on civilians were justified.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.5.3. Pro: 2/3 of British Muslims would [not inform](https://www.express.co.uk/news/uk/659913/two-in-three-British-Muslims-would-NOT-give-police-terror-tip-offs) the police if somebody close to them had gotten involved with terrorist sympathizers.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.6. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.2.1.1.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.7. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.6.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.8. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.1.1.4.1.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9. Pro: Taking in refugees may spark a political backlash, which increases political instability and the risk of conflict.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.1. Con: Most countries can take care of a public backlash against unpopular policies within the confines of their political systems, for example by punishing the incumbent party. Backlash does by no means automatically create instability or conflict.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.2. Con: Refugees, who are by definition desperately fleeing existential threats, and those who support their continued existence, should not be held responsible for the lashing back by xenophobic elements of the population.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3. Pro: In many high income countries, particularly in ones that are threatened by terror, accepting refugees [has reinforced prejudices](http://www.pewresearch.org/fact-tank/2016/09/16/european-opinions-of-the-refugee-crisis-in-5-charts/) against the ethnic and religious minority groups that those refugees are a part of.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.1. Con: People reinforce their own prejudices by subscribing only to feeds that confirm their existing biases and which often have political designs to do so.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.2. Con: Between the options of taking in refugees and exposing them to animosity in high-income countries, and not taking in refugees on the other hand, the second option is most likely still in the best interest of refugees.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.3. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.10.3.1.4.4.2.3.5.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.4. Pro: In Europe, after accepting refugees, negative attitudes towards refugees rose, with a [median of 59%](http://www.pewresearch.org/fact-tank/2016/09/16/european-opinions-of-the-refugee-crisis-in-5-charts/) of Europeans believing refugees are a terror threat, and an [overwhelming majority](http://www.pewresearch.org/fact-tank/2016/09/16/european-opinions-of-the-refugee-crisis-in-5-charts/) opposing the current policy of nations that accept refugees.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.5. Pro: In many high income countries, taking in refugees has ignited hostile anti-immigrant, anti-refugee rhetoric from both citizens and politicians, [such as in Poland, France, Hungary, and the United States.](https://www.nytimes.com/2016/09/06/world/europe/denmark-migrants-refugees-racism.html)
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.5.1. Pro: In the United States, hate crimes against Muslims [increased](http://www.newsweek.com/fbi-hate-crimes-are-down-us-not-against-muslims-395578) following the acceptance of Syrian refugees.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.5.1.1. Con: Correlation does not imply causation. This increase in hate crimes against Muslims took place in a general climate of anti-Muslim rhetoric that existed before refugees were ever accepted, as well as in a context of increased publicity surrounding the action of groups like ISIS which many associate with Islam.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.5.2. Pro: Europe has seen a [surge](http://www.thedailybeast.com/europe-prepares-for-anti-refugee-violence) in anti-refugee violence following the acceptance of refugees and the political backlash against them.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.5.3. Con: This is an argument for better hate crimes legislation or minority civil rights protections, not that refugees should not be accepted.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.5.3.1. Con: A population's freedom and liberty to express its discontent shouldn't be affected by the presence of foreign elements in its midst. Restricting these freedoms creates a totalitarian state where the citizens have no voice.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.5.4. Con: Poland and Hungary have [barely taken in any refugees](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/jun/13/eu-takes-action-against-eastern-states-for-refusing-to-take-refugees). Anti-refugee sentiment within them predates any refugee intake.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.3.5.4.1. Con: The anti-refugee rhetoric might be a reaction to the prospect of following in the footsteps of other countries that took in refugees. In that sense, it may well be a reaction to high-income countries taking in refugees.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4. Pro: The backlash that has come from accepting refugees has helped galvanize far-right populist parties, and has allowed them to advance their agenda on a range of issues.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.1. Con: In France, Marine Le Pen was [crushed](https://fivethirtyeight.com/features/donald-trump-is-making-europe-liberal-again/) in a landslide by Emmanuel Macron in June. Austria's right-wing Freedom Party was [defeated](https://fivethirtyeight.com/features/donald-trump-is-making-europe-liberal-again/) earlier in 2017, and a far-right challenge in the Netherlands election of 2017 was similarly [defeated.](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/mar/16/europes-governments-signal-relief-as-dutch-election-defeats-far-right)
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.1.1. Con: Le Pen and the Freedom Party both made it to runoff elections; this indicates that they are a significant force in the politics of these countries.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.2. Con: In the United Kingdom, the anti-immigrant UKIP suffered historical losses in the [2017 general election](http://www.bbc.com/news/election/2017/results/england).
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.2.1. Con: UKIP suffered losses because it was seen as having fulfilled its purpose: namely, initiated a UK exit from the EU.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.3. Con: Despite the election of Donald Trump, little of the most radical and populist elements of his agenda have been passed by Congress.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.3.1. Con: Trump has been in office for less than one year. Many large-scale policy changes need time and might be implemented during the remainder of his term in office.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.3.2. Con: Thanks to Trump's executive orders, arrests of undocumented immigrants for illegally residing in the US have [increased massively](https://www.theatlantic.com/news/archive/2017/05/under-trump-immigrants-arrests-are-up-but-deportation-is-down/527103/).
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.3.2.1. Con: Deportations were higher [in parts of](http://www.newsweek.com/illegal-immigration-undocumented-migrants-obama-trump-585726) Obama's presidency than they have been in Trump's.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.3.3. Con: Trump withdrew from the Paris Agreement on climate change, a move which is incredibly destructive to the planet's future.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.4. Pro: The French National Front increased the share of votes it won in elections for the European Parliament from [6%](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2009_\(France\)) in 2009 to [25%](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Parliament_election,_2014_\(France\)) in 2014, and from [9%](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_regional_elections,_2010) in 2010 to [27%](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_regional_elections,_2015) in 2015 in elections for domestic regional councils.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.4.1. Con: Elsewhere, the party had far less success. In the 2017 election for the French National Assembly it increased its share of the seats [from 2 to 8](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_legislative_election,_2017), or from 0.3 to 1.4%.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.5. Pro: The [Freedom Party of Austria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freedom_Party_of_Austria#Strache_leadership_.282005.E2.80.93present.29) has increased its share of votes from 11% in 2006 to 20.5% in 2013. While the party might under-perform in polls done close to the election, it has seen a steady rise of votes in recent years nonetheless.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.5.1. Pro: It's also worth noting that the role of [President](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_Austria) in Austria is for the most part a ceremonial figurehead which suggests that party elections are far more relevant.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.6. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.6.1.3.1.1.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.7. Pro: Even if right-wing parties did not win elections, they have been able to use the refugee crisis as a means of galvanizing support.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.7.1. Con: In the Netherlands, the [Party for Freedom](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Party_for_Freedom) with its agenda against Islamic influences did not do better in the [2017 general election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dutch_general_election,_2017) than in previous years. It won 13% of the vote, compared to 10% in 2012 and 15% in 2010.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.7.2. Con: In France, the [National Front](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Front_\(France\)) with its nationalist agenda against refugees and Muslim influences won 13% of the seats in the [National Assembly in 2017](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/French_legislative_election,_2017), which is well in line with the results of previous elections.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.7.3. Pro: In Austria, the far-right [Freedom Party of Austria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freedom_Party_of_Austria) almost won the [2016 Presidential election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Austrian_presidential_election,_2016), mainly driven by an election campaign [about refugees](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/oct/11/social-democrats-win-vienna-election-despite-freedom-party-gains). In the [previous election](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Austrian_presidential_election,_2010) it had won merely 15%.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.7.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.13.4.1.1.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.4.8. Con: "The xenophobes are mad that other cultures are here!" is not a valid reason to be xenophobic. These people would be xenophobic regardless.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.5. Con: Any backlash or presence of prejudice does not mean that nations should simply give into that backlash. In nearly every effort to advance minority rights, these efforts have faced political backlash. This is simply a reason for societies to work harder to overcome prejudices.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.6. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.10.3.1.4.4.2.3.5.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.7. Pro: Crimes committed against refugees are rising significantly.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.7.1. Pro: Over 3,000 [crimes](https://www.thelocal.de/20170203/thousands-of-crimes-against-refugees-reported-in-2016-report) were committed against refugees or their homes in Germany 2016. The number of crimes against asylum homes in 2016 were roughly 5 times higher than the crimes of the same type in 2014.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.7.1.1. Con: This is at least partially explainable by the massively increased numbers of asylum seekers in Germany, rather than any increase in the rate of crimes committed against asylum seekers.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.7.2. Pro: According to the Council of Crime-Prevention \([BRÅ](https://www.bra.se/bra-in-english/home/crime-and-statistics/hate-crime.html)\), Sweden has seen a clear rise in hate crimes identified with xenophobic/racist motives in recent years.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.8. Pro: [Research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Immigration_and_crime) suggests that people tend to overestimate the relationship between immigration and crime.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.10. Con: [Studies indicate](https://www.nytimes.com/2017/01/26/us/trump-illegal-immigrants-crime.html) that immigrants do not commit crimes at higher rates than the general population. This indicates that the entrance of new people into a country is unlikely to raise crime.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.10.1. Con: The propensity of immigrants \(who typically voluntarily move countries for economic reasons, and are explicitly chosen by the host country\) to commit crimes is not necessarily related to the propensity of refugees, a very different group of people, to commit crimes.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.10.2. Con: Research findings in European countries indicate that some second-generation immigrant groups have crime rates that [drastically exceed](https://www.theatlantic.com/news/archive/2016/06/immigrants-and-crime/486884/) those of the native-born population.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.10.2.1. Con: Simultaneously, research in the US [indicates](https://www.theatlantic.com/news/archive/2016/06/immigrants-and-crime/486884/) that immigrants commit crime at a lower rate than the native-born population.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.10.3. Con: the studies you link were published in 2007, when migration was primarily between like-minded cultures \(same gods or secular\). The current concern is with the peoples "the west" has been actively destabilising and bombing since then.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.10.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.8.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.8. Pro: There aren't immigrants with more motivation than refugees fleeing war or persecution. As returning to their country of origin would likely mean death, their best option is to try to make a life for themselves in the host country.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.8.1. Pro: While not all refugees have the opportunity to, they all have the incentive to adapt to their new circumstances in the host country, as in order to be able to have autonomy over their socioeconomic situation, they need jobs which require skills such as being able to speak the native language.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.8.2. Con: [Out of principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-refoulement), asylum seekers are not sent back to their home country if they face persecution or other threats there. Refugees therefore do not face a choice between doing their best and death at home.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.9. Con: The ones who gain in taking on large numbers of refugees are the high income people in the country.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.9.1. Pro: Refugees increase competition among low-skilled workers, lowering wages and benefiting higher-income consumers.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.10. Pro: Diversity helps spark innovation and [entrepreneurship](https://hbr.org/2016/10/immigrants-play-a-disproportionate-role-in-american-entrepreneurship). [Immigrants and refugees](https://hbr.org/2016/10/why-are-immigrants-more-entrepreneurial) are also among the most entrepreneurial groups in a society.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.10.1. Pro: The recent wave of immigration in Germany has [boosted](https://www.economist.com/europe/2017/02/04/immigrants-are-bringing-entrepreneurial-flair-to-germany) the number of new startups. 44% of new businesses in 2015 were founded by people with foreign passports.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.11. Pro: Being a refugee can happen to anyone in the near or far future.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.11.1. Pro: Accepting others benefits the citizens of high-income countries as it might spark reciprocity if they are in need for shelter in the future.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.11.1.1. Con: It will take a long time for a country from which refugees have originated from to be in a position to accept refugees themselves.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.11.2. Pro: Supporting global commitments to refugees makes it more likely that others will be there to help you if you need it in the future.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.12. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.2.7.2.4.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.13. Con: Taking in refugees will bring down the quality of life in the host country.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.13.1. Pro: Property prices and rents in many German cities are rising to a degree that [they wouldn't](https://www.cnbc.com/2016/03/16/refugees-to-trigger-german-house-price-spike-postbank.html) without the influx of refugees. In Berlin, for example, the price of apartments is expected to rise by 15% until 2030. Without the refugee influx, this rise would be about six percent lower, as a [study](https://www.postbank.de/postbank/pr_presseinformation_2016_03_03_postbank_studie_2016_leben_in_der_stadt.html) argues.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.13.1.1. Con: The study also points towards the flip side of the refugee influx: other cities, especially in Germany's East, might profit from the population influx as they are expected to shrink.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.14. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.9.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.15. Pro: Refusing to accept refugees generates geopolitical instability.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.15.1. Con: Vice versa. Fundamentally, geopolitical instability generates refugees not the other way round.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.15.2. Con: Since the crisis is the refugees themselves coming, accepting refugees will encourage [more refugees](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/feb/27/ngo-rescues-off-libya-encourage-traffickers-eu-borders-chief) to attempt reaching high-income countries, thus increasing geopolitical instability.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.15.2.1. Pro: Accepting refugees gives another option to those trapped in unstable countries when they might otherwise work together towards political stability.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.15.2.1.1. Con: This may be true in limited circumstances \(although no examples given\). But could European Jews work with the Nazis to achieve a political solution? Diplomatic efforts in Syria, Eritrea and Myanmar did not solve the problems and staying in place demonstrably put lives at risk.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.15.2.2. Con: Refugees moving from unstable areas to countries with stable governments increases stability of the worldwide population.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.15.3. Pro: There are fears that the refusal of many European countries to accept refugees could lead to a [breakup of the EU](http://www.cnbc.com/2016/02/25/fears-eu-is-self-destructing-on-migrant-crisis.html).
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.15.4. Pro: The refusal of high-income countries to accept refugees means that many refugees remain concentrated in [Turkey](http://www.migrationpolicy.org/research/syrian-refugees-turkey-long-road-ahead) and [Jordan](http://theconversation.com/syrian-refugees-in-turkey-jordan-and-lebanon-face-an-uncertain-2017-70747), leading to [volatile](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/middle-east/turkey-shooting-dead-syrian-refugees-flee-civil-war-a6960971.html) situations.
1.1.6.5.3.1.1.16. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.10.3.1.4.4.2.
1.1.6.5.3.1.2. Con: Nations are not obligated to enact every policy that is credibly beneficial to them. They are empowered to prioritize and take risks as they see fit.
1.1.6.5.3.2. Pro: A state's right to self determination supersedes any moral obligations to non-citizens.
1.1.6.5.3.3. Pro: The very idea of democracy requires that political decisions be premised on the popular opinion and wishes of citizens.
1.1.6.5.3.3.1. Con: Most democracies have mechanisms to override popular opinion if the population decides to do something immoral - for example, constitutions which protect minority rights. So self-determination does not override moral responsibility in all instances.
1.1.6.5.3.3.1.1. Pro: Similarly, international treaties obligate nations to provide certain [minimum standards of living](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_to_an_adequate_standard_of_living) to all their citizens, regardless of public opinion.
1.1.6.5.3.3.1.1.1. Con: International law is [nonbinding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soft_law) unless countries consent to subject themselves to it, so the demands of international law are ultimately democratic demands.
1.1.6.5.3.3.2. Pro: The idea of "moral obligation" subverts democracy because it seeks to override the will of the people.
1.1.6.5.3.3.2.1. Con: Moral obligation only subverts the idea of unlimited democracy, and the associated tendency toward tyranny of the majority. Most systems rightly limit the authority of the will of the people with things like constitutional rights, representatives empowered to defy popular will at risk of their reelection chances, and other legal and institutional protections of the wise course.
1.1.6.5.3.3.2.1.1. Con: The idea of a tyranny of the majority is counter factual. A representative government was not instituted to subvert the will of the people. It was instituted to allow a division of labor just as every almost every job is in a modern economy. The idea that representatives should subvert the will of the people is equivalent to saying patients hire doctors not to promote health but to kill them if the doctor thinks they deserve to die.
1.1.6.5.3.4. Pro: If political communities must have self-determination, then no moral obligation to help refugees can exist.
1.1.6.5.3.4.1. Con: This only follows if political communities have limitless self-determination. Even very slight limits to self-determination can allow for a moral obligation to help refugees when you can. And limits to a state's self-determination certainly exist, such as borders, water rights, and obligations under ratified treaties.
1.1.6.5.3.4.2. Pro: By its nature, a moral obligation to take in refugees must be categorical, i.e. it must hold regardless of what the citizens of a country want. But this would imply that even if the citizens of a country didn't want to take in refugees, they still should; a conclusion which violates their right to self-determination.
1.1.6.5.3.4.3. Con: This conclusion is too strong - it implies that no moral constraints can act on countries. But some clearly can: for example, countries are not morally permitted to bomb civilians in other countries.
1.1.6.5.3.4.3.1. Con: The conclusion merely implies that no positive moral obligations act on countries - that countries cannot be obligated to do certain things. It does not imply that countries cannot be obligated not to do certain things.
1.1.6.5.3.4.4. Pro: Every country and its government have only a moral obligation to take care of their own people and thereby ensure that no people feel the need to flee.
1.1.6.5.3.5. Con: Individual people also have a right to self-determination. This does not absolve them of moral responsibilities to other people.
1.1.6.5.3.5.1. Con: The vast majority of such individuals are not responsible for the circumstances refugees face that have forced them to flee their countries; they aren't lobbyists for the military industrial complex or writing policies at D.C. think tanks. You can't ascribe collective moral responsibility to native-born citizens for crimes they have not committed. Foreign aid is a more reasonable alternative, as it still gives citizens the right to self-determine.
1.1.6.5.3.5.2. Pro: Our ability to self-determine and our power to accomplish things brings accompanying duties to use that freedom in a morally responsible way.
1.1.6.5.3.5.3. Con: While we may have moral responsibilities to assist refugees in certain ways, these responsibilities need not override our absolute right to associate with whomever we choose.
1.1.6.5.3.6. Pro: Moral obligations are arbitrary and by no means inherent. It's up to the voters or dictators of those high-income countries to decide if they recognize the obligations or not.
1.1.6.5.3.7. Con: Political communities do not have an absolute right to free association. For example, they cannot decide to not associate with some of their own citizens, by expelling them.
1.1.6.5.3.7.1. Con: A citizen can [lose citizenship](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loss_of_citizenship) for a number of reasons, for example when the community feels treason has been committed. So while rare, citizens can be expelled.
1.1.6.5.3.8. Pro: People that happen to be born in high-income countries can't be forced to pay for refugees. All help must be voluntary.
1.1.6.5.3.8.1. Con: They can be forced, through taxation, to assist. Just as those opposed to neocolonialism can be taxed to pay for neocolonialist programs that create refugee crises. Perhaps all help ought to be voluntary, but that is not the present reality.
1.1.6.5.4. Pro: While an individual might have a responsibility to help a person in need, states operate under different moral principles. States can legitimately commit acts of violence to keep citizens safe, for example bombing a foreign country in wartime. So even if keeping refugees out is perceived as an act of violence this does not necessarily make it illegitimate.
1.1.6.5.5. Con: Individuals are born with human rights. States' rights, on the other hand, are artificially constructed by collectives of human beings through laws and institutions. The inherent nature of human rights versus the artificial nature of states' rights means that we ought prioritize the rights of individuals to seek safe haven over the rights of states to deny refugees access to their nation.
1.1.6.6. Pro: The borders of high-income countries are often the only thing standing between refugees reaching safety and being forced to return to a wartorn country.
1.1.7. Con: This would imply that countries have no right to enforce their borders at all, which is an unreasonable conclusion. So this reasoning cannot be true.
1.1.7.1. Con: Countries [should](https://www.theatlantic.com/business/archive/2015/10/get-rid-borders-completely/409501/) move towards completely open borders.
1.1.7.2. Con: Enforcing a border is only immoral when doing so involves turning away someone in desperate need. Stopping a well-off person from entering your country is not immoral.
1.1.7.2.1. Con: There are plenty of non-refugees who are worse off than the average person living in a high-income country. Thus, given that every human being is of equal dignity and humanity, there would have to be open borders for everyone, not merely refugees.
1.1.7.2.1.1. Con: Many refugees do not merely live in bad conditions but face immediate threats, for example ongoing war and persecution by the governments of their home countries. This puts them in a different situation than people who are merely poor.
1.1.7.2.1.2. Con: What sets refugees apart from all other foreigners in need of support is that they actively ask for it and are in a situation of urgent need.
1.1.7.2.1.3. Pro: Taking in refugees provides them with benefits and the chance for a better life in a high-income country that non-refugees might also want to have yet are denied. This is unfair.
1.1.7.2.1.3.1. Con: People should not be allowed to suffer simply because it is hard to mitigate their suffering without providing them with an "unfair" benefit.
1.1.7.2.1.3.2. Con: Refugees are usually admitted on a temporary basis and expected to return home once the situation there improves. As a result, many of them are not given the chance to permanently start over.
1.1.7.2.1.3.2.1. Con: This suggests that refugees should be taken in by countries that lack that expectation that refugees will soon leave, as is the case with Canada, Germany, and the USA.
1.1.7.2.2. Pro: There are many desperate people in the world who are not refugees, but they are not actively requesting to enter our country. If they were, we would have a moral obligation to take them in.
1.1.7.2.3. Con: This statement seeks to quantify a moral criteria. Wealthy people often wish to enter a country not just because of the pursuit of wealth but also safer conditions, stronger culture and education.
1.1.7.3. Pro: If borders are a form of active violence, then it is always immoral to enforce border controls against people seeking better lives.
1.1.8. Con: The ability to enforce borders is an essential component of having legitimate governance over a state.
1.1.8.1. Pro: The idea of a nation is intrinsically linked to concepts of citizenship and group membership, both of which require enforceable borders to exclude non-citizens when it is desired.
1.1.8.1.1. Con: There is no necessary link between the concept of a nation and an exclusive territory. Nations can exist as Diasporas and include non-members on their territory.
1.1.8.1.1.1. Pro: Various states are [multinational in nature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multinational_state), for example the United Kingdom with the nations of England, Northern Ireland, Scotland, and Wales, as well as Belgium with the nations of Flanders and Wallonia.
1.1.8.1.1.2. Con: If nations existed solely as diasporas, they would become splintered and dispersed very quickly. This would make it hard to maintain national governance and identity.
1.1.8.1.2. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.13.
1.1.8.2. Pro: [Sovereignty](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sovereignty) is the right and power of a governing body to govern itself without any interference from outside sources or bodies. This right to have national borders which are respected is necessary to prevent war over territory. Citizens play an essential role for states to be able to exercise their sovereignty.
1.1.8.2.1. Con: War can be prevented in the absence of borders. For example, no wars have occurred within the [Schengen Area](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schengen_Area).
1.1.8.2.1.1. Con: The Schengen area only exists because of an agreement between the nations that uphold it. Even if traveling between those countries does not require a passport, the concept of sovereignty is still recognized and appreciated by other countries within Schengen.
1.1.8.2.1.2. Con: Civil wars frequently erupt between the borders of countries. For example, the [Yugoslav Wars](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yugoslav_Wars) erupted within the borders of the Yugoslav state which subsequently broke up into smaller republics. War over territories thus can erupt within national borders as well.
1.1.8.2.2. Pro: Sovereignty allows people to govern themselves in the way they best see fit. Not every community has the same laws or the same morality, this subjective interpretation of norms is necessary for better functioning of society.
1.1.8.2.3. Con: -> See 1.1.6.4.2.2.4.2.
1.1.8.3. Con: While it may be essential that countries retain the power to enforce borders, we can recognise that while still maintaining that using that power to keep out desperate refugees is immoral.
1.1.8.3.1. Con: Keeping out individuals from other countries is a primary reason for borders to exist. If individuals who wish to seek an education or a better life, but aren't refugees, are actively turned away, then refugees should not receive preferential treatment either.
1.1.8.3.1.1. Con: Refugees are not the same as economic migrants. The UN certifies the difference by authorizing some asylum-seekers as refugees and denying refugee status to others.
1.1.8.3.1.2. Con: The primary reason for borders to exist is to trace and control the movements of people. This means that borders can fulfill their primary function without states using them to commit immoral acts by denying entrance for those that desperately need asylum.
1.1.8.3.1.2.1. Pro: Travel within the European Union for EU citizens works like this. Borders are open, but there are still checks to monitor who is passing over borders and things like that. There is no reason borders could not function like this on a worldwide level.
1.1.8.3.1.2.1.1. Con: The abolition of border control within the Schengen Area has allowed for the fluid travel of terrorists throughout Europe, and as such is not a model that the rest of the world should seek to emulate.
1.1.8.3.1.2.1.1.1. Pro: Following an attack at a Christmas market in Berlin, the perpetrator traveled [unhindered](http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2016/12/23/berlin-attack-europes-open-borders-putting-britains-security/) through three countries before finally being killed in Italy.
1.1.8.3.1.2.1.1.2. Pro: Members of the ISIS cell that carried out the 2015 Paris Attacks and the 2016 Brussels Bombings [spent months](http://www.pbs.org/wgbh/frontline/article/how-europe-left-itself-open-to-terrorism/) freely roaming Europe in plot preparation, despite many of them being on terrorist watch-lists and having international arrest warrants.
1.1.8.3.1.2.1.1.3. Con: Borders or border controls do not stop terrorists. Members of Hezbollah and other terrorist groups have [entered](http://www.politifact.com/florida/statements/2016/apr/04/ron-desantis/terrorists-groups-besides-isis-crossing-us-mexico-/) the US illegally in the past.
1.1.8.3.1.3. Pro: Many countries enforce this notion by building physical barriers on their borders to keep anyone from crossing them. Some of the best-known examples are specifically aimed at stopping refugees, for example in [Spain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ceuta_border_fence), [Macedonia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Macedonian_border_barrier), [Hungary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hungarian_border_barrier), or the [US-Mexican](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mexico%E2%80%93United_States_barrier) border wall.
1.1.9. Con: -> See 1.1.6.4.2.
1.1.10. Pro: Significant numbers of refugees would be subjected to imprisonment, torture or starvation if they returned to their home region. The act of actively sending people where they will be mistreated is morally unbearable. This is not an argument for accepting all migrants; it's an argument for accepting those most in need of protection.
1.1.11. Con: One nation refusing access to refugees does not guarantee harm to those refugees, as they can seek refuge elsewhere. Responsibility \(and blame if refused from every single country\) is collective, not at the individual nation level.
1.1.11.1. Pro: Some nations might not be able to secure what the Convention defines as "safety" without severe, maybe unmanageable effects to their socio-economic conditions.
1.1.11.2. Pro: If a nation, by its government, [promotes and encourages](https://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/europe/germany-opens-its-gates-berlin-says-all-syrian-asylum-seekers-are-welcome-to-remain-as-britain-is-10470062.html) people to seek refuge, and by this invokes attempts of said people to access to 3rd party nations \(that [would not agree](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/2015/nov/08/angela-merkel-refugee-crisis-europe) with valid [concerns of safety](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/mar/01/refugees-isis-nato-commander-terrorists) on a security basis for example\), then the encouraging nation holds more responsibility than those 3rd party nations. The latter nevertheless are affected regardless of- and [even pressured against](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/sep/03/germany-france-eu-refugee-quotas-migration-crisis) their will.
1.1.12. Con: Unintended and unexpected harm as a result of an action are not acts of violence. Typical prosecution clause would be unintentional direct harm or in other words harm caused by gross negligence. Refusal of provision of refuge does not imply intended outcome for a refugee, but for the country. The former is an unknown and intent can't be proven, therefore can't be called an act of violence.
1.1.13. Con: States enforce border controls all the time. This is not widely decried as an 'act of violence'.
1.1.13.1. Con: Border controls only work because force can be deployed.  They are implicitly violent.
1.1.13.2. Con: Whether something is 'widely decried' or not does not alter the fundamental of that something. Ignorance of something's nature does not change that something's nature.
1.1.14. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.4.
1.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.
1.3. Con: Any responsibility that high-income countries have towards refugees can typically be discharged without taking them in.
1.3.1. Pro: The most efficient way to help refugees is through the United Nations Refugee Agency \(UNHCR\), which has been calling for additional funding for years but has not yet received the financial backing from high-income countries that it requires.
1.3.1.1. Pro: As of 2015, the head of the UNHCR [described](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2015/sep/06/refugee-crisis-un-agencies-broke-failing) the international humanitarian community as "financially broke", with funding for emergencies such as Syria and South Sudan remaining about a third behind the agency's requests.
1.3.1.2. Pro: The UNHCR is dealing with a growing gap between the funds it requires and the voluntary donations it receives from states, even as numbers of globally displaced people are [at a record high](http://www.unhcr.org/news/latest/2016/6/5763b65a4/global-forced-displacement-hits-record-high.html) \([UNHCR, p. 18](http://www.unhcr.org/publications/fundraising/564da0e20/unhcr-global-appeal-2016-2017-needs-funding-requirements.html)\).
1.3.1.3. Con: The UNHCR does not provide an alternative to taking in refugees. It lists the [resettlement of refugees](http://www.unhcr.org/resettlement.html) as one of its core competencies and emphasizes that the hosting of refugees in camps should be an [exceptional and temporary measure](http://www.unhcr.org/alternatives-to-camps.html).
1.3.1.3.1. Pro: Via the [New York Declaration for Refugees and Migrants](http://www.unhcr.org/towards-a-global-compact-on-refugees.html), which was adopted by all UN member states in 2016, the UNHCR was tasked with - among other things - expanding the "access to resettlement in third countries" of refugees.
1.3.1.3.2. Con: Until high-income countries take their share of responsibility in receiving the refugees, the best an individual high-income country can do to help refugees is to give money to the UNHCR.
1.3.1.3.2.1. Pro: High-income countries will never take their shared responsibility seriously unless there is some form of supranational agency to force them - as has been evident for at least 30 years.
1.3.1.3.2.2. Con: A collective responsibility of providing safety does not mean each individual country taking in refugees, be it at relative or equal intake.
1.3.1.3.2.3. Con: The commitment to the responsibility under the Convention is to provide asylum, but it does not set a quantity guarantee or resettling refugees to other safe countries.
1.3.1.3.3. Con: Funding the UNHCR is an alternative for countries whom want to help refugees but are opposed to take them in themselves.
1.3.2. Pro: High income countries could pay countries closer to refugees' homes to settle refugees, as has been done with [Turkey](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/mar/18/eu-strikes-deal-with-turkey-to-send-back-refugees-from-greece) and [Jordan](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/feb/03/syrian-refugees-jordan-london-conference).
1.3.2.1. Con: The situation that many refugees face in countries closer to their home is significantly worse than in high-income countries, and is oftentimes inhumane.
1.3.2.1.1. Con: The refugees would not be sent to random countries; we could just engage with the best countries. Therefore, appealing to the worst examples in the region does not undermine the parent.
1.3.2.1.2. Pro: Among the Syrian refugees in Lebanon, [about 70%](https://reliefweb.int/report/lebanon/vulnerability-assessment-syrian-refugees-lebanon-2016) of households live below the poverty line. In the urban areas of Jordan, this number is estimated to be [over 90%](https://www.amnesty.org/en/latest/news/2016/02/syrias-refugee-crisis-in-numbers/).
1.3.2.1.3. Pro: In Lebanon as well as other countries, refugees are met with [hostility and exploitation](http://www.npr.org/sections/parallels/2017/09/02/547906231/in-lebanon-syrian-refugees-met-with-harassment-and-hostility). Reports speak of wide-spread [sexual abuse and child labor](https://theconversation.com/syrian-refugees-in-lebanon-are-falling-into-slavery-and-exploitation-57521), with over 60% of Syrian refugee children in Lebanon working.
1.3.2.1.3.1. Con: The refugees in the given examples are not in officially sanctioned or administered camps: the NPR article, for example, explicitly states "Lebanon doesn't allow formal refugee camps."
1.3.2.1.3.1.1. Con: Refugee camps, where many refugees in countries like Turkey and Jordan spend months and years, have been described as a [failed concept](https://bostonreview.net/editors-picks-world/elizabeth-dunn-failure-refugee-camps) that may keep refugees alive but prevent them from living a worthy life; as inhumane and "[soul-destroying](https://www.independent.co.uk/voices/dispelling-the-myth-of-the-humane-refugee-camp-a6915746.html)".
1.3.2.1.3.1.1.1. Con: Of the refugees from Syria, [less than 10%](http://data.unhcr.org/syrianrefugees/regional.php) currently live in camps.
1.3.2.1.3.1.1.1.1. Con: It is unlikely that those who do not live in camps are any better off than those who do.
1.3.2.1.3.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: Given that those not living in camps are still refugees who reside within the borders of the country they wish to flee, it is unlikely that they are living in significantly better conditions.
1.3.2.1.4. Pro: -> See 1.3.2.1.3.1.1.
1.3.2.1.5. Con: If high-income countries provided money and resources to countries hosting refugees, the situations of refugees in those countries would improve considerably.
1.3.2.1.5.1. Pro: It is much much cheaper to care for refugees in poorer countries because the welfare there is less "luxurious" than in rich countries.
1.3.2.1.5.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.5.1.
1.3.2.1.5.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.5.2.
1.3.2.1.5.1.3. Con: One advantage of spending money on refugees at home over spending money on refugees abroad is that the money doesn't leave the country. What the governments spends at home goes back into the domestic economy.
1.3.2.1.5.1.4. Pro: The [Center for Immigration Studies](https://cis.org/Report/High-Cost-Resettling-Middle-Eastern-Refugees) has estimated that the cost of resettling immigrants/refugees to other Middle Eastern countries is 1/12th the cost of settling them in America. Recognizing that high-income countries do not have infinite resources, this suggests that likely the most cost-effective approach is to resettle people abroad.
1.3.2.1.5.1.4.1. Con: The methodology employed by this study primarily just considers fiscal costs/benefits; it does not consider net economic effects \(e.g. increasing labor/entrepreneurial supply, increasing consumption\).
1.3.2.1.5.1.4.1.1. Con: Those example factors are both negative overall economic effects, not positives, so do not counteract the methodology of the study.
1.3.2.1.6. Con: The situation for countless non-refugees across the world is also worse than that of first world or is inhumane, such as Dalits being abused and treated like garbage in India, women in Nepal forced to wash their husbands feet and drink the water, or children sold into slavery in parts of Africa. Yet this doesn't mean resettlement is the best or only solution for them. The existence of a better situation elsewhere doesn't ethically demand resettlement vs. alternative solutions.
1.3.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.2.1.5.1.4.
1.3.2.3. Pro: A refugee fleeing from war or a natural catastrophe should be provided with shelter as nearby in the region as possible, so that they can return as soon as possible. Those neighboring countries can be supported by high income countries.
1.3.2.3.1. Con: There's no reason returning to the country of origin should be a priority. After a war or other disaster, there may be no home worth returning to.
1.3.2.3.1.1. Con: A possible worst-case negative future doesn't negate the benefits of settling people near to their country of origin, where differences of language of culture are less, and the chance of remaining or reuniting with family greater.
1.3.2.3.2. Pro: By supporting neighboring countries in typically low cost countries, every dollar/euro spent has more impact and helps the surrounding economies.
1.3.2.3.3. Con: It may take many years for a crisis to be resolved. If this is the case, any refugees who have fled will have fully settled in a new home. It would be counterproductive for them to uproot themselves once again.
1.3.2.3.3.1. Con: It is doubtful anyone would feel 'fully settled' when away from their home, family, language, and culture in only a few years.
1.3.2.3.3.2. Con: If refugees theoretically would not want to return home to family and culture and language from a nearby location because they felt 'settled in,' then it would be even less likely they would want to return from a distant location where the transition would be more difficult.
1.3.2.4. Con: In a world in which geographic distances are becoming ever less of an obstacle, there is no reason why geographic proximity should be the decisive factor about where to settle refugees.
1.3.2.4.1. Pro: The cost of flying [has been declining](https://www.telegraph.co.uk/travel/comment/how-airfares-have-fallen-since-golden-age-of-flying/) for decades while, at the same time, the capacities of airlines and the number of people flying have been [steadily rising](https://data.worldbank.org/indicator/IS.AIR.PSGR).
1.3.2.5. Con: Countries experiencing enough socioeconomic strife to produce refugees are often located in economically poor regions. Even with a subsidy, it is unlikely that neighboring countries have the resources necessary to maintain humanitarian conditions long-term, and it is unfair for them to be uniquely saddled with the responsibility.
1.3.2.5.1. Con: If a particular region is economically poor, this also means that the costs of settling and housing refugees there will be lower than the costs of doing the same in high-income countries.
1.3.2.6. Con: This can lead to countries, such as Turkey, using refugees as political leverage and threatening to renege on the agreements signed.
1.3.2.7. Pro: Typically neighboring poor countries share the refugees' language and culture, thus a lower degree of integration is required there.
1.3.2.8. Con: Paying other countries to solve the problem is an offloading of moral responsibility, not a fulfillment of it.
1.3.2.8.1. Con: What matters morally is the consequences for the refugees. If we can help more refugees for the same cost by paying other countries to take them we should.
1.3.2.8.1.1. Con: Having these refugees taken in by other countries might make it possible to help more of them with the same money, yet this is outweighed by the bad conditions they have to live in there.
1.3.2.8.1.1.1. Pro: Refugee camps in Turkey are [overcrowded and dangerous](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/europe/pictures-of-life-for-turkeys-25-million-syrian-refugees-crisis-migrant-a6969551.html).
1.3.2.8.1.1.2. Con: More survival help, although very basic, to desperate people, is never bad for them. It is perhaps only the privileged who can imagine that such help can ever be "outweighed" by it not leading to some arbitrary high standard of living for the recipients.
1.3.2.9. Con: Turkey [lacks a long-term plan](https://hbr.org/2017/04/turkey-badly-needs-a-long-term-plan-for-syrian-refugees) for settling refugees and has begun experiencing increased hostility towards its refugee population.
1.3.2.9.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.2.8.1.1.1.
1.3.2.9.2. Con: This is a problem of Turkey's limited resources; if other high income countries provided funding and expertise, the situation in Turkey could be improved.
1.3.3. Con: While refugees continually suffer injustice, telling them you could be helping another way does not solve their problems. If they are searching for a home, and a country turns them away, that country is part of the problem.
1.3.4. Pro: Displacing people with different language, values, cultures and beliefs into other countries puts them at a great disadvantage when it comes to being rewarded with life, liberty, and prosperity, especially if those values are incompatible with the country they take refuge in.
1.3.4.1. Con: The opportunity for rewarding life, liberty and prosperity is even less in a refugee's home country. The absence of the conditions for a rewarding life are inherent to the concept of refugee.
1.3.4.1.1. Con: What makes a life rewarding depends on one's values. If a refugee's values don't align with the values of the country that refuge is taken in, it is by definition impossible for them to be happy.
1.3.4.2. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.1.6.
1.3.5. Pro: High-income countries have a moral obligation to help refugees in the most efficient way. This is not achieved by taking in refugees.
1.3.5.1. Con: Settlement into high-income countries is typically much cheaper than intervening in foreign wars that cause refugee crises or trying to build a new society out of a refugee camp.
1.3.5.1.1. Con: -> See 1.3.2.1.5.1.
1.3.5.1.2. Con: It's approximately [12 times more expensive to resettle refugees](https://www.kialo.com/do-high-income-countries-have-a-moral-obligation-to-take-in-refugees-2214/2214.0=2214.1-2214.26+2214.28+2214.1776) in the U.S., not cheaper. In their first five years in the United States, each refugee from the Middle East [costs](https://cis.org/Report/High-Cost-Resettling-Middle-Eastern-Refugees) taxpayers $64,370 - 12 times what the UN estimates it costs to care for one refugee in neighboring Middle Eastern countries.
1.3.5.1.3. Pro: In 2016, the German government [spent](http://www.dw.com/en/germany-spent-20-billion-euros-on-refugees-in-2016/a-38963299) €9.3 billion to care for more than a million refugees and asylum seekers and €11 billion to battle the causes of forced displacement. The latter did have little effect; to make a difference, it would probably take much more money than it takes to care for arrivals.
1.3.5.1.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.5.6.2.
1.3.5.1.4. Pro: The Iraq and Afghanistan wars jointly caused the US [$1.6 trillion](http://www.politifact.com/truth-o-meter/statements/2016/oct/27/donald-trump/did-us-spend-6-trillion-middle-east-wars/), and largely failed to achieve their objectives of creating peace and stability.
1.3.5.1.5. Pro: Humanitarian military intervention in Libya [largely failed](https://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/libya/obamas-libya-debacle).
1.3.5.2. Con: The refugee crisis is a very large and complex issue and it's unlikely that any single approach to supporting refugees will be effective or sufficient enough on its own.
1.3.5.3. Pro: Providing housing and aid for development in the refugees' home countries achieves the overall goal of helping them without causing political instability that mass movement of millions of people can cause.
1.3.5.3.1. Con: Providing housing and aid in the midst or edge of a war will likely only lead to another mass movement in the near future when the war falls on them again
1.3.5.4. Pro: It is more efficient for the high income country to spend its limited resources on creating peace or better living conditions for all vulnerable people in the refugees' home country than to take in a small number of refugees, while allowing the others to suffer.
1.3.5.4.1. Con: -> See 1.3.2.1.5.1.3.
1.3.5.5. Pro: Money spent on integration into a new society is money lost to provide basic necessities. The larger the cultural and language divide, the higher the costs of integration. Settlement in the region reduces this cost.
1.3.6. Pro: High income countries could attempt to resolve the conflicts which displace refugees in the first place.
1.3.6.1. Con: The needs of refugees are immediate. Any attempt at solving the conflicts that caused them to leave their home countries would take years, if not decades, and is wholly inappropriate for dealing with the suffering at hand.
1.3.6.1.1. Pro: Not only would conflicts need to end, but massive reconstruction efforts would need to take place to allow conflict zones to begin housing refugees again.
1.3.6.1.2. Pro: Even after major conflicts end, violence usually persists for a while afterwards, as has been the case in Iraq and Afghanistan. So there is no immediate prospect of refugees returning to a peaceful country.
1.3.6.1.2.1. Pro: Afghanistan is [still at war](https://www.nytimes.com/2017/08/21/world/asia/trump-afghanistan-war-taliban.html) with the insurgent Taliban.
1.3.6.1.2.2. Pro: Iraq continues to fight [ISIS](https://www.vox.com/world/2017/9/18/16309558/isis-iraq-kurds-trump-us-mosul-syria-war-terrorism), which holds some territory and launches frequent terrorist attacks.
1.3.6.1.2.3. Pro: In 2016, [Afghanistan](https://www.statista.com/statistics/251408/number-of-deaths-in-afghanistan-due-to-terrorism/) suffered from 1,340 terrorist attacks which killed 4,561 people, and in [Iraq](https://www.statista.com/statistics/202861/number-of-deaths-in-iraq-due-to-terrorism/) 9,764 people were killed by terrorism.
1.3.6.2. Pro: The Syria crisis has worsened because of the US's [reluctance to intervene decisively.](https://www.theatlantic.com/international/archive/2016/10/syria-washington-obama-iraq-middle-east-intervention-assad-isis/505202/)
1.3.6.3. Con: Bashar al-Assad is likely to [win the civil war.](http://www.jpost.com/Middle-East/Aleppo-victory-shifts-civil-war-in-Assads-favor-475201) For most refugees, returning to live under his regime is not a viable option.
1.3.6.3.1. Pro: According to a [2015 survey](https://www.adoptrevolution.org/en/pr-survey/) among Syrian refugees in Germany, the majority did want to return home - but not as long as Bashar al-Assad remained in power. A return in the short run was and is therefore not feasible for them.
1.3.6.3.1.1. Con: It is usually the host country that determines whether someone has to return home. The political preferences of refugees are irrelevant in this context.
1.3.6.4. Con: Intervention efforts generally fail, and are often actively counterproductive.
1.3.6.4.1. Pro: [More than half](http://www.unhcr.org/figures-at-a-glance.html) of the world's refugees currently come from Syria, Afghanistan and South Sudan. Years of engagement by the international community have not been able to pacify the situation in either country. This is unlikely to change.
1.3.6.4.1.1. Pro: Ever since South Sudan was founded as a state, a UN peace-keeping mission has been active there with [almost 20.000 troops](https://unmiss.unmissions.org/facts-and-figures). Nevertheless, [two million people](http://data.unhcr.org/SouthSudan/regional.php) have fled the country since then because of conflict.
1.3.6.4.1.2. Pro: In other countries, too, prolonged efforts of the international community have been unable to pacify long-lasting conflicts that have sparked large refugee streams. Examples are the Somalia, Sudan and the DR Congo \([UNHCR, p. 16](http://www.unhcr.org/576408cd7.pdf)\).
1.3.6.4.1.3. Pro: Even after 16 years of military presence by the international community, Afghanistan is in a bad situation. The number of civilian casualties is [at a record high](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/jul/17/civilian-deaths-in-afghanistan-war-at-record-high-says-un) as of July 2017, with the numbers of children and women killed by the ongoing conflict rising especially fast.
1.3.6.4.1.4. Pro: In Syria, numerous countries - among them the US, Russia, Iran, Turkey and Saudi Arabia - have been intervening on different sides for years without any lasting effect. It is unlikely that international intervention on a politically feasible level can provide a solution for the conflict.
1.3.6.4.1.4.1. Pro: Numerous outside countries [have taken sides](https://www.theguardian.com/world/ng-interactive/2015/oct/09/who-backs-whom-in-the-syrian-conflict) in the Syrian conflict: all of them against the Islamic State, Russia and Iran through military support for the Syrian government, most other countries through diplomatic and logistical support for moderate groups, several Arab nations through support for Islamist groups. Yet none of this support was able to propel one side to victory, thereby ending the conflict.
1.3.6.4.2. Pro: The US should not intervene in Syria, since it risks [provoking conflict with Russia.](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/world/americas/us-politics/syria-war-us-russia-tensions-iran-drones-jet-miss-assad-putin-trump-latest-a7799576.html)
1.3.6.4.3. Pro: Significant foreign intervention in Syria is likely to fail, as it did under similar circumstances [in Libya.](https://www.foreignaffairs.com/articles/libya/obamas-libya-debacle)
1.3.6.4.4. Pro: Interventions by high income countries in the Middle East, like the Iraq War, have been [instrumental](http://www.aljazeera.com/blogs/middleeast/2015/09/iraq-war-root-europe-refugee-crisis-150908151855527.html) in creating the current refugee crisis.
1.3.6.4.4.1. Pro: The American invasion of Iraq, the subsequent destabilization of the country as well as the region have [been blamed](http://nationalinterest.org/feature/the-collapse-iraq-the-rise-isis-made-america-13913) for the rise of ISIS, which has fueled many of the conflicts that now cause refugee streams.
1.3.6.4.4.2. Con: The current refugee crisis is [largely caused](http://www.unhcr.org/figures-at-a-glance.html) by the [Syrian civil war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Refugees_of_the_Syrian_Civil_War), which is not the fault of the other countries.
1.3.6.4.4.2.1. Pro: The civil war was [sparked by protests](http://www.bbc.com/news/world-middle-east-35806229) following the Arab Spring, not due to any foreign intervention.
1.3.6.4.4.2.2. Con: Foreign intervention \(specifically [by Russia](https://www.nytimes.com/2016/02/11/world/middleeast/russian-intervention-in-syrian-war-has-sharply-reduced-us-options.html)\) has made the conflict worse.
1.3.6.4.4.2.2.1. Con: Russia first intervened in Syria [in September 2015](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Syrian_Civil_War), more than four years after the country's civil unrest began. At this time, the conflict had already produced [more than 4.3 million](http://data.unhcr.org/syrianrefugees/regional.php) refugees, a number that has only increased slightly since.
1.3.6.4.4.2.3. Con: The US's failure to [either commit fully or refrain from interfering](http://theweek.com/articles/667310/how-obama-made-syrias-civil-war-much-much-worse) made the conflict more intractable.
1.3.6.4.4.2.3.1. Con: Failure to proactively solve a problem does not usually make an actor morally culpable. By this standard, we are all culpable for failing to solve poverty in low-income countries by donating our money.
1.3.6.4.4.2.3.2. Con: One can hardly blame the United States for not intervening in Syria after the interventions in [Afghanistan, Iraq and Libya](https://www.theatlantic.com/magazine/archive/2016/04/the-obama-doctrine/471525/) all turned into international and domestic failures, and strained the American military.
1.3.6.4.4.3. Pro: The West's increasing reliance on aerial warfare and drone strikes \(instead of boots on the ground\) in various conflict theaters in the Middle East [has been linked](http://journals.sfu.ca/jd/index.php/jd/article/view/36) to radicalization and a focus on violence among those affected.
1.3.6.4.4.4. Con: Forced regime change, while it did cause a high level of instability in the region, was not colonial in nature. The goal was always the installation and support of democracy and independent self governance.
1.3.6.4.4.4.1. Con: The act of imposing a political and economic system on another country has a colonial and imperialistic connotation regardless of the nature of these systems.
1.3.6.4.4.5. Con: Precisely those high-income countries who caused the refugee crisis should be only countries with burden of accepting refugees and making their life better.
1.3.6.4.4.5.1. Con: While this may apply to some countries that have been the origin of refugee movements, one cannot generalize on this basis.
1.3.6.4.4.5.2. Pro: Major European countries like Germany and France [were opposed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Opposition_to_the_Iraq_War#Opposition_in_European_countries) to the 2003 Iraq War and did not participate. Thus, they can hardly be blamed for the regional instability it brought.
1.3.6.4.4.5.2.1. Con: Yes, Germany and France indeed had an anti-war stance. But the contention is, they did not do enough to prevent US+UK from raging a war. No sanctions, diplomatic posturing, embargo or any other punitive actions were taken by Germany/France and other European high-income countries. Their opposition, if any, was limited to scathing speeches in the UN, which had no tangible effect at all. The govt. of Italy and Spain practically supported the war.
1.3.6.5. Con: The actions of high income countries are often at the root of the very issues and conflicts that displace people.
1.3.6.6. Con: The citizens of high-income countries have no interest in further interventions abroad for the purpose of resolving conflicts there.
1.3.6.6.1. Pro: In a [2018 survey](https://www.thenation.com/article/new-poll-shows-public-overwhelmingly-opposed-to-endless-us-military-interventions/) of American citizens, a majority indicated a desire for restraint in using the American military abroad and for purposes that are not a last resort.
1.3.6.6.2. Pro: [Research](https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=2989040) suggests that sensitivity and a critical attitude towards the human cost of American military intervention abroad can be decisive in national elections in the US.
1.3.6.6.3. Pro: As of 2013, majorities of the citizens of the EU, the US and Turkey indicated in surveys that they oppose an intervention of their respective countries in Syria \([GMF, p. 6](http://www.gmfus.org/publications/transatlantic-trends-2013)\).
1.3.6.6.4. Con: Small-scale interventions with clear goals can find popular support. When France [intervened in Mali in 2013](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_Serval) against the rise of Islamic militants, a clear majority of French citizens voiced their support in surveys \([PEW, p. 2](http://assets.pewresearch.org/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2013/07/Pew-Research-Global-Attitudes-Project-Mali-Report-FINAL-July-25-2013.pdf)\).
1.3.6.7. Con: At the moment, military interventions by high-income countries are not a feasible or practical due to a lack of political will.
1.3.6.7.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.6.4.1.4.
1.3.6.7.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.6.4.1.3.
1.3.6.7.3. Con: Taking in very large numbers of refugees is likely similarly politically unfeasible.
1.3.7. Con: Once a refugee is displaced and seeking asylum, the damage has already been done. Any non-immediate alternatives to asylum--e.g. regional stabilization or conflict resolution--are rendered moot.
1.3.8. Con: In order to truly compensate the victims of Western colonialism, high-income countries must allow people from colonised countries to enter high-income countries in order to access the resources that were extracted and plundered.
1.3.8.1. Con: Amount of compensation is subjective and can be as high as practically infinite, given possibilities of most optimistic development foregone due to conquest and colonization.
1.3.8.1.1. Pro: If no amount of money can correct colonialism's sins, then no amount of refugees taken in will work either, so colonial pasts are no justification for refugee acceptance.
1.3.9. Pro: Due to the higher cost in high-income countries, more people could be helped where they are for the same price.
1.3.9.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.2.1.5.1.4.
1.3.9.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.5.4.
1.3.9.3. Con: -> See 1.3.6.6.
1.3.9.4. Con: Preventing people becoming refugees is a separate issue from the question of moral obligations towards people who have already become refugees.
1.3.10. Pro: It would be better for high-income countries to help improve the economies of low-income ones. This is better than letting poverty spread, putting pressure on richer countries' infrastructures and simply weakening their economies too along with other problems caused by the demographic changes, such as damage to the environment.
1.3.10.1. Pro: If a country has surplus money then it should help giving that money to develop other countries and build infrastructure there instead of accepting refugees. Since refugees often end up doing low-wage menial jobs, the benefits to them of accepting them are small compared to the potential benefits of infrastructure building in poor countries.
1.3.10.1.1. Con: These two things are not alternative to each other. Helping developing countries does not exclude accepting refugees. The consequences of rejecting refugees however are torture and death, which cannot be offset by the benefits of foreign aid.
1.3.11. Pro: Most wealthy nations had their own periods of historical struggle and poverty; they had revolutions and changed politics internally until they got to where they are now. This should be no different for currently less-wealthy nations.
1.3.11.1. Con: This claim assumes that there is only one pre-written path to development involving the turmoils that today's high-income countries went through. The past is not necessarily the present.
1.3.11.1.1. Con: No single development model is described, so it is impossible to perceive only one path without a biased approach or one of context, which we have none of.
1.3.12. Con: Alternative solutions are at best temporary. The only way to achieve a permanent resolution of the problem is to offer refugees asylum in high-income countries.
1.3.12.1. Pro: Refugees need a permanent place to live, since they will be unable in the foreseeable future to return to places like Syria. The countries that currently house them \(like Turkey, Jordan, or Greece\) are unwilling to keep them permanently.
1.3.12.1.1. Con: Syria is more stable and displaced refugees have begun moving back. The focus should be on rebuilding Syria.
Over 600,000 Displaced Syrians Returned Home in First 7 Months of [2017](https://www.iom.int/news/over-600000-displaced-syrians-returned-home-first-7-months-2017).
1.3.12.1.2. Pro: The Jordanian government has [obstructed](https://www.huffingtonpost.com/vicky-kelberer/no-place-to-call-home-gov_b_7737890.html) the construction of shelter for refugees, potentially because shelter would imply permanent settlement.
1.3.12.1.3. Pro: [Most refugees](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/mar/01/refugee-crisis-european-leaders-demand-urgent-support-for-greece) intend to move on from Greece, and Greece lacks the resources to continue supporting all the refugees who are stuck there.
1.3.12.1.4. Pro: Syria is still torn by [violence and lack of resources](http://metro.co.uk/2017/10/26/children-at-doors-of-starvation-as-syrian-civil-war-rages-on-7030691/), so returning in the near future is not a viable option.
1.3.12.1.5. Con: If being unwilling to house them is a justifiable reason for these countries rejecting the refugees, then it is a justifiable reason for Western countries to reject them also.
1.3.12.1.5.1. Con: As these countries have already taken on the most refugees, and have the least amount of resources to spend on them, they're a lot more justified in rejecting refugees than High Income countries.
1.3.12.1.5.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.6.1.2.
1.3.12.1.5.1.2. Con: The resources you have at your disposal should not effect your willingness to help others.
1.3.12.1.5.1.2.1. Con: The unwillingness in this context is coming due to a lack of ability to help more refugees, making it more understandable to reject more refugees.
1.3.12.1.6. Pro: Turkey has already begun [expelling refugees](https://www.nytimes.com/2016/04/02/world/europe/turkey-forcibly-returned-refugees-to-syria-amnesty-report.html).
1.3.12.2. Con: The refugee experience in many wealthy countries has been characterized by systemic discrimination, language barriers, and poor socio-economic opportunities. It's not clear that this constitutes an acceptable 'permanent' resolution.
1.3.12.2.1. Pro: Some high-income countries rank among the world's least diverse in terms of ethnicity and culture, for example South Korea and Japan. Refugees would have a hard time fitting in and being accepted there \([Fearon, p. 215ff.](https://link.springer.com/article/10.1023/A%3A1024419522867)\).
1.3.12.2.1.1. Pro: The few refugees whom Japan accepts [report](http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2017/05/04/not-welcome-japan-refuses-99-percent-refugee-applications/) difficulties due to the language barrier and a lack of state support.
1.3.12.2.2. Pro: In 2016, [an average of ten attacks](http://www.bbc.com/news/world-europe-39096833) per day on migrants or their residences were committed in Germany. Amnesty International [has accused](https://www.amnesty.org/en/documents/eur23/4112/2016/en/) the German government of failing to protect migrants and deal with this rapid rise of hate crimes.
1.3.12.2.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.2.1.1.
1.3.12.3. Con: Refugees have nothing to do upon arrival due to a lack of opportunities and barriers that prevent them from being able to be productive members of the society that hosts them.
1.3.12.3.1. Pro: They are poor, uneducated, and unskilled people that inevitably end up on welfare or societal assistance. Rather than contributing to the welfare of society, they become a drain upon society. No one is obligated to drain themselves for the sake of others.
1.3.12.3.1.1. Con: Refugees are victims of the extreme circumstances that made them flee and there shouldn't be any expectations placed upon the refugees that are granted asylum.
1.3.12.3.1.1.1. Pro: Society do not place any expectations on citizens that are written sick for being traumatized from having gone through similarly horrible experiences. It is unclear why society should place a higher burden on refugees.
1.3.12.3.1.2. Con: Society protects vulnerable citizens and takes care of those who are unable to be productive members of society due to circumstances out of their control. This shows that society do take on certain obligations to 'drain' themselves for the sake of others.
1.3.12.3.2. Pro: Refugees are unable to pursue work in host countries due to language barriers and lack of legal permits to perform work in most job areas.
1.3.12.3.3. Pro: High performing economies usually have demanding educational systems. Refugees have to compete in an environment that does not permit the best immediate outcome due to a lack of compatible education.
1.3.12.4. Con: Asylum is temporary. This is conflating asylum with migration.
1.3.12.5. Con: Refugees are running from a war. Wars are temporary, so a temporary solution is all you need until the war is over and they can go home and rebuild.
1.3.12.5.1. Con: Even if wars are temporary, they often last long enough to make it unfeasible for refugees to plan for a return. For example, [Afghanistan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/War_in_Afghanistan_\(1978%E2%80%93present\)) has been in a state of conflict for decades and [Somalia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Somali_Civil_War) has been in a state of civil war for over 25 years.
1.3.12.5.2. Con: Many refugees flee persecution, rather than war; for example, [Rohingya](http://edition.cnn.com/2017/09/13/world/iyw-rohingya-refugees-flee-violence/index.html) refugees from Myanmar. Since the prejudices they are fleeing are long-lasting, they are unlikely to be able to return in the near future.
