1.3.12.6. Pro: Most refugees are stuck in limbo, living on assistance and in camps for years. Only a small share are ever resettled via international processes: in 2016, the share stood at [less than 1%](http://www.unhcr.org/figures-at-a-glance.html) of the world's population of registered refugees. Assisting them with resettlement is therefore vital.
1.3.12.7. Pro: Most plans by high-income countries to accept refugees involve [permanent resettlement](http://www.aljazeera.com/news/2017/09/european-commission-seeks-resettle-50000-refugees-170927170931800.html).
1.3.12.8. Pro: Attempting to improve the situation in refugees' home countries is unlikely to be a viable permanent solution, so resettlement is the only available option.
1.3.12.8.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.6.1.2.
1.3.12.8.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.6.4.
1.3.12.8.3. Pro: There is no feasible model of intervention by high income countries that can solve systemic problems of war and institutional failure.
1.3.12.8.3.1. Con: The UN exists as a mechanism for the international community to intervene in order to solve or alleviate conflicts, and provides several additional means for solving problems of war and institutional failure.
1.3.12.8.3.1.1. Pro: The things that the UN provide includes; acting as a forum for mediation, for trials against war crimes, and peacekeeping forces.
1.3.12.8.3.1.2. Pro: The UN is capable of mobilizing large quantities of resources to aid and rebuild war-torn countries.
1.3.12.8.3.1.3. Pro: -> See discussion #15052: The UN is a good thing
1.3.12.8.4. Con: Mass education provided by NGOs is the only long-term solution to national instability. While military intervention from a foreign government is likely to worsen the situation, funding local NGOs on the ground has proven long-term benefit.
1.3.12.8.4.1. Con: This doesn't negate the need for accepting refugees in the medium-term.
1.3.12.9. Con: Relocation hardly “permanently resolves the issue”, as the conflicts originating the migration still remain, causing more migration.
1.3.13. Pro: Most displaced people would prefer to return home but can't. Our responsibility is to act in their interest and to support the stabilization and pacification of their home countries. We should do what the displaced want, not what we think is better for them.
1.3.13.1. Con: In the short term, many refugees want to settle in other countries as returning to their place of origin is out of question. Following the logic that the preferences of the displaced should be supreme, they should be taken in.
1.3.13.1.1. Pro: In both 2015 and 2016, [more than 1.2 million](http://ec.europa.eu/eurostat/statistics-explained/index.php/Asylum_statistics) people applied for asylum in the EU-28 countries alone. The fact that they traveled there and ask to stay indicates that they do not plan to return home in the short run.
1.3.13.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.6.3.1.
1.3.13.1.3. Pro: During the first quarter of 2017, 95% of asylum applications by Syrians and 60% by Afghans [were successful](http://ec.europa.eu/eurostat/statistics-explained/index.php/Asylum_quarterly_report#cite_note-4) in the EU-28 countries. This indicates that even the countries where they apply recognize that returning home is out of the question.
1.3.13.2. Pro: It is more important to focus on dealing with the horrible conditions that makes people flee. There are many issues in the countries that people are fleeing from, that high-income countries could help fixing.
1.3.13.2.1. Pro: High-income countries could build hospitals and schools in poor countries, in order to educate and cure children and let people live in their homeland without having to face a dangerous journey and the displacement in a totally foreign land.
1.3.13.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.6.
1.3.13.2.3. Pro: High-income countries should stop selling weapons that ends up being used in the conflicts that the refugees are fleeing from.
1.3.13.2.3.1. Pro: Selling arms does not merely involve profiting from war, but also actively enabling the perpetration of that war. This makes rich countries complicit in the consequences of that war.
1.3.13.2.3.1.1. Con: If someone buys weapons and then uses them immorally, the moral responsibility lies with that person, not the vendor of the weaponry.
1.3.13.2.3.1.1.1. Pro: Stores that sell guns are not morally complicit in all shooting deaths.
1.3.13.2.3.1.1.2. Con: High-income countries can reliably predict that when, for example, they sell arms to Saudi Arabia, those weapons will be used to kill civilians in Yemen. They know what the weapons will be used for, and sell them anyways.
1.3.13.2.4. Pro: The number of refugees accepted shouldn't be significant compared to the number of people granted humanitarian aid. Foreign humanitarian aid should be more significant than immigration. Countries can and should help far more foreigners where they are than the number of refugees.
1.3.13.3. Pro: High-income countries could fund refugee camps and build shelter for refugees in countries close to their home countries.
1.3.13.3.1. Pro: Refugees fare much better when they are close to [home](http://www.cbc.ca/news/canada/kitchener-waterloo/why-syria-refugees-might-rather-stay-closer-to-home-rami-shamma-1.3317607).
1.3.13.4. Pro: -> See 1.3.2.
1.3.13.5. Con: -> See 1.3.12.8.3.
1.4. Pro: As part of a global community we are all global citizens and have moral responsibilities towards each other that transcend national boundaries.
1.4.1. Pro: It has become generally accepted that the responsibility to protect people from harm moves from the individual state to the international community in case the former is unable or unwilling to live up to it. This idea has been enshrined in the [Responsibility to Protect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Responsibility_to_protect).
1.4.1.1. Pro: The Responsibility to Protect has been [de-facto used](http://www.globalpolicyjournal.com/blog/28/03/2014/libya-and-responsibility-protect-results-and-prospects) by the UN Security Council to overrule other established principles, such as state sovereignty, and mandate international intervention with military force in Libya in 2011.
1.4.1.1.1. Con: The current state of Libya does not suggest it was a healthy strategy.
1.4.1.1.1.1. Pro: The case of Libya [harmed the idea](http://www.nytimes.com/2011/11/08/opinion/r2p-rip.html) of the Responsibility to Protect as it supported the worst fears of its critics: that the principle enables rich, Western countries to use their militaries to enact regime change under the cover of humanitarian goodwill.
1.4.1.1.2. Con: Even Barack Obama, who [pushed personally](https://www.vanityfair.com/news/2012/10/michael-lewis-profile-barack-obama) for the intervention in Libya against resistance in the White House, has placed [American interests](https://www.theatlantic.com/magazine/archive/2016/04/the-obama-doctrine/471525/) above the Responsibility to Protect and not endorsed it where it might have been obvious, e.g. in his Nobel Prize acceptance speech.
1.4.1.2. Con: The Responsibility to Protect is far from universally accepted as a doctrine.
1.4.1.2.1. Pro: There have been numerous cases in the past years in which the international community's responsibility to protect would have clearly applied, yet only the responsibility to protect of the state in question was called out and no action undertaken by the international community, most likely out of convenience and a lack of strategic incentives. \([Hehir, 2010](http://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/0047117809366205) and [Hehir, 2015](http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/13642987.2015.1082829)\)
1.4.1.2.2. Pro: A study of UN Security Council resolutions concerning the Arab Spring between 2011 and 2015 found that, though Responsibility to Protect was mentioned in a few resolutions, it was only ever with regard to states having a responsibility to protect their own people in countries where violence was occurring, with not one of them referencing the international community's responsibility to protect the citizens of other countries. \([Hehir, 2016](http://journals.sagepub.com/doi/abs/10.1177/0010836715612849)\)
1.4.1.2.3. Pro: "The discussions about Responsibility to Protect \(R2P\), or its cousin “humanitarian intervention”, are regularly disturbed by the rattling of a skeleton in the closet: history, to the present moment." [Statement by Professor Noam Chomsky to the United Nations General Assembly Thematic Dialogue on the Responsibility to Protect](http://www.un.org/ga/president/63/interactive/protect/noam.pdf)
1.4.1.2.4. Con: The members of the United Nations agreed on accepting the Responsibility to Protect in the final document of the [2005 World Summit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2005_World_Summit).
1.4.1.3. Con: This is a legal claim, hence irrelevant to an ethical discussion. What is legal and what is right are two different things.
1.4.1.3.1. Con: The Responsibility to Protect has an essential ethical component: the idea that certain foundations of the international order \(e.g. state sovereignty\) can be overruled for ethical reasons \(e.g. the protection of civilians\).
1.4.1.4. Con: -> See 1.4.1.2.3.
1.4.2. Con: National boundaries also reflect cultural ones. Some societies have different cultural values that are incompatible with a free democratic society.
1.4.2.1. Con: Accepting refugees helps evangelise the cause of free democracy to the refugees and their society. This is a desirable result.
1.4.2.1.1. Con: This is only true in a controlled immigration flow. When refugees are taken in large numbers \(such as in the crisis\), they tend to stay together in [segregated communities](https://www.economist.com/news/europe/21572248-young-diverse-and-unemployed-forgotten-banlieues) instead of integrating.
1.4.2.1.2. Pro: Studies show that [Mexicans who migrate](http://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/0010414009331733) to the US produce more democratic attitudes and behavior at home through cross-border communication and after their return.
1.4.2.1.3. Pro: Studies on the experience of [Eastern European migrant workers](http://libproxy.snu.ac.kr/7ce359d/_Lib_Proxy_Url/journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/0010414011428591) indicate that migration to high-income countries leads to an association of a better life with democracy as well as a growing interest and participation in some political issues \(though not all\).
1.4.2.1.4. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.10.3.1.4.4.3.3.
1.4.2.2. Pro: Liberal democratic values are a product of a specific time, place, and people who experienced a very specific history and produced a specific culture. Suggesting the universality of these values is ethnocentric and temporally arrogant. In fact, ethical universalism itself is a product of Greco-western philosophy.
1.4.2.2.1. Pro: Even non western cultures influenced by western democratic values are [seeking](http://carnegieeurope.eu/2015/09/08/puzzle-of-non-western-democracy-pub-60725) their own, unique forms of what that democracy would look like.
1.4.2.2.2. Pro: The majority of countries in Africa and the Middle East are [non-democratic.](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Democracy_in_the_Middle_East)
1.4.2.2.2.1. Con: In a poll of 12 arab countries, [71% of respondents](https://www.vox.com/2015/12/23/10653840/poll-arab-world) said they did not think Islam opposed democracy.
1.4.2.2.3. Con: Numerous countries from presumably "un-democratic" backgrounds have become well-established and thriving democracies, for example Japan, South Korea, India and Indonesia.
1.4.2.2.3.1. Pro: The Economist's [democracy index](https://infographics.economist.com/2017/DemocracyIndex/), which rates countries on how well they embody the ideas of liberal democracy, shows that democracies exist on every continent.
1.4.2.2.4. Con: The [viral spread](https://ourworldindata.org/democracy#number-of-democracies) of democracy since the mid-1800s suggests that it has universal appeal.
1.4.2.2.5. Pro: 20% of Arabs in a 12 country poll, a significant minority, hold the view that [democracy is incompatible](https://www.vox.com/2015/12/23/10653840/poll-arab-world) with Islam.
1.4.2.2.6. Pro: Not everyone globally supports [representative or direct democracy.](http://www.pewglobal.org/2017/10/16/globally-broad-support-for-representative-and-direct-democracy/) A quarter of the people in Latin-American countries do and around 15% of the people in high-income countries like Spain and Hungary have critical attitudes towards democracy.
1.4.2.2.7. Pro: In [surveys](https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/monkey-cage/wp/2016/04/09/do-americans-still-believe-in-democracy/?utm_term=.6cc75d5e3577), 10% or more of Americans are classified as authoritarians.
1.4.2.3. Con: Even if refugees may come from a culture with incompatible values, that is no guarantee their anti-democratic values will remain stagnant. The World Survey database has found that people moving to more stable, democratic cultures has in turn [increased their likelihood](http://file:///C:/Users/Jenai/Downloads/F00001107-Are_Levels_of_Democracy_Affected_by_Mass_Attitudes.pdf) of developing democratic values.
1.4.2.4. Pro: "Democracy depends on certain attitudes \(equality, individual freedom, non-violent conflict resolution, “rule of law”, etc.\) that are alledged to be inherent in cultures and inconsistent with others.". - [New York University](http://www.nyu.edu/gsas/dept/politics/faculty/clark/comparative_s02/democracy1_short.pdf) class on democracy
1.4.2.5. Pro: Pakistan and some other south Asian countries have culturally [resisted the development of democratic ideals](http://www.nytimes.com/2004/05/16/weekinreview/the-world-india-and-pakistan-so-close-but-so-different.html).
1.4.2.5.1. Con: The example of Pakistan is of little relevance at the moment as hardly any refugees come from there.
1.4.2.6. Con: Many refugees may come from non-democratic backgrounds, yet this does not mean that they are not in favor of democratic values and governance.
1.4.2.6.1. Pro: Refugees in Germany show the same attitudes towards democracy as Germans. A 2016 survey found that about the same share of them support democratic governance  \(96%\), governance by an authoritarian leader \(21%\) or by a technocracy \(55%\) \([BAMF, p. 56](https://www.bamf.de/SharedDocs/Anlagen/DE/Publikationen/Forschungsberichte/fb29-iab-bamf-soep-befragung-gefluechtete.pdf?__blob=publicationFile)\).
1.4.2.6.2. Pro: Many refugees make the long and arduous trip towards high-income countries in search for a better life - which, presumably, [includes democratic governance](http://foreignpolicy.com/2015/09/10/the-refugees-arent-fleeing-democracy-european-union-syria/) and the rights and safety this includes. It is difficult to imagine why else they would travel primarily to democratic countries.
1.4.2.6.3. Pro: The existence of non-democratic countries is not evidence that people, on the whole, do not prefer democracy. By assumption, nondemocratic governments do not reflect the preferences of their citizens.
1.4.2.6.3.1. Con: The universal democratic inclinations of all peoples everywhere is also merely an assumption. There seem to be instances even within western civilization when masses shift towards a preference for undemocratic leadership.
1.4.2.7. Con: -> See 1.4.2.6.3.
1.4.3. Con: A concept of global citizenship is not meaningful as it has no formal or practical relevance: no institution stands behind this citizenship and can confer it with rights or duties.
1.4.3.1. Con: The idea of global citizenship refers to moral obligations that we have towards each other as people living on the same planet with inevitably interlinked destinies. It does not refer to a legal understanding of citizenship.
1.4.4. Con: Not all differences can transcend national boundaries, therefore global citizenship cannot replace a set of pluralistic nation states.
1.4.4.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.15.2.1.7.3.1.4.
1.4.4.2. Con: Just because we can't transcend all differences, doesn't mean we have no responsibility to transcend the ones we can.
1.4.4.2.1. Pro: Every world culture would agree that the hungry should be fed, or that the innocent should not be persecuted.
1.4.4.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.15.2.1.7.3.
1.4.5. Con: National identity is a fundamental part of the lives of most humans, and it is far more significant to us than our status as global citizens.
1.4.5.1. Con: We can acknowledge the general moral significance of nationality while acknowledging that, in certain instances, our status as global citizens creates moral responsibilities which override the prejudices of nationality.
1.4.5.1.1. Con: We can, but there is no such thing as absolute morals so while you might consider it a given, it is certainly not for many of us. In short, it's an ideological argument, not a fact based one.
1.4.5.2. Con: National identity is an ambiguous notion which varies from social group to social group.
1.4.5.2.1. Pro: The national identity of a southern cultural group may vary greatly from the national identity of a northwestern cultural group thereby indicating that a common national identity is inclusive of an expansive domain of national identities whose unifying themes are often opposed and varied to the extent that there are cultural groups themselves.
1.4.5.3. Con: Refugees do not take away national identity from anyone. National identity and global citizenship identity do not exclude each other
1.4.5.3.1. Pro: Most people have multiple identities which are technically exclusive, but do not in practice conflict with each other. For example, people identify with their city, province, and country at the same time. Similarly, people can identify with their country and with the world at the same time.
1.4.5.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.11.
1.4.5.5. Con: Global citizenship is premised on our status as humans, which is far more fundamental to our lives than our status as members of a culture, community or nation.
1.4.5.5.1. Con: While this may be true on paper, most people intuitively first look at strangers depending on factors such as culture and nationality, not based on shared humanity.
1.4.5.5.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.15.2.1.7.3.
1.4.5.6. Pro: When asked about who they are, people often identify themselves with markers like their nationality, or their participation in a culture.
1.4.5.7. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.15.2.
1.4.6. Pro: Liberal understandings of justice and fairness support the view that it would be immoral for high-income states to refuse to take refugees in.
1.4.6.1. Con: It doesn't follow that the consequences are of the liberal ideal simply because the intentions are liberal. Pragmatically, the burden could be such that a state loses the prosperity that affords it the ability to host refugees in the first place. Becoming the environment that a refugee is fleeing from does not make for a more liberal world.
1.4.6.2. Con: The refugees did not ask for the situation they are in, but the high-income countries didn't either. It would be immoral for the refugees to have other bystanders take the responsibility of fixing their own problem.
1.4.6.3. Con: A country should not be the moral unit taking care of refugees. Individuals should.
1.4.6.3.1. Con: Individuals can hardly take in refugees without them first being admitted to enter and stay by a government. Therefore, governments still have to decide whether to take refugees in, even if the burden is then shouldered by individuals.
1.4.6.3.2. Pro: A country can't have any moral obligation because moral questions relate to individuals. Providing care for refugees is a job that individuals have to do on their own.
1.4.6.3.2.1. Con: This notion is different from established national and international practices which assume that nations do actually have responsibilities.
1.4.6.3.2.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.1.2.4.
1.4.6.3.3. Pro: The[Canadian private sponsorship model](http://ccrweb.ca/en/private-sponsorship-refugees) has shown that refugees taken in by individuals rather than government end up finding employment faster, thus having better integration outcomes.
1.4.6.3.3.1. Con: Even in the case of Canada's private sponsorship model, the government makes decisions about taking in refugees, for example through [imposing upper limits](http://www.cbc.ca/news/canada/british-columbia/canada-limits-the-number-of-privately-sponsored-syrian-refugee-applicants-in-2017-1.3911172) on the number of people that can be taken in via the program.
1.4.6.3.3.2. Con: The decision whether an individual refugee can enter Canada and stay there is still [made by the government](http://www.cic.gc.ca/english/resources/publications/ref-sponsor/section-2.asp#a2.16). This is based on screenings for eligibility and refugee status as well as for medical, criminal and security checks.
1.4.6.3.3.3. Con: A basic principle of the sponsorship program is that it is [an addition](http://ccrweb.ca/en/private-sponsorship-refugees) to the government programs to take in and resettle refugees, not a replacement. Thus, its scale and relevance for Canada's approach to refugees should not be overstated.
1.4.6.3.3.3.1. Con: In times of great need, the program is nevertheless highly significant. In 2015, [more refugees](http://ccrweb.ca/en/infographic-refugee-resettlement) were resettled to Canada based on private sponsorship than by the government.
1.4.6.3.4. Pro: Individuals should be able to sponsor refugees, in addition to national obligations based on treaties. Laws which prohibit the sponsorship of asylum-seekers, such as those in Denmark, prevent people from fulfilling these moral responsibilities.
1.4.6.3.4.1. Pro: Denmark has "opted out" of the common immigration system and judicial framework of the EU, enforcing anti-immigrant policies through judicial tradition and various laws.
1.4.6.3.4.1.1. Pro: Denmark proudly displays its anti-foreigner and anti-immigration laws on government websites.
1.4.6.3.4.1.2. Pro: Denmark disregards UNHCR guidance as non-binding "soft law" and does not enforce the articles that encourage acceptance of refugees.
1.4.6.4. Con: -> See 1.4.2.2.
1.4.6.5. Con: Think of a country as a home, you can decide who goes in. Lets compare a rich country to a rich house, if you are the owner of the house and you see someone without a home or with a smaller or poorer home, you don't have the obligation to bring them in. Yes you can bring them in but you are not obligated by force
1.4.6.5.1. Con: A more accurate analogy would be 'you' being the child of a rich household which may build a small shelter for a homeless person in the backyard. Your parents will provide the homeless person with a shelter if you are ok with it.
1.4.6.5.1.1. Pro: This does mean that it'll be less convenient and you'll possibly feel less comfortable playing in the backyard, as well as your parents having slightly less money to spend on you. However just as with a high-income country taking in refugees, this will ultimately not be more than an inconvenience on you, while saving the life of someone in desperate need.
1.4.6.6. Pro: We ought decide how our principles of justice should look like based on what people would decide on if placed in the Rawlsian [Original Position](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/original-position/). In this position, a world where high income countries take in refugees would be chosen.
1.4.6.6.1. Pro: In the Original Position people would have no idea what characteristics they would possess or where in the world they would be born \(the [Veil of Ignorance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Veil_of_ignorance)\). It then follows that they would choose principles of justice that did not excessively privilege particular characteristics, in case they were born without them.
1.4.6.6.1.1. Pro: A world in which high income countries do not take in refugees places a huge penalty on being born into a part of the world where a refugee crisis has occurred. Therefore, people in the Original Position would decide on a conception of distributive justice whereby high income countries were compelled to take in refugees.
1.4.6.6.2. Con: From the Original Position, it might be preferable for states to enact a general policy of caring primarily for their own citizens, since this would lead to better consequences on the whole.
1.4.6.6.3. Pro: In his sketch of a liberal system of international law based on the Original Position, Rawls includes as one of his principles that "Peoples have a duty to assist other peoples living under unfavorable conditions that prevent their having a just or decent political and social regime." \([SEP](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/rawls/#LawPeoLibForPol)\).
1.4.6.6.4. Con: The Rawlsian Original Position cannot function as an adequate theory of justice.
1.4.6.6.4.1. Pro: Contractualist theories like Rawls's cannot accommodate the fact of changing population sizes. For example, choosing whether to adopt Policy A might affect the future size of the population. In that case, it is impossible to decide how many people get to be in the Original Position to decide on Policy A, since it's unclear whether the people Policy A might create should get a say.
1.4.6.6.4.2. Pro: The Original Position requires us to abstract away from our everyday moral circumstances to a degree that is unreasonable. Moral reasoning should be grounded in our actual circumstances and perspective.
1.4.6.6.4.2.1. Con: Our actual circumstances make our reasoning biased. Because we are born into wealthy, privileged positions, we are more likely to support principles which allow us to keep our wealth. That is not a good form of moral reasoning.
1.4.6.7. Pro: The principles of [utilitarianism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Utilitarianism), according to which we should aim to minimise pain and maximise pleasure in the world, demand that high income countries should take in refugees.
1.4.6.7.1. Pro: The amount citizens of high income countries have to give up to take in refugees is clearly less than the pain that refugees have to endure if they are not taken in. It is therefore the case that a utilitarian moral burden to take in refugees exists.
1.4.6.7.1.1. Con: The moral burden is relative in this case. Not all citizens are equal within a country. The elite class may not be affected much, but poor and working class citizens may end up in worse conditions depending on the amount of refugees taken in. So in a Utilitarian sense, it is morally wrong to take in refugees as it will worsen the living conditions of an already disadvantaged class of society.
1.4.6.7.1.2. Pro: If someone is in severe distress and we are uniquely positioned to help them, we have a clear moral duty to do so to the best of our ability.
1.4.6.7.1.2.1. Con: High-income countries are not uniquely positioned to help them. Any country in the world could help refugees if they wanted to.
1.4.6.7.1.2.1.1. Con: High-income countries, by definition, have more disposable resources available to help.
1.4.6.7.1.2.1.2. Pro: Cultural, linguistic or religious similarities could be as significant as wealth when considering suitability to accept refugees.
1.4.6.7.1.2.1.3. Pro: [Conflicting](https://nypost.com/2016/03/22/muslim-ghettos-in-europe-are-hotbeds-for-terror/) or [incompatible](https://www.indiatoday.in/pti-feed/story/all-muslim-enclaves-believe-uk-an-islamic-country-report-658037-2016-12-04) cultures or religions can be a sufficient cause for most high-income countries to not be well suited to welcome refugees from some low-income countries.
1.4.6.7.1.2.2. Con: Precisely - to the best of our ability. As it happens, for any rich country today "taking them in" is not the way to help them to the best of our ability.
1.4.6.7.1.2.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.3.1.
1.4.6.7.1.2.3. Pro: The [Golden Rule](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golden_Rule), which features in practically all religions and ethical traditions, commends to treat others as one would like to be treated by others. One can assume that one would like to receive help and refuge if one were a refugee.
1.4.6.7.1.2.4. Pro: Not helping while in a position of power can be akin to criminal negligence. Sure, there might not be an organisation with the power to penalise the criminal negligence of nations, but that doesn't mean the negligence becomes any less criminal.
1.4.6.7.1.2.4.1. Con: For it to be criminally negligent it would have to be a deviation from the standard behavior and duties of a nation and it is not within the standard behavior for a nation to support and protect non citizens or residents.
1.4.6.7.1.2.4.2. Con: Negligence is the failure to fulfill a duty owed. Yet it is by no means clear that any duty is owed to non-citizens.
1.4.6.7.1.2.4.3. Pro: Some countries have signed [Duty to Rescue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duty_to_rescue) laws, where individuals are bound by law to protect and rescue someone \(regardless of nationality\) as long as intervening does not put them in personal danger. If such a country acts differently towards refugees, then it would be fundamentally going against something they've deemed to be criminal.
1.4.6.7.1.2.4.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.
1.4.6.7.1.2.4.5. Con: If you take this statement in isolation you will find thousands of instances where this maxim is not applied in the real world. This rationale only makes sense in furtherance of the pro-immigrant argument
1.4.6.7.1.2.4.5.1. Pro: Thousands of people die every day due to diseases that high-income countries could prevent by increasing their foreign aid budgets. Yet high-income countries are not criminally negligent for failing to prevent these deaths.
1.4.6.7.1.2.4.6. Con: From an individual standpoint that might be correct, however from the state's point of view that is not possible. There are far more humans in need of help than the state resources.
1.4.6.7.1.2.5. Pro: Human beings exist in society for the purpose of mutual benefit. Therefore we are duty bound to help those in need the best we can to fulfill our social responsibility.
1.4.6.7.1.2.5.1. Con: This claim is dependent on whether citizens of high-income countries perceive themselves to be part of the same society as refugees. Most would probably not.
1.4.6.7.1.2.5.2. Con: Humans are born into society, absent the choice of whether or not to partake in it. It is not fair to push responsibilities onto people because of conditions they did not choose to be part of.
1.4.6.7.1.2.5.2.1. Con: When an adult continues to live in a high-income country and lives an accordingly wealthy lifestyle, they have chosen to "partake in society." This is a morally significant choice and carries social responsibilities with it.
1.4.6.7.1.2.5.2.1.1. Con: Sometimes there is no other choice than keep living in that country. This does not imply a voluntary choice, but is involuntary.
1.4.6.7.1.2.6. Con: The [bystander effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bystander_effect) if applied to countries would mean that most countries expect other countries to step up and take the refugee burden rather than taking it up themselves. If morality in a democratic society is deemed by the way most people are expected to behave, then in this case, countries who don't take on refugees are not acting immorally but aren't taking a moral step either.
1.4.6.7.1.2.6.1. Con: Morality in a democratic society is not determined by the way most people are expected to behave. For example, slavery used to be common practice. This does not make it moral.
1.4.6.7.1.2.6.2. Pro: Generally, the bystander effect is a phenomenon where people are less likely to help victims when other people are present. It is a central question of human social life and has even been the basis of a [TV show](http://www.imdb.com/title/tt1361787/).
1.4.6.7.1.2.7. Con: For libertarians, positive rights are unacceptable in any form.
1.4.6.7.1.2.7.1. Pro: Negative rights mean that one can only be stopped or punished for an action against another's life and property. People should not be forced to do or share anything.
1.4.6.7.1.2.7.1.1. Pro: Positive rights are the rights which cannot be fulfilled by inaction. All your negative rights are by definition fulfilled when you are alone - then there is no-one who can violate them. Positive rights assumes there is someone who can be forced to help you, e.g. right to food means there is someone who have food and someone who is willing to threaten to the food owner by violence if he won't share the food. This is somehow blurred when talking about taxation, but the principle is the same.
1.4.6.7.1.2.7.2. Pro: If you are forced, you are a slave - positive rights means slavery. A refugee's only legitimate right is not to be harmed and our only duty is not to harm them \(but not to defend them\).
1.4.6.7.1.2.8. Con: Moral obligations are not the same as legal obligations. An obligation can be moral and/or legal. The same moral framework that suggests a duty to help refugees would also suggest that refugees have a duty to their country of origin in its time of need.
1.4.6.7.1.2.9. Pro: There are many examples of this in everyday life: if we pass a drowning child, or someone injured by the side of the road, or so forth, we ought to stop and help them. There is no relevant difference between these cases and the case of refugees.
1.4.6.7.1.2.9.1. Con: The analogy of a drowning child is apt. One has to be careful when attempting to rescue a drowning person as they have a tendency of grabbing of their potential life saver and drowning them as well. Similarly, if high-income countries accept too many immigrants they run the risk of overburdening their public services to the point of collapse and in the process bankrupting themselves. In this case they would not be wealthy; their finances would be sunk, drowned.
1.4.6.7.2. Con: Basing conceptions of justice on utilitarianism leads to some troubling implications. This indicates that we cannot use it to justify a moral burden to take in refugees.
1.4.6.7.2.1. Pro: Torture and/or murder of innocents can be justified by invoking utilitarianism if doing so could potentially save other lives. Most of us still feel that killing/torturing an innocent to save other lives is intuitively wrong, so this indicates that utilitarainism is not a very useful moral framework.
1.4.6.7.2.1.1. Con: Even if, in these cases, the recommendations of utilitarianism are overridden by other, more important moral considerations, this is not true in the case of accepting refugees.
1.4.6.7.2.2. Pro: Utilitarianism would imply that states should completely dismantle domestic welfare programs and devote all the money to foreign aid. This is not a realistic model of political decisionmaking.
1.4.6.7.2.2.1. Pro: Such an action would quickly cripple an economy, making the state incapable of providing future assistance.
1.4.6.7.2.3. Con: We do not need to fully embrace utilitarianism; we can simply note that it offers valuable advice on what to do when states have the ability to do a large amount of good for those in need.
1.4.6.7.2.4. Pro: A utilitarian approach would justify killing people for their organs in order to save multiple other people.
1.4.6.7.2.4.1. Con: A utilitarian would not slaughter healthy humans to save sick people. It is more utilitarian to keep healthy people healthy than to sacrifice them to sick people.
1.4.6.7.2.4.1.1. Con: If more people end up being healthy the utilitarian would sacrifice them.
1.4.6.7.2.5. Pro: Societies should not be expected to act according to utilitarian rules as we’re incapable of knowing any absolute moral truth. This is why we only demand that humans follow the basic rules which make civilized society possible.
1.4.6.7.2.5.1. Pro: It is impossible to know the future. Hence, society lack the ability to assess what the 'best utilitarian' option is.
1.4.6.7.2.5.1.1. Con: Even if we cannot perfectly predict the future, we can make reliable predictions about expected utility.
1.4.6.7.2.5.2. Con: We do not have to know absolute moral truth in order to have some responsibility to act in a utilitarian fashion, we can simply try to be as moral as possible given what we do know. If we assess this scenario by balancing the probabilities then acting in a utilitarian fashion seems to give us the best chance of producing the most good.
1.4.6.8. Con: States should ultimately respect the democratic mandate that elects them. If the democratic mandate supports more conservative conceptions of justice - such as those that emphasize national identity - then states should follow those conceptions of justice.
1.4.6.8.1. Con: Conservative conceptions of justice are also skeptical of untempered democratic mandate. Nations should do what is right, not necessarily what has the most popular support.
1.4.6.8.1.1. Pro: "Your representative owes you, not his industry only, but his judgment; and he betrays, instead of serving you, if he sacrifices it to your opinion. My worthy colleague says, his will ought to be subservient to yours. But government and legislation are matters of reason and judgment; and what sort of reason is that, in which the determination precedes the discussion; in which one set of men deliberate, and another decide?" - [Edmund Burke](http://press-pubs.uchicago.edu/founders/documents/v1ch13s7.html), father of Conservatism in the English-speaking world.
1.4.6.8.1.2. Pro: "By liberty I mean the assurance that every man shall be protected in doing what he believes is his duty, against the influence of authority and majorities, custom and opinion."
\[...\] "The one pervading evil of democracy is the tyranny of the majority, or rather of that party, not always the majority, that succeeds, by force or fraud, in carrying elections."
- [Lord Acton](https://mises.org/library/lord-acton-liberty-and-government), 19th century British politician.
1.4.6.8.1.3. Pro: "Democracy has never been and never can be so durable as aristocracy or monarchy; but while it lasts, it is more bloody than either. … Remember, democracy never lasts long. It soon wastes, exhausts, and murders itself. There never was a democracy yet that did not commit suicide." - [John Adams](https://www.goodreads.com/quotes/49810-i-do-not-say-that-democracy-has-been-more-pernicious) \(He is referring here to direct democracy, like Athens, as opposed to a representative democracy like the Roman Republic.\).
1.4.6.8.1.4. Pro: "Real liberty is neither found in despotism or the extremes of democracy, but in moderate governments." 
-[Alexander Hamilton](http://thefederalistpapers.org/founders/hamilton/alexander-hamilton-real-liberty-is-neither-found-in-despotism-or-the-extremes-of-democracy-but-in-moderate-governments), debate on the floor of the Constitutional Convention, 26 Jun 1787.
1.4.6.8.1.5. Pro: “So long as power can be denied to pure numbers, so long as great fields of human activity are exempt from the influence of government, so long as constitutions limit the scope of legislation: so long as these things endure, democratic despotism is kept at bay.” - Historian [Russell Kirk](http://www.kirkcenter.org/images/uploads/kirkconservativesummary.pdf), in "The Conservative Mind," summarizing Alexis de Tocqueville's views from "Democracy in America".
1.4.6.8.1.6. Pro: "I mean to live my life an obedient man, but obedient to God, subservient to the wisdom of my ancestors; never to the authority of political truths arrived at yesterday at the voting booth." - [William F. Buckley](https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/William_F._Buckley,_Jr.), "Up From Liberalism".
1.4.6.9. Con: Liberal understandings of justice aren't necessarily the only way of looking at how justice can be dispensed. Justice can be dispensed in ways which fundamentally go against liberal conceptions of it.
1.4.6.9.1. Pro: Every person should first try and better their own situation before seeking the help of others.
1.4.6.9.1.1. Con: It stands to reason that people who flee and leave everything behind - oftentimes even their loved ones - see no further possibility of improving their own lot at home.
1.4.6.9.1.1.1. Pro: According to a 2016 survey conducted in Afghanistan, more than half of those who would like to leave the country if given the opportunity cite unemployment as a reason \([Asia Foundation, p. 14](http://asiafoundation.org/wp-content/uploads/2016/12/2016_Survey-of-the-Afghan-People_full-survey.Apr2017.pdf)\).
1.4.6.9.1.2. Con: It is not clear what the majority of refugees are supposed to do in the face of armed conflict and economic deprivation, especially as most refugees have little power over their circumstances.
1.4.6.9.1.2.1. Pro: Of all refugees tracked by the UNHCR, more than half are currently children under 18 years \([UNHCR, p. 3](http://www.unhcr.org/576408cd7.pdf)\).
1.4.6.9.1.2.1.1. Pro: Of the refugees from [Syria](http://data.unhcr.org/syrianrefugees/regional.php) and [South Sudan](http://data.unhcr.org/SouthSudan/regional.php), two of the [primary countries](http://www.unhcr.org/figures-at-a-glance.html) of refugees' origin, about half or more are children under 18 years.
1.4.6.9.1.2.1.1.1. Con: Those under 18 are not necessarily vulnerable or innocent. After all, [51%](https://docs.unocha.org/sites/dms/southsudan/south_sudan_media_briefing_pack/south%20sudan%20humanitarian%20and%20development%20statistics%20-%20december%202013.pdf) of the population of South Sudan is under 18. Given the country has one of the highest [homicide rates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_intentional_homicide_rate) it can be assumed that many of the homicides were perpetrated by those under 18.
1.4.6.9.1.2.1.1.1.1. Con: South Sudan has been in a state of civil war pretty much ever since the country was founded in 2011. It is questionable whether the homicide rates are particularly telling coming from this sort of context.
1.4.6.9.1.2.1.2. Pro: In 2015, about 100.000 asylum applicants worldwide were not only children but also unaccompanied \([UNICEF, p. 9](https://www.unicef.org/publications/files/UNICEF_Education_Uprooted.pdf)\).
1.4.6.9.1.2.1.3. Pro: Among the 1.2 million refugees who applied for asylum in Germany during 2015 and 2016, a third were children under 18 \([UNICEF, p. 17](https://www.unicef.org/publications/files/UNICEF_Education_Uprooted.pdf)\).
1.4.6.9.2. Pro: Instead of catering to a symptom of conflict we should tackle the conflict itself. By allowing a way out for countries in conflict by offering their citizens a safe abode, we prevent countries which are the source of refugees to actually be fixed which is the source of the refugee problem.
1.4.6.9.2.1. Pro: Locals in positions of power such as [warlords](https://www.hrw.org/news/2017/05/05/afghanistan-warlords-grandiose-and-damaging-return) or [corrupt](https://www.migrationpolicy.org/article/finding-connections-nexus-between-migration-and-corruption) rulers should be held accountable for causing people to flee their homes. High income countries should not have to shoulder this responsibility arbitrarily.
1.4.6.9.2.2. Con: Offering citizens a safe abode does nothing to resolve a foreign conflict. Fewer innocent lives may be taken but the conflict itself remains.
1.4.6.9.2.3. Pro: -> See 1.3.5.3.
1.4.6.9.2.4. Con: Even if a citizen wishes to improve their own country, it's possible for the situation there to be so dangerous that it is better for them to leave. Given this possibility, taking in refugees does not imply that their home countries should not be improved.
1.4.6.9.2.5. Con: Refugees tend to come from countries that are either in the midst of war or have despotic rulers who have demonstrated a lack of humanity towards their citizenry, thus refusing to accept refugees is unlikely to motivate those governments to improve the situation.
1.4.6.9.2.5.1. Con: It is the responsibility of the refugees to make their governments responsible for their actions and rectify poor policies. When refugees migrate to other countries, they have no incentive to fix their own countries since they're not stakeholders in them any more.
1.4.6.9.3. Pro: A conception of distributive justice which emphasises the importance of countries prioritising their own citizens could also be legitimate.
1.4.6.9.3.1. Pro: A country has no positive moral obligations to offer aid to non-citizens.
1.4.6.9.3.1.1. Pro: If a state acknowledges a moral obligation it is acting as a [Good Samaritan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Good_Samaritan_law): helping when the need is great, and the cost to its own citizens is low. Such actions are usually seen as supererogatory: morally good, but not morally obligatory.
1.4.6.9.3.1.1.1. Con: If a state acknowledges that something is moral obligation, it implies it must do it regardless of the cost to its own citizens - out of the moral principle itself.
1.4.6.9.3.1.2. Pro: Abuse and ineptitude by a country's homeland, leaders, or cultures does not morally entitle non-citizens to hospitality elsewhere. The moral debt owed to a people is owed by one's own people, not by others.
1.4.6.9.3.1.2.1. Con: Almost by definition, the people and state of a refugee's homeland are either unwilling or unable to help them. That being the case, the moral burden to help must pass onto an actor which has the means to do so, which high income countries do.
1.4.6.9.3.1.2.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.7.1.2.
1.4.6.9.3.1.2.2. Pro: The moral obligation to address the refugee problem rests with the countries and regimes the refugees come from; change begins at home.
1.4.6.9.3.1.3. Pro: Despite what they might hope, refugees have no entitlement to citizenship even if a country takes them in, as this is done for temporary protection and as a waypoint for relocation. Anyone seeking permanent residency should be expected to go through the same immigration process as any other foreign national.
1.4.6.9.3.1.4. Pro: Morality is derived from a selfish desire to cater to one's interests. Therefore, basing morality on another person's perspective whose interests are in conflict to your own does not justify a moral burden as you, yourself are not compelled to find that action moral.
1.4.6.9.3.1.4.1. Con: Objective moral facts clearly exist. Murdering children for sport is wrong. It is wrong to torture someone needlessly.
1.4.6.9.3.1.4.2. Con: Many philosophies and religions include some form of the Golden Rule, which contradicts ethics based on selfish interests only.
1.4.6.9.3.1.5. Pro: Claiming the moral responsibility to take in refugees sends a patronizing paternalistic message to lower income countries.
1.4.6.9.3.1.5.1. Pro: By taking in refugees, high income countries sends a signal to lower income countries that they should expect to be bailed out when things fail, and that they should give up on trying to handle things themselves.
1.4.6.9.3.1.5.1.1. Con: If anything, this signal would be sent to the citizens of troubled countries and assure them that they do not stand alone. No poor country is getting bailed out if rich countries take in their citizens as refugees.
1.4.6.9.3.1.5.2. Pro: Any actual moral obligation would lie with helping the citizens of lower income countries to work and improve their own lot, in their home country. A universal citizens income paid by high income countries to low income countries could facilitate this.
1.4.6.9.3.1.5.2.1. Con: Whilst this may be helpful in some instances, the fact is that many situations that produce refugees are ones where people cannot just work to improve their lot. It is unclear what good a universal citizen's income would do for somebody living in a war zone in Syria, for example.
1.4.6.9.3.1.5.3. Con: It is significantly more paternalistic for high income countries to refuse to take in refugees on grounds that essentially say they do not know what is best for themselves.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6. Con: High-income countries have signed the Universal Declaration of Human Rights [\(UDHR\)](http://www.un.org/en/universal-declaration-human-rights/). Because they have accepted this responsibility and are capable of upholding it, they ought to host refugees.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1. Pro: By denying refugees, first world countries are breaking several articles of the [UDHR](http://www.un.org/en/universal-declaration-human-rights/).
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.1. Pro: Article 14 of the Declaration of Human Rights states that everyone has the right to seek asylum. If a country refuses to take in refugees, they are violating against the Human Rights
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.2. Pro: The first article of the [UDHR](http://www.un.org/en/universal-declaration-human-rights/) states: "All human beings are born free and equal in dignity and rights. They are endowed with reason and conscience and should act towards one another in a spirit of brotherhood."
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.3. Pro: Article 2 says that everyone is entitled to all the rights and freedoms set forth in the declaration, regardless of national or social origin.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.4. Pro: Article 3 guarantees the right to life, liberty and security of person, while Article 5 guarantees that no one shall be subjected to cruel, inhuman or degrading treatment. Denying refugees entrance means refugees are denied the rights of these articles.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.5. Pro: Article 9 states: "No one shall be subjected to arbitrary arrest, detention or exile." Arbitrary arrests and detention occurs in several places across Europe, including Greece and Hungary.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.5.1. Con: Refugees can be denied access without arbitrary arrests and detentions. Breaking Article 9 is therefore by no means a given if countries reject refugees.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.5.2. Pro: Thousands of refugees were [arbitrarily detained](https://www.amnesty.org/en/countries/europe-and-central-asia/greece/report-greece/) in Greece between 2016 and 2017.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.6. Con: No country has ever been found guilty for rejecting refugees in an international court.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.6.1. Con: This is simply an inaccurate statement. Every year there are cases brought challenging the appropriateness of State denials of refugee status, also known as [rejecting asylumseekers](https://www.hrw.org/report/2017/02/13/pakistan-coercion-un-complicity/mass-forced-return-afghan-refugees), and States are constantly, constantly found to have misapplied the rules with the result of denying asylum-seekers refugee status and protection. States have been using [subjective credibility assessments to deny protection](http://www.unhcr.org/protection/operations/51a8a08a9/full-report-beyond-proof-credibility-assessment-eu-asylum-systems.html) \(p.15\).
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.6.2. Con: The EU has taken [legal](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/jun/13/eu-takes-action-against-eastern-states-for-refusing-to-take-refugees) action against several [Eastern European](https://medium.com/trt-world/how-6-eastern-european-nations-have-hunted-down-refugees-in-policy-and-in-practice-2e6040ab9264) countries who have refused to take in refugees and some have even built fences to keep refugees out.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.6.2.1. Pro: The handling of refugees and Asylum seekers in the [EU](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Asylum_in_the_European_Union) has its base in the UDHR.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.6.2.2. Con: The proceedings against these EU countries have nothing to do with the UDHR but are based on a [2015 agreement](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2017/jun/13/eu-takes-action-against-eastern-states-for-refusing-to-take-refugees) within the EU about the relocation of refugees.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.6.3. Con: The lack of legal enforcement arises because there are no feasible mechanisms of international enforcement. The documents are still meant, on a principled level, to be morally and legally binding.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.6.4. Pro: [Israel](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/politics/syrian-refugee-crisis-how-different-countries-have-responded-france-lebanon-uk-a7220616.html) has refused to take any refugees, and has not suffered any legal repercussions.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2. Con: The [UDHR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights) is not legally binding.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.6.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.2. Pro: Signing off on an agreement may denote setting an arbitrary moral standard that countries do not mean to bind themselves by.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.2.1. Pro: Even if the UK [added parts](https://www.liberty-human-rights.org.uk/human-rights/what-are-human-rights/human-rights-act) of the ECHR to their constitution thereby binding their government, the rest of the ECHR has only a [persuasive impact](https://definitions.uslegal.com/p/persuasive-authority/) on UK legislation. This shows that countries can limit the effect International agreements have on them.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.2.2. Con: Signing the UDHR is best seen as a promise - a signal of one's intention that is not legally binding, but is still morally important.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.2.2.1. Con: Signing the UDHR without committing to them legally signals that countries want to appear morally good, but that they would not actually act morally if doing so would be inconvenient to them.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.2.2.1.1. Con: Signing the UDHR without completely committing to them in practice signals the intent and desire for the articles within them, but with the recognition that upholding them may be difficult at times.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.2.3. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.6.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3. Con: The UDHR has been used as the base of many legally binding international agreements.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.1. Pro: The United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees \(UNHCR\) was created in 1951 and was put in charge of protecting refugees and finding solutions for their future.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.2. Pro: The UDHR definitions of fundamental freedoms and human rights is used in the [United Nations Charter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universal_Declaration_of_Human_Rights#Significance_and_legal_effect), which is binding on all member states.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.3. Pro: After the Second World War, the countries of the world agreed they have a responsibility to help those fleeing persecution in their states and signed the [United Nations Convention Relating to the Status of Refugees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convention_Relating_to_the_Status_of_Refugees) in 1951.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.4. Pro: The International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights \([ICESCR\)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Economic,_Social_and_Cultural_Rights) is a multilateral treaty adopted by the UN's General Assembly in 1966. It commits its parties to work toward the granting of economic, social, and cultural rights.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.5. Pro: The International Covenant on Civil and Political Rights \([ICCPR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/International_Covenant_on_Civil_and_Political_Rights)\) is a multilateral treaty adopted by the UN's General Assembly. It commits its parties to respect the civil and political rights of individuals.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.6. Con: Many of these agreements are either ineffective, counterproductive, openly ignored or otherwise flawed. They do not provide adequate legal accountability for nations breaking these treaties.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.6.1. Pro: The [UN Human Rights Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Human_Rights_Committee) that is tasked with holding member states accountable to the international treaties on human rights is very ineffective.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.6.1.1. Pro: When [Libya](https://clg.portalxm.com/library/keytext.cfm?keytext_id=122) submitted an arguably dishonest report in 1995 - while at the same time persecuting expatriate dissidents - it took the committee three years to formulate an answer which expressed concern over the assassinations of dissidents. Libya ignored the comments and was elected chair of the committee in 2003.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.6.1.2. Pro: The committee has been [criticized](https://clg.portalxm.com/library/keytext.cfm?keytext_id=122) for meeting too infrequently, does not hold hearings, and lacks adversarial procedures. It relies on states' self-reporting.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.6.2. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.6.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.6.3. Pro: [The Dublin regulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dublin_Regulation#Criticism) fails to provide fair, efficient and effective protection for refugees. The regulation is criticized as undermining refugee rights.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.6.3.1. Pro: Increased pressure on border states [has led to](https://euobserver.com/justice/25910) unacceptable conditions for asylum claimants, and the risk of other serious human rights violations.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.6.4. Con: This just adds weight to the idea that states should make amends for failing their treaties by taking in significant numbers of refugees, or publicly admit their intentions and leave the treaties.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.4. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.2.2.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4. Con: The principles of international law typically recognise that state sovereignty is supreme over most positive international obligations. If countries do not want to take in refugees, they do not need to.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.2.2.1.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4.2. Con: Most countries accept [Customary Law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Custom_\(law\)), showing that an international law with "nearly universal acceptance" can be implemented as domestic law.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4.2.1. Pro: The US Supreme Court has long [considered itself bound](https://scholarship.law.nd.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1141&context=jleg) to apply [customary international law](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Customary_international_law).
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4.2.1.1. Pro: The US [recognises the Vienna Convention](https://www.state.gov/s/l/treaty/faqs/70139.htm) as "customary international law", and as such legally [binding upon them](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_the_Law_of_Treaties#cite_note-Is_the_United_States_a_party_to_the_Vienna_Convention_on_the_Law_of_Treaties?-4).
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4.3. Con: The principles and definitions of international law are defined in the [Vienna Convention](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_the_Law_of_Treaties), which gives no provision for state sovereignty overruling international treaties.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4.3.1. Con: The US did not sign the Vienna Convention.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4.3.1.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4.2.1.1.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4.4. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.2.2.
1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4.5. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.1.6.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.7. Con: -> See 1.4.1.
1.4.6.9.3.1.8. Con: -> See 1.4.6.7.1.2.4.
1.4.6.9.3.1.9. Pro: The countries the refugees come from, in all likelihood, would not be willing or eager to accept refugees from high-income countries. Thus, our feelings of obligation are not being considered in an equal light, and should only be held if other nations are held to the same standard.
1.4.6.9.3.1.9.1. Con: Obligation in an equal light is not reasonable since refugees are fleeing just because of inequality; in safety, economy or human rights.
1.4.6.9.3.1.9.2. Con: Many of the countries that refugees come from are not and have never been in a position in which taking in large numbers of refugees has been feasible.
1.4.6.9.3.1.9.3. Con: High-income countries rarely generate refugees.
1.4.6.9.3.1.9.4. Con: If a high-income country generated refugees, they would be more likely to seek asylum in another high-income country for the sake of lifestyle consistency and countries that are currently generating refugees would never be called upon to fulfill any obligation to take them in.
1.4.6.9.3.1.9.5. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.6.1.2.
1.4.6.9.3.1.10. Pro: This would seem to hinge on a question of morality. Morality is rarely ever a constant across cultures. If a country's morals are not the same as those of the refugees, no such moral obligation would even be registered. 

In short, can we say that any morality is universal?

And if so, that should be established before this claim can truly deserve  discussion at an objective scale.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11. Pro: Governments have responsibilities to their citizens because those citizens pay taxes and elect those governments. No such relationship exists between governments and non-citizens.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11.1. Con: Governments still possess moral responsibility towards children who were born in that country, despite the fact that children do not vote or pay taxes.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11.1.1. Con: Responsibility to children exists as an extension of affinity that those children have towards the state by virtue of their parents being a part of the state. The state does not have that same affinity towards children from other countries and therefore does not help them.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11.1.1.1. Con: The fact that children are closely related to citizens of a country is not sufficient to explain a state's duty to those children. For example, if Bob's brother lives in another country, Bob's country does not owe duties to Bob's brother.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11.2. Con: Governments still possess a moral obligation to not do active harm to citizens of other states if they pose no threat. There is no moral distinction between turning a refugee away when they could have been helped and actively harming that refugee.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11.2.1. Pro: The death toll of migrants attempting to cross into different countries exceeded [10,000 in 2016](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/sep/17/migrant-death-toll-2016-syria-united-nations). Many of these were refugees trying to enter countries through illegal means. This means that governments know that they are condemning many to death by enforcing border controls and not taking refugees in.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11.2.1.1. Con: If refugees go for illegal means and try to force entry into a country, that country owes no obligation to let them in. Just as an act of murder committed under desperation is still murder. If someone acts illegally, while their situation might be sympathetic, that person does not deserve to be accommodated.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11.2.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11.2.3. Con: A government doesn't, and should not be expected to, have an individual trait like morality. It is not the government's responsibility to enforce morality, or make decisions that would impact its populous based upon morality alone.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11.2.3.1. Pro: The government is not a moral entity, however religious organizations are. They receive a tax exempt status for the purpose of allowing them to aid their communities according to their morality. It is the responsibility of the church in a community to spearhead humanitarian efforts, not the government.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11.2.4. Con: There is absolutely a moral distinction between doing nothing to prevent harm and doing something to bring about harm.
1.4.6.9.3.1.11.2.4.1. Pro: Some philosophers have [argued](https://muse.jhu.edu/chapter/682033) that whereas it is justiﬁed to allow one person to die so that one can save a larger number of people, it is not permissible to kill one person to achieve the same purpose. These are not equivalent moral deeds. Similarly, not offering help to refugees is morally incomparable to actively harming them.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12. Con: Individuals should not be treated better or worse than other individuals for circumstances beyond their control, for example their country of origin and nationality.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.1. Con: Individuals cannot be treated independently of their origin, because they are products of their origin, both genetic and environmental. They are steeped in the language, cultural norms, religion and world view of their society, and derive their identity from their bloodline and the experiences in their formative years. Saying that they have no control over their origin is like saying that a work of art has no control over who created it - it does not even make sense.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.1.1. Pro: An individual is a temporary instantiation of their family, nation and civilization, which persist even though individuals die, and shape their members through their values, beliefs, traditions, institutions, and material wealth.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.1.2. Pro: An individual is brought into existence by a particular set of circumstances, meaning that any specific individual could not have come into existence at any other place or time or in a different state of affairs than was actually the case. Since an individual is determined by genetics and environmental inputs, there is no way to replicate an individual either from different biological parents or in different environmental circumstances.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.2. Con: In a struggle between a group with strong in-group preference and another group without, the latter will be eradicated. Treating members of a foreign nation equal to one's own, while they treat each other preferentially, must lead to the foreign nation conquering the host nation. A nation, in order to continue its existence, must therefore treat its members preferentially; this is not a question of moral worth, but of group survival strategy.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.2.1. Pro: It is also a basis for showing bias in allowing to enter those that are deemed more likely to become loyal group members.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.2.2. Con: Immigrants generally change their perceived in-group to the host nation.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.2.2.1. Pro: 92% of Muslims in Britain responded to a large-scale survey that they strongly feel like they belong to the country, even though only half of them were born there \([ICM, p. 24, 45](https://www.icmunlimited.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/12/Policy-Exchange-ICM-Muslims-Survey-web.pdf)\).
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.2.2.1.1. Con: At the same time, 87% of the Muslims in Britain who were born abroad also state that they feel a strong connection to the country of their birth \([ICM, p. 52](https://www.icmunlimited.com/wp-content/uploads/2016/12/Policy-Exchange-ICM-Muslims-Survey-web.pdf)\).
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.2.2.2. Pro: Across major European countries, three out of four Muslims state in surveys that they feel attached or even strongly attached to their country of residence, even though 70% of them are first-generation immigrants \([FRA, p. 19f., 62](https://www.google.co.kr/url?sa=t&rct=j&q=&esrc=s&source=web&cd=1&cad=rja&uact=8&ved=0ahUKEwilzPrp0a3XAhXHTrwKHS0ODucQFggmMAA&url=http%3A%2F%2Ffra.europa.eu%2Fsites%2Fdefault%2Ffiles%2Ffra_uploads%2Ffra-2017-eu-minorities-survey-muslims-selected-findings_en.pdf&usg=AOvVaw2LxLze8-fo-4pXD6h--bXY)\).
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.2.2.3. Pro: Only [four in ten](http://www.pewforum.org/2017/07/26/demographic-portrait-of-muslim-americans/) Muslims in the US were born there, yet [95%](http://www.pewresearch.org/fact-tank/2017/08/09/muslims-and-islam-key-findings-in-the-u-s-and-around-the-world/) of American Muslims say they are proud to be American.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.2.3. Con: In the modern world there is no danger of generous nations being "conquered" by other nations.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.3. Con: -> See 1.1.8.2.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.4. Con: Denying the moral relevance of citizenship leads to absurd conclusions: for example, that it is unjustly discriminatory for countries to provide welfare or social security payments only to their own citizens or residents.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.4.1. Con: In fact, most countries do not provide welfare services only to their citizens. Individuals staying in a foreign country can, for example, usually sign up for the national health care system. This indicates that countries provide services to whoever pays taxes \(or the required fees\), and do not discriminate on the basis of arbitrary factors like citizenship.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.4.1.1. Pro: For example, in Germany foreigners [can usually join](https://www.sympat.me/germany-healthcare-insurance/) the public health system or opt for private insurance and, in the case of EU citizens, potentially [can even claim](https://www.angloinfo.com/how-to/germany/working/unemployment/unemployment-benefits) unemployment benefits.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.4.2. Con: In fact, it would be unjust for a country to only provide police protection or emergency treatment to its citizens. Similarly, if a refugee arrives in desperate need, it is discriminatory to refuse to assist them on the grounds that they are not a resident or citizen.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.5. Con: This is a morally valid point of view from an individual's perspective, but a state is meant to serve its citizens' interests and neglect those of others.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6. Con: Wealth is also determined by arbitrary factors. A person born into a rich family will have a much better life than one born into a poor one since wealth is used to fulfill one's needs and wants. If wealth is an acceptable way to determine who gets what in society, then citizenship should be no different as both operate in a similarly discriminatory fashion.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.1. Pro: The example of wealth shows that other considerations - the benefits of a capitalist system, for example - can trump brute considerations of fairness. Even though capitalism is "unfair," it is nevertheless a morally acceptable system.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.1.1. Con: Refusing to accept refugees is not as beneficial as capitalism.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.1.2. Con: Capitalism cannot simultaneously be an unfair and a morally acceptable system. The existence and dominance of capitalism only proves that it is a good system for those with the power to change it.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.1.2.1. Con: Capitalism can be unfair but moral, if other aspects of the system are sufficiently morally good to outweigh the moral harm of the unfairness.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.1.3. Pro: Citizenship does not seek to differentiate between people's moral worthiness, it categorizes people into a system which enables states to effectively function. While it is flawed and unfair, there is no viable alternative to replace it with.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.1.3.1. Con: Treating citizens and non-citizens as of equal moral worth does not do away with the concept of citizenship and the many functional needs it fulfills.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.1.4. Con: Capitalism is focused on wealth accumulation and profits, which necessarily means that human rights and interests are not its main interest. This interest of [profit over people](https://www.dailykos.com/stories/2012/2/16/1065641/-Profit-Over-People-The-Immorality-of-Capitalism) makes it immoral.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.2. Con: Two wrongs do not make a right. Enabling wealth discrimination does not make discrimination based on citizenship any less wrong.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.2.1. Con: Claiming that wealth discrimination is wrong involves rejecting the capitalist system in its entirety, including whatever benefits it provides. This is illogical as capitalism is currently the prevalent economic system.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.2.1.1. Con: Enabling unfair conditions should be considered wrong regardless of existing norms or inaction.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.2.1.1.1. Pro: Previously, people discriminated on the basis of membership in racial or religious groups. We now recognize that these prejudices are unacceptable, and should extend the same logic to discrimination on the basis of nationality.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.2.1.2. Con: Rejecting capitalism is clearly not inherently illogical even if it is currently the prevalent economic system. There are numerous bodies of still influential philosophical, political and economic thought that oppose it.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.2.1.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.1.4.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.2.1.2.2. Pro: Marxists argue that the inequalities built into capitalism \(namely that some individuals own capital and others do not\) leads to the mass [exploitation of workers by capitalists](https://www.marxists.org/subject/japan/sakisaka/exploitation.htm). This is clearly immoral.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.7. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.1.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.8. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.12.6.2.1.1.1.
1.4.6.9.3.1.12.9. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.15.2.1.6.1.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13. Con: While countries may not have moral obligations to non-citizens by default, when countries interfere with the affairs of other countries and create harmful consequences, they acquire moral obligations as a result of their actions.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.1. Con: High income countries did not cause the current suffering that leads people to flee.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.1.1. Pro: Refugees from Afghanistan, the [second-largest source](http://ec.europa.eu/eurostat/statistics-explained/index.php/File:Countries_of_origin_of_\(non-EU\)_asylum_seekers_in_the_EU-28_Member_States,_2015_and_2016_\(thousands_of_first_time_applicants\)_YB17.png) of refugees in recent years, are fleeing from a [conflict between](https://www.hrw.org/news/2015/09/16/dispatches-why-afghans-are-leaving) the government and the Taliban, which preceded the international presence in the country.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.1.1.1. Con: The presence of the Taliban in Afghanistan does predate the invasion of 2001, but it does not predate US and British support of the Mujahideen in the 1980s. This directly caused the series of events that lead to the [birth of the Taliban](http://www.differencebetween.net/miscellaneous/difference-between-taliban-and-mujahideen/).
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.1.2. Pro: Refugees from Nigeria, which is [among the largest sources](http://ec.europa.eu/eurostat/statistics-explained/index.php/File:Countries_of_origin_of_\(non-EU\)_asylum_seekers_in_the_EU-28_Member_States,_2015_and_2016_\(thousands_of_first_time_applicants\)_YB17.png) of refugees in recent years, often flee the violence and instability caused [by Boko Haram](http://www.bbc.com/news/av/world-africa-35250284/inside-the-refugee-camps-as-nigerians-flee-boko-haram), a regional extremist group.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.3.6.4.4.2.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.1.4. Pro: The 2017 crisis surrounding hundreds of thousands of [Rohingya refugees](http://news.nationalgeographic.com/2017/09/rohingya-refugee-crisis-myanmar-burma-spd/) from Myanmar has its root in their domestic persecution and ethnic cleansings.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.1.4.1. Con: The ethnic mess within Myanmar is historically linked to the strategic division of power and manipulated creation of an artificial state by the former British Colony \([Smith, p. 22](http://www.ibiblio.org/obl/docs3/Ethnic_Groups_in_Burma-ocr.pdf)\).
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.1.5. Con: -> See 1.3.6.4.4.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.2. Con: Past behavior does not predetermine any present or future obligations.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3. Pro: High-income countries have a continuing history of colonialism and thus have a historical obligation towards refugees that results from their actions.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.1. Con: Some refugees come from former colonial powers, such as Iran or Turkey, while some high-income countries have been colonized, such as Finland or Ireland. If the moral obligation is based on practice of colonialism, it would follow that it would be more moral for the UK to accept Indian refugees than for Finland to accept Iranian refugees, even if the Iranian refugees were in greater danger than the Indian, and Finland would be in a better position to accept refugees than the UK.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.1.1. Con: A history of colonialism is not an overriding justification for allocating refugees to a particular country; it is merely one reason among many, and can be outweighed by factors like the Iranian refugees being in greater danger.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.2. Pro: If a country is morally responsible for harming someone - for example, by causing them to be displaced - that country has an obligation to help that person as much as possible.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.2.1. Con: If a country is not morally responsible for harming someone, that country does not have an obligation to help that person.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.2.2. Con: Relations between countries differ from those between humans. If a state has a bigger army and stays in peace with a little island, it's irrational but it might still have some economic interests. But power relations is what countries always have in mind at the end of the day and military budgets confirm this consideration.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3. Con: High-income countries already discharge the obligations placed on them by colonialism through foreign aid and charitable donations.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.1. Con: This is hardly an achievement. More than half of all OECD countries spend [less than a quarter of a percent](http://www2.compareyourcountry.org/oda?cr=oecd&lg=en) of their GNI on Official Development Aid and all but six miss the target of 0.7% that they agreed on.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.2. Pro: The OECD spends about [$135b a year](https://www.theguardian.com/global-development/2015/apr/08/foreign-aid-spending-2014-least-developed-countries) on foreign aid.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.2.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.1.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.3. Con: Foreign aid provided to developing countries does not offset the damage currently being done to them.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.3.1. Pro: Aid from high income countries makes it harder for the recipient countries to develop.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.3.1.1. Pro: The [subsidiarity principle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subsidiarity) is more useful in this context. Central governments are as likely to harm as to help. Let individuals have the right to sponsor refugees.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.3.1.2. Pro: Economists have [argued](https://www.theguardian.com/business/economics-blog/2014/jan/06/pros-cons-aid-developing-economies) that much of the aid we currently spend does not achieve its goal.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.3.1.2.1. Pro: Although these economists are optimistic about the prospects of aid in general, they would agree that the aid high income countries have given has not been beneficial enough to discharge their burden.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.3.2. Pro: Though foreign aid is certainly welcome, alone it is not enough to rectify the material and cultural devastation caused by colonialism.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.3.3. Pro: Aid either actively engenders [corruption](https://www.spectator.co.uk/2014/01/why-aid-fails/), or does nothing to combat corrupt institutions, which are the primary cause of the poverty of countries.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.3.3.1. Con: Aid can help [strengthen institutional structures](http://blogs.worldbank.org/africacan/aid-and-corruption) which fight corruption.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.3.4. Con: Foreign aid has been crucial in enabling the development of many formerly poor countries; for example, [South Korea](https://www.theguardian.com/global-development/poverty-matters/2011/nov/28/south-korea-development-model).
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.4. Con: The amount that high-income countries give in foreign aid is a lot less than what they gain through the continuous [exploitation](http://www.globalpolicyjournal.com/blog/12/12/2013/donors%E2%80%99-dilemma-aid-reverse-how-poor-countries-develop-rich-countries) of developing countries.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.5. Pro: High-income countries engage in substantial trade with developing ones, an activity which helps those countries accelerate their development.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.5.1. Con: Trade is also beneficial to the high income countries, so trading with poor countries does not really involve discharging a moral obligation to help those countries.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.5.2. Pro: [Singapore](https://www.economist.com/blogs/economist-explains/2015/03/economist-explains-23) went from a very poor to a very wealthy country thanks to trade.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.6. Pro: Worldwide poverty has [decreased massively](https://www.economist.com/blogs/economist-explains/2013/06/economist-explains-0) over the last few decades. This is in large part due to the effort of high-income countries in giving foreign aid, creating development institutions like the World Bank, and engaging in trade.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.6.1. Con: [Three quarters](https://www.economist.com/news/leaders/21578665-nearly-1-billion-people-have-been-taken-out-extreme-poverty-20-years-world-should-aim) of the global reduction of poverty during the past decades were achieved by one country: China.
