Development aid played a very small role in achieving this.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.6.2. Con: High average income can disguise significant income inequalities which may cause displacement through extreme poverty.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.6.3. Con: Foreign aid and especially the [world bank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Bank#Criticisms) are used to influence and exploit poorer countries.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.6.3.1. Pro: The help is mainly issued to promote a capitalist and imperialistic economic system. Often only the ruling class of a country benefits from foreign aid and it is contributing to corruption and inequality.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.7. Pro: [Australia, New Zealand, the US, and the UK](https://www.theguardian.com/news/datablog/2010/sep/08/charitable-giving-country) are high on the list of countries who give large quantities of money to charity in private donations.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.7.1. Con: Charitable giving by private individuals does not fulfill the national obligations that result from a history of colonial governance. These type of obligations must be fulfilled by the national government.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.7.2. Con: Many of those donations go to domestic charities, within those states.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.8. Con: The total resources drained during colonization by Britain are [too much](https://blast-from-indias-past.quora.com/Colonial-Damage-in-Numbers). [Currently](http://www.huffingtonpost.co.uk/entry/uk-foreign-aid-budget_uk_57d953afe4b00f741735477f) the aid provided to countries by Britain that composed India are a mere percentage of the amount of money drained from these countries.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.8.1. Con: If Britons can be collectively held liable for the deeds of some elites then it stands to reason that Indians are indebted for all Western contributions to India, including vaccines, institutions, inventions, knowledge and infrastructure.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.4. Pro: Even some high-income countries that never had colonies would not be in their current position if not for colonialism. Among them is, for example, [Switzerland](http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/pdf/10.1080/1369801X.2015.1042395).
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.5. Con: It is anything but clear what kind of moral obligations are imposed by the history of colonialism.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.5.1. Pro: There is no clear standard for delineating the time span within which events are counted as part of this history of colonialism.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.5.1.1. Pro: Refugees are a side effect of time and shifting geopolitical relationships. Conditions 70 years ago are themselves  determined by conditions before then. Exactly why that cut-off point is chosen and not, for example, the fall of the Holy Roman Empire, isn’t clear.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.5.2. Pro: There is no clear standard for defining what countries count as colonial powers and how their colonial history should translate into present obligations.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6. Con: Historical actions are irrelevant to present-day moral responsibility.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1. Con: Countries are moral actors which can possess moral responsibility, even when the people living within them change.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1.1. Con: "Countries" are not meaningfully distinct actors - a country is just formed out of the people within it. If moral responsibility cannot be transferred between people, it cannot persist with a country.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1.1.1. Con: Wholes can have emergent properties even when none of their parts have that property. This can be true of moral responsibility also. Thus, while no single individual may be responsible for the actions of the past, collectivities may still be.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1.1.1.1. Con: Morality is present on individual level, so can not be an emergent trait on collective level. It can be inherited, given sufficient consistency, which is not the case.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1.1.1.2. Pro: A company that, under the leadership of a chief executive, cheats many customers out of their money may still be morally liable to pay recompense even after the chief executive is gone.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1.1.2. Pro: Assuming that countries can have an individual trait like "morality" is a [fallacy of composition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fallacy_of_composition).
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1.2. Pro: Countries clearly have a responsibility to pay back their debt, even if the initial loan was taken out by a past government or generation.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1.2.1. Con: We enforce this as a legal obligation so that the system of international lending can function. However, there is no real underlying moral responsibility to pay back debt accumulated by a previous government.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1.3. Pro: Germany [continues](http://www.spiegel.de/international/germany/germany-to-pay-772-million-euros-in-reparations-to-holocaust-survivors-a-902528.html) to pay reparations to Holocaust survivors.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1.4. Pro: States are separate entities legally and can sue or be sued. This autonomy is integral for state functioning and is present when deciding whether to take in refugees; there is no automatic moral burden imposed.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2. Con: Colonialism is a feature of the recent past and the present, as well as the distant past.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.1. Con: Even if colonialist activities happened in the past few decades, the majority of people currently alive in high-income countries would not have voted for the governments who perpetrated those actions and are therefore not complicit in those actions.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.1.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.2. Pro: Most of the military interventions by high income countries which have contributed to the refugee crisis are recent, occurring in the past several years or decades.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.3. Pro: Many African countries only gained independence from colonial powers in the [1960s or 1970s](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decolonisation_of_Africa).
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.4. Pro: Many people living in high income countries today were alive when remnants of colonialism existed, and many voted for governments who pursued these policies. These people are directly morally responsible for the consequences of that colonialism.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.5. Pro: Consumers in high income countries are able to enjoy cheap consumer goods off the back of extremely poorly paid manufacturing workers in the developing world, while corporations in high income countries turn a tidy profit through this process. This is an exploitative, neo-colonial relationship.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.5.1. Con: Sweatshops are [beneficial for the economic development](https://qz.com/800707/new-research-finds-sweatshops-may-be-a-necessary-evil-in-the-development-of-economies/) of poor countries.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.5.1.1. Con: Whilst sweatshops may be better than the alternative of no work at all, it is still the case that corporations from high income countries could pay workers better wages and still make huge profits. Instead they abuse their poor bargaining position to pay them as little as possible, so a relationship of exploitation still exists.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.5.2. Pro: Economic and social inequality are caused in large part by a discrepancy created by the current world economic and social system: goods and wealth cross borders much more easily than people do. Rich countries have taken and take advantage from this. We living in developed countries should  recognize we didn't deserve this wealth but mostly inherited it. Altruism tells us to share with and welcome the less lucky even if that implies losing undeserved peace/safety. Rebalance to reduce differences.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.5.3. Pro: High-income countries often exploit worse human-rights conditions in underdeveloped countries to their own profit.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.6. Pro: Extremely generous agricultural subsidies in the US and the EU lead to overproduction and the dumping of excess produce into developing markets in Africa and Central America. This enriches farmers in high income countries at the direct cost of devastating farmers in the developing world.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.6.1. Con: This leads to cheaper food prices in developing countries, which is good for poor consumers.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.6.2. Pro: Neocolonialism is the practice of using economic, cultural or political pressures \(in lieu of direct military control\) to influence other countries, especially former colonies. Dumping excess agricultural produce onto developing markets prevents those countries from being able to autonomously develop their own agricultural sector, so it falls within this definition.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3. Pro: People do not choose to be born into their country. It is thus unfair to force them to "inherit" moral responsibility for actions beyond their control.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1. Con: If somebody gains from being born into a country that benefits from a colonial past and present, they still owe some kind of responsibility to the victims of those actions, even if they are not directly morally culpable.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.1. Pro: Given that high income countries have benefited from colonialism, they have a moral duty to return the products of colonial plunder by compensating its worst-off victims, refugees.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.2. Con: Given the world's history of warfare and slavery, all ownership of property can be causally traced back at some point to some injustice, yet not all ownership is illegitimate.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.2.1. Pro: Since the alternative is abandon the idea of legitimate property altogether, in considering whether property ownership is legitimate we should only consider whether the current owner legitimately acquired it, not how it was acquired several generations ago.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.2.1.1. Con: Restituting property to the original owners or their heirs can also be a question of [justice](http://www.pbs.org/newshour/updates/why-finding-nazi-looted-art-is-a-question-of-justice/). Art looted by the Nazi regime from all over Europe is returned to the owners or their families, often after an extensive research effort.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.2.2. Con: If all initial acquisition of property was based on injustice then all property ownership is illegitimate to some degree. Distributional decisions, including whether or not to take in refugees, should be based on purely utilitarian concerns, such as need. Therefore, we should still accept refugees.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.2.2.1. Pro: Many [influential accounts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Entitlement_theory) of property rights include just initial acquisition as a necessary condition for just ownership.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.2.2.2. Con: If property rights can only be justified on utilitarian grounds, then the state would be justified in forcibly expropriating everyone's property to donate to the most efficient charities.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.2.2.2.1. Con: Strong property rights can still be justified on utilitarian grounds on the basis that they provide the incentives needed to create wealth in the first place.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.2.2.2.1.1. Pro: Given the state's propensity to abuse its power, and the chaos and uncertainty that would be caused by expropriation, these are strong utilitarian reasons to favour robust property rights in most cases.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3. Pro: Colonialism consisted of the extraction of wealth and natural resources from the developing world on a massive scale. Citizens of high income countries continue to benefit from this extraction and its legacy. Since this wealth was effectively stolen from the developing world, it is illegitimate to prevent refugees from these countries having access to it through the enforcement of borders.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.1. Con: By this logic, all residents of developing countries have a right to access high income countries. It is unclear why this is an argument for singling out refugees in particular.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.1.1. Pro: Even if refugees are the most vulnerable residents of developing countries, this does not mean that they are the people who have been most harmed by colonialism specifically. So it is not clear why they have the strongest claim to the products of colonialism.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.1.2. Con: It may well be the case that all residents of developing countries have a right to access high income countries. Refugees are the group most likely to actually need to avail of this though, and as such a focus on them is warranted.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.2. Con: This wealth could instead be returned to the victims of colonialism through foreign aid or other methods. There is no reason that compensation must be paid by accepting refugees, specifically.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.2.1. Con: The amounts that high income countries spend on foreign aid are negligible, relative to what those countries could spend. In 2013, [only 5 members of the OECD](https://www.theguardian.com/global-development-professionals-network/2015/sep/09/foreign-aid-which-countries-are-the-most-generous) were donating more than 0.7% of their GDP.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.2.1.1. Con: This is an argument for increasing foreign aid, not increasing refugee intake.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.3. Pro: High income countries have benefited massively from the slavery, trade, and industrialisation that [colonialism](https://www.theguardian.com/politics/2005/aug/20/past.hearafrica05) that colonialism brought with it.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.4. Pro: The foundation of the British welfare state was funded to a large degree by colonial extraction, as Ernest Bevin once explained in a cabinet meeting \([Weiler, 1993, p. 172](https://books.google.ie/books?id=ydEeDAAAQBAJ&pg=PT5&lpg=PT5&dq=p+weiler+ernest+bevin+biography&source=bl&ots=bO16NOfZwb&sig=jVJzJAVEncv0WGg6q2XceYeQ2NI&hl=en&sa=X&ved=0ahUKEwiK9vrwut7UAhXJbVAKHTa0DSwQ6AEINDAD#v=onepage&q&f=false)\).
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.5. Pro: In countries [with economies of scale](http://blogs.worldbank.org/allaboutfinance/did-yesterday-s-patterns-of-colonial-exploitation-determine-today-s-patterns-of-poverty), colonists exploited local labour in order to extract and ship away the country's natural resources.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.4. Con: It is impossible in practice to determine whether the wealth that individuals currently possess is legitimately acquired, or in some way linked to colonialism.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.2. Con: While the generation of people who ruled the state that committed atrocities in the past is no longer alive, the operating state itself still exist.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.2.1. Pro: While a parent may not pass down their moral responsibility onto their child, they will continue to personally inhabit that responsibility for as long as they live. A state that has continued to exist should also continue to be morally responsible for past actions committed by it.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.2.1.1. Con: A state is not a living being and only functions by the actions of actual living beings. Only humans are morally responsible for their actions.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.3. Con: It is no less unfair than denying refuge to people who have had to flee their home country for incidents that were beyond their control.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.4. Con: All moral responsibilities vary according to arbitrary circumstance. If a person passes a child drowning in a pond, that person inherits a moral responsibility to save the child, even though the source of their responsibility is arbitrary circumstance.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.4.1. Con: This is a flawed interpretation of the "inherit" concept - a person may have a moral responsibility to help a drowning child. The person didn't inherit the situation. This chain of reasoning talks about whether actions of predecessors mean people today inherit their responsibilities. The parent argument is covered [here](https://www.kialo.com/high-income-countries-have-a-moral-obligation-to-take-in-refugees-2214/2214.0=2214.1+2214.27+2214.80).
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4. Pro: Moral responsibility does not persist across generations - if one's parent committed an ill, they have no moral obligation to that person. The fact that some of their ancestors did something wrong does not create any special responsibility for them.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.2.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.2. Con: If an entity has benefited from \(and continues to benefit from\) an injustice in the past, that entity bears responsibility for it until they have made some recompense.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.2.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.2.2. Pro: High income countries owe restitution to under-developed countries for past and present wrongs, helping refugees seeking asylum is the least they can do.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.2.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.3. Con: If one's parent stole something from someone and then passed it on to them, they would owe an obligation to the victim to return their property. Colonialism consisted of theft of natural resources, labour through slavery and many other things, so the same principle applies.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.3.2. Pro: If somebody found a stolen item, we would still expect them to return that item to the victim, even if it wasn't their 'fault' that they found it.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.3.3. Con: Logically, this extends to grandparents, great-grandparents, great-great-grandparents, etc. Everyone in the world is guilty of the crimes of all of their ancestors.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.5. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.2.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.6. Pro: Historical actions should not be a criteria for justifying current moral responsibility, as that provides a slippery slope.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.6.1. Pro: Political history used as a basis for current actions is problematic as many countries have committed historical transgressions or provided benefits to each other. Holding onto them biases present actions to not be held on their own merit.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.6.1.1. Con: Absolving high income countries of moral responsibility to help refugees cannot be considered to be a beneficial move for the refugees nor the countries which the refugees are fleeing from.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.6.1.2. Pro: Britain and France had many long standing [conflicts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War) but during later years, that did not stop them from [bonding together](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triple_Entente) against a common foe when threatened.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.6.1.2.1. Con: If both parties of a past conflict has mutually decided to forget about that conflict and move forward, the moral responsibility has been abolished.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.6.2. Con: Even if this is generally the case, it is clear that some past crimes were so horrible that they should and do inform current moral responsibility. For example, Germany paid reparations for the Holocaust. Many of the crimes of colonialism fall within a similar bracket.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.6.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.6.4. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.6.5. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.4.2.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.6.6. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.1.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.7. Con: Colonialism and imperialism were not only practiced by today's high-income countries, but also by other countries. The former should therefore not be singled out for any moral obligations that arise from these past practices.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.7.1. Pro: For example, slavery was [practiced](https://web.archive.org/web/20040804001522/http://www.slaveryinamerica.org/history/hs_es_indians_slavery.htm) among the Native tribes of North America.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.7.1.1. Con: Most enslaved Indians were adopted into the tribes as replacement for fallen warriors.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.7.2. Con: Traditions of slavery varied widely between Africa and the Americas.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.7.2.1. Pro: Slavery in Africa was not heritable, ie. children born to slaves were free.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.7.3. Con: The transatlantic slave trade was by far the biggest. Research shows that [over 12 million](http://www.pbs.org/wnet/african-americans-many-rivers-to-cross/history/how-many-slaves-landed-in-the-us/) Africans were traded to the Americas.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.8. Con: Not all high income countries are former colonial powers. Some, such as Ireland and Finland, are themselves former colonies. It is therefore unfair to place a moral obligation on them for actions they were not complicit in.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.8.1. Con: Whilst high income countries which were not colonial powers did not take part in colonialism themselves, they were still material beneficiaries of the global colonial process which saw wealth and resources extracted from colonies and transferred to Europe and North America. They are therefore still complicit.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.8.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.8.1.2. Con: Quite some European countries, eg. contemporary Czech Republic, Slovakia, Croatia, Romania etc., had no benefit from colonies because they were occupied territory \(by the [Turkish Osmanian empire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe) or by the [northern branch of the Austro-Habsburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_of_White_Mountain) empire\).
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.8.1.3. Con: Under this logic, [Iraq](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Iraq#Ottoman_Iraq) would share the same level of complicity and guilt as [Serbia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_Serbia) or [Croatia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Croatian%E2%80%93Ottoman_wars) because both were conquered and occupied by the very same Turkish Osman empire.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.8.1.4. Con: In many cases,[time for which](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/German_East_Africa) many of the African territories were occupied by the colonial powers \(decades\) is comparable with [time for which](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe) certain European countries were occupied by earlier colonial powers like the Turkish Osman Empire or [Mongolian raiders](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Rus%27). Hence, either the Mongolians and Turks share the same level of complicity as developed European Middle- and Souther-European nations, or any formerly colonized or occupied territory needs to be excluded from the "colonist guilt".
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9. Pro: Colonialism by high income countries is a [major driving factor](http://blogs.worldbank.org/allaboutfinance/did-yesterday-s-patterns-of-colonial-exploitation-determine-today-s-patterns-of-poverty) behind the persistent poverty of certain countries; poverty which in turn creates economic migration or engenders conflicts that create refugees.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9.1. Con: Former colonies have been independent for decades now, and have had every opportunity to reform the institutions established by colonial powers.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9.2. Pro: The export of slaves, for the example to the Caribbean and the US, in the context of colonialism hurt the African continent and stifled its development until today \([Nunn, p. 139](http://scholar.harvard.edu/files/nunn/files/the_long_term_effects.pdf), [Whatley/Gillezeau, p. 1](http://www-personal.umich.edu/~baileymj/Whatley_Gillezeau.pdf)\).
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9.2.1. Con: The principal problem of African development is unlikely the abduction of male population but more the long-term consequences of [colonization](http://www.academicjournals.org/article/article1425047376_Asafa.pdf).
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9.3. Pro: Colonialism prevented [economic development](https://papers.ssrn.com/sol3/papers.cfm?abstract_id=2029575) in former colonies.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9.3.1. Pro: Colonizing countries set up [exploitative institutions](https://economics.mit.edu/files/4123) that persist to the present, harming the economic development of the colonies.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9.3.1.1. Con: The very source cited in the parent indicates in table 1 that European settlement existing in 1900 is an indicator for slightly better economic performance on average in modern times.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9.4. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.6.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9.5. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.5.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9.6. Pro: As [Frantz Fanon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Wretched_of_the_Earth) argues, colonialism unleashed a form of psychological violence whose effects linger to this day. Language was used to mold imperialist identities \(eg. master and slave\). This continues to negatively effect societal and individual mental health in post-colonial states today.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9.6.1. Pro: What Fanon describes is effectively a form of [inter-generational PTSD](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transgenerational_trauma). Constructing a successful post-colonial state, and hence avoiding poverty and conflict, is especially difficult when citizens and society often suffer from an inter-generational psychological complex linked to inferiority and slavery.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.10. Pro: -> See 1.3.6.4.4.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11. Con: Outreach to the world by high income countries was beneficial for developing countries. If anything, these countries have an obligation to pay back the investment made.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.1. Pro: Colonization brought development.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.1.1. Pro: The British built large institutions like courts, universities, and administrative agencies in India. This facilitated the transformation to democracy later on.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.1.2. Pro: The [Dutch Ethical Policy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dutch_Ethical_Policy) focused on economic development of Indonesia.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.1.2.1. Con: This policy is remarkable because it contrasts with the previous Dutch policy of extracting profit above all else, which devastated Dutch colonies.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.1.3. Pro: In South African territories formerly populated by tribal groups technology and infrastructure was brought by colonizing Afrikaans.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.1.4. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.9.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.1.5. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.2. Pro: Western colonialism brought [civilization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civilizing_mission) to the world.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.2.1. Pro: South African states is a great example where colonizing power brought huge advances in technology and infrastructure. This economical boom allowed local population to flourish.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.2.2. Con: Many [civilizations](https://www.ancienthistorylists.com/ancient-civilizations/10-oldest-ancient-civilizations-ever-existed/) existed long before western ones came in. Western colonialism was a ruse of extracting raw material from countries under the guise of providing natives progress and technology, usually by deposing whatever current civilization existed.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.3. Con: Colonialism [exploited](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exploitation_colonialism) developing countries. Today, [neocolonialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neocolonialism) continues to exploit them.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.12. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.1.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.13. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.5.
1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.14. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.
1.4.6.9.3.1.14. Pro: Governments derive moral responsibility from the mandate given to them by the people who elect them. That mandate is typically to provide for citizens of that country.
1.4.6.9.3.1.14.1. Con: Governments also derive moral responsibility from promising to uphold certain rights and principles.
1.4.6.9.3.1.14.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.
1.4.6.9.3.1.14.2. Con: The mandate of a government to care for its citizens is not incompatible with a mandate to also care for non-citizens, especially if both can be fulfilled without conflict.
1.4.6.9.4. Con: The primary conservative theory of [distributive justice](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/justice-distributive/) is libertarianism. Yet libertarianism supports the idea that states should take in refugees.
1.4.6.9.4.1. Con: Genuinely consistent libertarianism is in fact a rarity amongst conservatives. The primary conservative theory of distributive justice more accurately reflects a form of [communitarianism](http://www.philosophybasics.com/branch_communitarianism.html) which emphasises the importance of a tight-knit, self determining community. These communities would necessarily be permitted to turn refugees away if they so chose.
1.4.6.9.4.2. Con: Libertarianism opposes government redistribution of property. Taking in refugees who rely on government handouts requires such a redistribution of property.
1.4.6.9.4.3. Pro: Libertarianism supports the idea of [open borders](https://openborders.info/) and the free movement of people.
1.4.6.9.4.3.1. Con: Libertarian philosophy does not support open borders when the result is to be robbed.
1.4.6.9.4.3.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.
1.4.6.9.4.3.1.2. Con: Immigrants generally have lower rates of criminal behavior than the general public, and refugees in particular have constant visitation by government agents for years further discouraging criminal behavior. This exception does not apply to this case.
1.4.6.9.4.3.2. Con: Libertarianism opposes redistributive government programs as a violation of the non-aggression principle. Since allowing in people who then receive government aid would exacerbate this violation, libertarian support for open borders ought to be contingent on the abolition of any government aid, at least towards new arrivals.
1.4.6.9.4.3.3. Con: While libertarianism supports the free movement of people, there's nothing inherent in libertarian philosophy that would support "open borders". Libertarians [strongly support the concept of private property](https://books.google.com/books?id=N8Cu2hi3sJ8C&pg=PA66&dq=narveson+libertarian+idea+%22liberty+is+property%22&hl=en&sa=X&ei=S5gCUef6IenZigLZsoHAAQ#v=onepage&q=narveson%20libertarian%20idea%20%22liberty%20is%20property%22&f=false). People are free to move anywhere they are invited and not free to move into private property uninvited. If the national border is considered the property of the citizenry, then the citizenry can justifiably decide which outsiders are invited and which are denied.
1.4.6.9.4.3.4. Con: Libertarian principles should also respect the right to self-determination of political communities, and thus the rights of political communities to refuse refugees.
1.4.6.9.4.3.4.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.
1.4.6.9.4.3.5. Pro: Libertarians typically believe that [forcibly interfering](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Non-aggression_principle) with someone is always immoral.
1.4.6.9.4.3.5.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.
1.4.6.9.4.3.6. Con: Libertarianism supports the freedom of people to move, but not necessarily open borders. Individuals always have the freedom to determine who they will allow to join their collective.
1.4.6.9.5. Pro: [Conservative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservatism) theories of distributive justice often emphasize the importance of property rights. It could be argued that since people in high-income countries created their wealth, they have an entitlement to keep it to themselves.
1.4.6.9.5.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.3.3.
1.4.6.9.5.2. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.1.4.
1.4.6.9.5.3. Pro: Conservative theories of property rights usually care less about the history of a particular piece of property, and more about the fact that the individual who currently owns it acquired it justly.
1.4.6.10. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.4.3.
1.4.6.11. Pro: Liberalism supports the idea of [prioritarianism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prioritarianism), according to which we have a special duty to help the worst-off in society.
1.4.6.11.1. Con: Refugees are not part of any society so implying they are "in society" is false.
1.4.6.11.1.1. Con: In a world in which global issues, the world economy and technological advances link humans across the globe, it is not far-fetched to speak of a global society that encompasses both citizens of rich countries and refugees from poor ones.
1.4.6.11.2. Con: Prioritarianism could also imply a duty to spend our resources helping the worse-off in our own society, or the worse-off globally who might not be refugees. It does not necessarily imply a duty to take in refugees.
1.4.7. Pro: High income countries have neglected these responsibilities in the past through a history of neglecting vulnerable refugees. Inhabitants of those countries therefore inherit an obligation to make up for those past moral failures.
1.4.7.1. Pro: The US turned back the [St Louis](https://www.ushmm.org/wlc/en/article.php?ModuleId=10005267), a ship carrying over 900 Jewish refugees in 1939, resulting in the deaths of 254 of the refugees in the Holocaust.
1.4.7.2. Pro: Even in 1941, when reports about the Holocaust began filtering in, the US State Department [placed stricter restrictions](https://www.ushmm.org/wlc/en/article.php?ModuleId=10005139) on immigration.
1.4.7.3. Con: After the [Vietnam War](https://borgenproject.org/vietnam-war-and-vietnamese-refugees/) ended, the Carter administration decided to take in around 2 million refugees from Vietnam, Laos, and surrounding areas despite opposition from a majority of US-Americans.
1.4.7.3.1. Pro: Most of the [refugees](https://qz.com/670921/forty-one-years-ago-the-us-took-a-big-gamble-on-vietnamese-refugees/) who came to the US in the late 1970s were either married to US personnel, had worked with the administration, or had family in the US, and were in danger from being killed or imprisoned by the opposing Ho Chi Minh regime.
1.4.7.4. Con: Until early 2017, the US had accepted any Cuban refugee provided they managed to make it to land. Under the so-called "wet foot, dry foot"- policy they received [preferential treatment](http://www.miamiherald.com/news/nation-world/world/americas/cuba/article126506564.html) compared to refugees from other countries who are routinely detained as illegal aliens.
1.4.7.4.1. Con: However during this time the US also turned away some especially vulnerable Cuban refugees; the most notable being the 2000 case of [Elián González](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eli%C3%A1n_Gonz%C3%A1lez), a 7 year-old boy who was found floating at sea in an inner tube after his mother died on the journey from Cuba. Despite his arduous journey and the fact that he had maternal family in Miami who wanted to care for him, he was taken into custody by the INS and sent back to Cuba.
1.4.7.4.1.1. Con: In this particular case, the issue was that González was taken to the US by his mother without the knowledge and consent of his father. This case is therefore not a good representation of US immigration policies as it has the added layer of a complex international familial custody battle.
1.4.7.4.1.2. Pro: Although strictly speaking this was in line with the "wet foot, dry foot" policy, sending the boy back was viewed by many as highly unethical considering the extremes that he went through in order to get to the US.
1.4.7.4.1.3. Con: Individual, highly publicised cases should not be taken as evidence against a massive long-term trend of accepting Cuban refugees.
1.4.7.5. Pro: While European countries have some [history](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/0031322X.2018.1433004) of helping refugees, there's also points where European [countries](https://www.washingtonpost.com/news/worldviews/wp/2015/09/08/this-map-helps-explain-why-some-european-countries-reject-refugees-and-others-love-them/?noredirect=on&utm_term=.d3a0df2665c0) could've helped, but chose not to.
1.4.7.6. Con: Sweden has a [long history](http://www.aljazeera.com/indepth/features/2013/11/sweden-refugee-policy-sets-high-standard-2013112485613526863.html) of incredible generosity towards refugees.
1.4.7.6.1. Con: Part of the reason Sweden is so remarkable is how anomalous it is: the vast majority of European countries are far, far less generous towards refugees.
1.4.8. Con: With this line of thought, if you reside in a high income country and you, yourself, are high income, you should be obligated to house refugees in your own home without a say in it.
1.4.8.1. Con: If it were the most effective means of discharging their moral obligation to refugees, then wealthy individuals ought house them within their own homes.
1.4.8.2. Con: It is not necessary to sacrifice personal privacy. Funding refugee assistance groups and integration programmes will suffice. This is an option in wealthy countries. They’re called “wealthy” because they have the means to assist without pushing their citizens to extremes.
1.4.9. Con: Individuals have groups they value over the global community. Family, friends, countrymen, religions etc. And it is not within their interest to accept subjugation of a global community.
1.4.10. Pro: All people have a moral responsibility to share extra resources with people who are suffering.
1.4.10.1. Con: Most people wouldn't give food and a bed in their house to a person in need. This is not because they are immoral, but because they satisfy their own need to be happy and safe.
1.4.11. Con: The term "global citizen" is a self-negation. Concepts, in order to be useful and functional, must define boundaries and exclude those things that they are not. A global citizen is everything, and therefore nothing.
1.4.11.1. Con: A global citizen is not "everything" - it is specifically defined as a citizen of the earth. Citizenship is not restricted to nation-states.
1.4.11.2. Con: The term "global citizen" refers to a concept that has many boundaries, many in common with the concept of a "citizen of a nation", nation state borders are just not among them.
1.4.11.3. Con: The set of all "humans" is equivalent to the set of all "global citizens," yet the concept of a "human" is clearly not meaningless.
1.4.12. Con: We do not agree to be born as global citizens, and therefore cannot inherit responsibilities from that fact.
1.4.12.1. Con: We do not agree to be born as national citizens, but we still inherit the responsibilities of the state we are born into \(responsibility to follow laws etc..\).
1.4.12.1.1. Pro: Citizenship is alterable \(typically by exchanging it for another one\), but by definition you cannot opt out of being a global citizen \(if it is understood to mean the entire human race\).
1.4.13. Con: We are individuals and are responsible for ourselves and our children. Any responsibility beyond that can only be voluntary without coercion on an individual basis.
1.4.13.1. Con: The argument of "no collective coercion" can be reduced ad absurdum to imply that the state should do nothing, not even enforce borders \(since this is costly and not unanimously agreed\). If my state wants to spend money keeping people out they are infringing my individual liberty since I didn't choose it.
1.4.13.2. Con: Being responsible in a modern society is important in the long term, as we all benefit from a well maintained society. If too many people care only about themselves, society will degrade and all individuals will lose it's benefits.
1.4.14. Con: An approach to moral behavior based on principles and predetermined responsibilities is not the only one available; one can also determine moral duties based on consequences, for example the maximization of happiness.
1.4.15. Con: National boundaries are not merely abstract lines: they reflect the history, experience and traditions of different countries, and therefore they should be preserved
1.4.15.1. Con: Accepting refugees is not a threat to national boundaries. This is especially true if refugees are vetted and only selectively admitted, since the vetting process is a bureaucracy in service of the border's recognition and preservation.
1.4.15.2. Pro: -> See 1.4.5.
1.4.15.3. Pro: The \(imaginary\) boundaries exist because that's precisely where they are needed - the borders exist where culture is no longer the same, where it conflicts. Where one tries to consume the other.
1.4.15.4. Con: Culture of any given nation is an ever-changing thing molding and changing as time passes and people exchange with each other. Implying any cultural heritage needs special protection in order for its "pureness" to not get damaged has no value by itself other than protecting feels of people who are not adapt to change.
1.4.15.5. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.10.3.1.4.4.1.1.
1.4.16. Con: While we may have a degree of moral responsibility to one another, that responsibility is limited in degree and duration. It is not moral to bankrupt a country or region by forcing them to accept more refugees than they can handle. Nor is it reasonable to make them responsible for those refugees indefinitely - harboring refugees should be a temporary situation.
1.4.16.1. Pro: Many refugees taken in by high-income countries are unlikely to leave anytime soon or ever again.
1.4.16.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.10.3.1.2.6.3.1.5.
1.4.16.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.11.3.
1.4.16.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.11.2.
1.4.16.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.5.
1.4.16.3. Con: There is no evidence that any country is in the danger of bankruptcy because of taking in refugees.
1.4.16.3.1. Pro: Many countries are not left alone with paying for refugees. In the case of Greece, for example, the European Union has or will provide about [€650 Million](http://ec.europa.eu/echo/what-we-do/humanitarian-aid/emergency-support-within-eu_en) from 2016 to 2019.
1.4.17. Con: We as individuals, may be a part of a community, which may be a part of a larger global community; however, as individuals \(be it a person, city, or country\) we have no obligation to help anyone else, not even our next door neighbors. All acts of community and charity are voluntary, at least in a democratic society, perhaps not in a communist/socialist society.
1.4.17.1. Con: It's not true that one is never obligated to help. Even democratic societies prescribe some - severely limited - legal obligations when one must help someone in trouble \(see: [Duty to rescue](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duty_to_rescue)\).
1.4.18. Con: If an individual flees their home country and thereby abandons their responsibility to contribute to safety and peace there, it is unreasonable to expect or suggest other people be responsible for this individual's safety.
1.4.19. Pro: As the world becomes increasingly interconnected and globalised, the moral responsibilities of citizens of high-income countries towards foreigners become stronger.
1.4.19.1. Con: Increasing globalization and interdependence also means increased economic and military competitiveness among countries.
1.4.19.1.1. Con: Countries are no longer [mercantilist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mercantilism) - they do not "compete economically." Rather, they trade in mutually beneficial ways that make them more interconnected.
1.4.19.1.1.1. Con: [Competition](http://www.investopedia.com/ask/answers/042215/what-difference-between-capitalist-system-and-free-market-system.asp) between nations is still quite prevalent today.
1.4.19.1.1.1.1. Pro: China [mass-produces steel](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/business/Leading_business_story/charts-that-show-why-china-really-is-to-blame-for-the-steel-crisis-a6978871.html) in order to undercut British producers.
1.4.19.1.1.2. Con: Political and economic [competition](https://ideas.repec.org/a/bla/rdevec/v10y2006i4p666-682.html) between countries stimulates innovation and is arguably responsible for Europe's sustained growth.
1.4.19.1.2. Con: Military competitiveness between countries has decreased drastically - we are living in the [most peaceful time](http://www.slate.com/articles/news_and_politics/foreigners/2014/12/the_world_is_not_falling_apart_the_trend_lines_reveal_an_increasingly_peaceful.html) in human history.
1.4.19.1.2.1. Con: While active conflicts have reduced drastically, [military expenditure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_military_expenditures) is still at an all time high and many states possess nuclear weapons. Just because countries do not engage in war does not mean competitiveness is absent. It only means that there are enough military deterrents to stop war.
1.4.19.1.2.2. Con: The current conflict between Islamic jihadists and the Western world is in fact [a world war with multiple front lines](http://www.huffingtonpost.com/joseph-v-micallef/has-world-war-iii-already_b_8647588.html) scattered across the world’s major cities.
1.4.19.1.3. Pro: Recently, during the [Cold War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cold_War) both America and Russia sought to outdo each other leading to events like the arms-race and the space-race. This further led to countries in the Eastern and Western bloc following suit of the two superpowers.
1.4.19.2. Pro: Science is revealing that we are fundamentally the same as other humans across the globe and we interdependent with nature.  Attending to others is a way of affirming our essential relatedness.
1.4.19.3. Pro: History has shown that the scope of our empathy is steadily expending: from families to tribes, from cities to nation states - and, most likely, next to humanity as a whole, as is visible in global efforts such as the fight against climate change.
1.4.19.4. Pro: The increased interconnectedness of global life means that citizens of high-income countries are never free from blame for the plight of people in other countries.
1.4.19.4.1. Con: The increased interconnectedness of global life means that citizens of high-income countries are helping people in other countries to improve their lives by trading, charity and the sharing of knowledge.
1.4.19.4.2. Con: Those who are wealthy or better off than others are not culprits of injustices; they have not necessarily wronged the poorer. The claim of wrong-doing needs to be proven before a moral debt can be argued.
1.4.19.4.2.1. Con: Not all moral obligation is the result of redemptive reaction to one's own past crimes. Some moral obligation is about the universal imperative to improve the human condition when possible.
1.4.19.4.2.2. Con: Some Western countries have practiced brutal imperialism and colonization of cultures across the western hemisphere, Africa, and Asia for centuries. Denying responsibility doesn't absolve that.
1.4.19.4.2.3. Con: Nowadays capitalist states can only cope with competitors when they are also [imperialistic](https://www.marxists.org/archive/lenin/works/1916/imp-hsc/), and therefore exploiting not just their own working class but other countries as well.
1.4.19.4.2.3.1. Pro: Following the logic of [World-Systems Theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World-systems_theory), there is a clear and inescapable distribution of tasks among countries: high-income countries focus on production that requires capital and skills; low-income countries on production based on labor and resources. As high-income countries cannot escape this division of labor, they inevitably help to exploit poor countries.
1.4.19.4.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.
1.4.19.5. Pro: Ease of communication and access to media coverage of other countries have caused us to develop closer relationships with the citizens of foreign countries.
1.4.19.6. Pro: Our increased capacity to provide aid to foreigners in need means that our moral obligations to them [become much stronger](http://philosophyfaculty.ucsd.edu/faculty/rarneson/Singeressayspring1972.pdf). This is because our failure to help them, when it is much easier for us to do so, is negligent behaviour.
1.4.19.6.1. Pro: Since global inequality is currently [worse than at any time](https://www.theguardian.com/global-development-professionals-network/2016/apr/08/global-inequality-may-be-much-worse-than-we-think) since the 19th century, rich countries have a moral obligation to take in and help foreign citizens in need.
1.4.19.7. Con: We have, in general, few strong positive duties to help others: we are not morally obliged to do things if doing so would be very [demanding](https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/consequentialism/#ConWhoLimDemMor). Even if we are capable of helping foreigners, we have no moral obligation to do so.
1.4.19.7.1. Con: Given the great humanitarian injustices in the world, morality ought to place high demands on people in safe, rich countries.
1.4.19.7.1.1. Pro: People who had the great fortune to be born into peaceful, high-income countries are in a unique position to help others. This means that they also inherit high moral responsibilities.
1.4.19.7.1.1.1. Con: Moral responsibility is not based on the circumstances of one's birth. Individuals from high income countries should not have more responsibility for the rest of humanity than those from low income ones, simply because they are able to do more.
1.4.19.7.1.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.3.4.
1.4.19.7.1.1.2. Pro: People whose situation is uncertain and dangerous will prioritize their survival over considerations of moral responsibilities, which is understandable and acceptable. Those who are safe and financially secure have less justification for dismissing moral responsibilities.
1.4.19.7.1.2. Con: You'd have to define rich and safe.  Is the qualifier for rich simply "more money than the refugee's country?"  Is the qualifier for safe simply "Safer than the refugee's country?" If so, it could be possible that there are numerous neighboring countries that they can move to rather than seeking passage specifically to European countries.
1.4.19.7.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.19.6.1.
1.4.19.7.2. Pro: Under the [Pragmatic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pragmatism) principles upon which most of the world operates, refugees offer inadequate physical utility to justify them being taken in. Therefore countries are not duty-bound.
1.4.19.7.3. Con: Morality is far more demanding if it demands of refugees that they suffer in silence, without an ability to make a moral claim to enter high-income countries.
1.4.19.7.3.1. Con: Refugees may protest against policies keeping them out and not suffer in silence. They may make a claim to enter high income countries, but these countries are not morally obliged to accommodate that claim.
1.4.20. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.15.2.1.
1.5. Pro: High-income countries are complicit in exploiting - and hence profiting from - many exchanges that have contributed to the current world-wide refugee crisis.
1.5.1. Pro: Whenever refugees are the result of conflict where high income countries are involved, the moral obligation arises to support the lives of those affected.
1.5.1.1. Con: Low-income countries, too, contribute to conflicts that result in refugee flows and therefore have an obligation to take these refugees in.
1.5.1.1.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.2.1.
1.5.1.1.2. Con: The above arguments do not claim that high-income nations have an exclusive moral obligation. Poor nations' obligations do not eliminate rich nations' obligations. They are not mutually exclusive.
1.5.1.1.3. Pro: The [Second Congo War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_Congo_War), the deadliest conflict since World War I, was sparked by regional events such as the [Rwandan genocide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rwandan_genocide) and the [First Congo War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_Congo_War). Even today, [more than 600.000](http://reporting.unhcr.org/sites/default/files/UNHCR%20DRC%20Factsheet%20-%20July%202017.pdf) refugees and asylum seekers from the Democratic Republic of Congo are in other African countries.
1.5.1.1.3.1. Con: High income countries themselves contributed to these regional conflicts. This means that they still have an obligation to these refugees, even if low income countries also contributed to their creation.
1.5.1.1.3.1.1. Pro: The enmity between Hutus and Tutsis that eventually lead to the Rwandan genocide was initially exacerbated by [Belgian colonists](https://umuvugizi.wordpress.com/2011/08/16/how-colonialism-affected-the-rwandan-genocide/).
1.5.1.1.3.1.2. Pro: A lot of the instability in the Democratic Republic of Congo that contributed to the two Congo wars dates back to the removal and assassination of Patrice Lumumba. There is [substantial evidence](http://www.bbc.com/news/world-africa-22006446) pointing to Western involvement in this event.
1.5.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.6.4.4.
1.5.2. Con: Every country has duties towards its citizens, namely to enhance their lives and do their will - even at a cost to others.
1.5.2.1. Pro: Perfectly legitimate deals between some low- and high-income countries aiming at the latter's access to raw resources can turn out to harm the former through the [resource curse](http://www.eoi.es/blogs/alvarohernandez/2014/01/10/an-overview-of-the-resource-curse-in-africa-a-hinder-for-development/). The main enabling factor of this channel is the suffering country's corruption or lack of accountability to its citizens.
1.5.2.2. Pro: An example of countries focusing on own their citizens and how that could come at a cost for others are investments from high-income countries initially aiming to secure domestic food and energy production. These can transform into [land-grab rushes](https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/09512748.2013.842314) if the less developed nations targeted have not established legal recognition of rights to land or exhibits significant corruption[\(Typical in Sub-Saharan Africa, but also notable in SE Asia and South America\).](https://landmatrix.org/en/get-the-idea/global-map-investments/)
1.5.3. Con: -> See 1.4.19.4.2.
1.5.4. Pro: Wealthy countries created the refugees in the first place by reshaping the borders of the Middle East after the World War 1 in a way that leads to constant sectarian conflict within the countries' borders.
1.5.4.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.5.1.1.
1.5.4.2. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.15.2.1.3.3.4.4.
1.5.4.3. Pro: Many conflicts that are based on the drawing of borders from decades ago have become intractable precisely because of these borders and because the powers that drew these borders have no interest in resolving these conflicts.
1.5.4.3.1. Pro: The Korean Peninsula was [split into two countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Division_of_Korea) by the United States and the Soviet Union in 1945. The prevailing conflict between North and South Korea and its intractability are a direct influence of the influence of great powers and its continuance \(with China playing an important role today\).
1.5.4.3.2. Pro: Pakistan was split off from India in 1947 under British rule through the [Radcliffe Line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radcliffe_Line). The line is the origin of a number of prevailing disputes and conflicts, for example in Punjab, Bengal and even Kashmir.
1.5.4.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.15.2.1.3.3.4.
1.5.4.5. Con: The origin of those nations cannot permanently be a source of justifications. They are their own independent nation-states now, and have been for a long time already.
1.5.5. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.
1.5.6. Pro: Even if not directly involved, high income countries \(i.e, most of the EU bloc\) have not done enough to stop the other powers from creating devastation that leads to the refugee crisis. High income countries sucker up to the super powers, thus creating automatic moral responsibility.
1.5.6.1. Con: -> See 1.3.6.4.4.5.2.
1.5.7. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.3.6.
1.5.8. Con: The status of high-income countries is the result not only of their relations with poorer countries, but also of their internal setup and culture.
1.5.8.1. Con: As the world's only functioning city state, Singapore is hardly representative in its development and success.
1.5.8.2. Con: High-income countries are in their position as they occupy a role in the global division of labor that allows them to exploit poorer countries, utilize their labor and reek the profits, thereby reproducing this [world system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World-systems_theory).
1.5.8.3. Con: High-income countries of today are in their position because they were able to utilize their power position of the past to conquer and exploit weaker countries.
1.5.8.3.1. Con: There is some legitimacy in this in some cases, but this cannot be applied as a "general rule". You'd have to go back to the Vikings to explain Norway. A much wider set of complex factors are in play, including rule of law, stability of bordering countries, cultural changes, etc etc.
1.5.8.3.2. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.6.2.5.
1.5.8.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.12.1.
1.5.8.5. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.12.2.
1.5.8.6. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.12.3.
1.5.9. Con: In most cases, the involvement of high-income countries elsewhere in the world creates win-win situations, for example through foreign direct investment.
1.5.10. Con: Even if a significant amount of the gathered wealth is due to exploitation of poorer countries, this wealth has mostly accumulated among the most wealthy members of high-income countries. Contrary to that, most of the burden will end up on the shoulders of the poor in the country.
1.5.10.1. Con: This might be true, but most wealthy countries are democracies, so laws can or should be passed to change the unfair distribution \(0.5 % vs 99.5 %\). Most refugees run away from dictators, they cannot change the laws in their countries without risking their lives.
1.5.10.2. Pro: In Germany, the [45 richest households](http://www.spiegel.de/international/business/inquality-and-wealth-distribution-in-germany-a-1190050.html) own as much wealth as the bottom-half of the population.
1.5.10.3. Pro: In the United States, the [top 0.1%](https://www.theguardian.com/business/2014/nov/13/us-wealth-inequality-top-01-worth-as-much-as-the-bottom-90) of the population own as much as the bottom-90%.
1.5.10.4. Pro: The higher competition to affordable housing or low qualification work is an example of how this takes place.
1.5.10.5. Con: Failure to create equitable economies in high wealth countries doesn't excuse them from their obligations to their victims.
1.5.10.5.1. Pro: The refugees aren't responsible for capitalism, they are also a victim of it. It's the responsibility of the people in capitalist countries to defend against exploitation of the working class. The borders aren't between countries, they are between the exploiters and the exploited.
1.5.11. Con: On the whole, high-income countries do more to help low-income countries than to exploit them. The USA government alone spends [$27.7 Billion on foreign aid](https://www.foreignassistance.gov/), and that does not including private charities and individual citizen donations.
1.5.11.1. Con: This is an unfair comparison. High-income countries started exploitation during the [colonialism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade) time and even before. Furthermore, even just by considering the current exploitation the comparison does not hold up. For instance, considering a single theft on the many that happen every day, just in [Africa](https://www.aljazeera.com/indepth/opinion/2017/05/africa-poor-stealing-wealth-170524063731884.html) multinationals dodge $68bn in taxes.
1.5.11.1.1. Con: Multinationals are not “wealthy nations”.
1.5.11.2. Con: No amount of money could make up for the way [colonialism](https://voxeu.org/article/economic-impact-colonialism) stopped development and created societies ripe with corruption.
1.5.11.2.1. Pro: [Colonialism](https://www.pambazuka.org/global-south/effects-colonialism-africas-past-and-present) nipped the bud of prosperity and self-sustainability of Low-income countries and sowed seeds of corruption and disunity in Low-income countries. [High-income countries](https://www.researchgate.net/profile/Will_Davis-Trepanier/post/how_has_western_politics_has_had_a_negative_impact_on_West_Africas_political_and_economic_stability/attachment/59d63ec979197b807799b597/AS:425336279179269\@1478419479829/download/Alexandra_Hrituleac_thesis_1_DEBashinjaga+amahanga+ko+++ntacyo+yabamariye+ahubwo+ngo+akomeza+kubakangisha+imfashanyo.pdf) also imposed current [economic](http://trace.tennessee.edu/cgi/viewcontent.cgi?article=1182&context=utk_chanhonoproj), diplomatic and political standards which are generally designed to favour fellow High-income countries.
1.5.11.2.2. Con: -> See 1.3.8.1.1.
1.5.12. Con: One cannot easily generalize about high-income countries as not all of them share equal blame for the current state of the world. Some might not deserve any blame at all.
1.5.12.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.8.
1.5.13. Con: Not all citizens of high-income countries agree with the actions of their governments. It is unfair to require these citizens to pay for those actions.
1.5.13.1. Con: The majority of [high-income countries are democratic](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2014/05/democracy-boost-economic-growth/), which means they are ruled on a majority vote basis, which does not cater to "all citizens".
1.5.13.2. Pro: Citizens do not take part in the decision-making regarding refugee intake and the majority opinion is then ignored, making the decision undemocratic. Example: Germany- [before](https://www.washingtontimes.com/news/2015/sep/10/angela-merkel-welcomes-refugees-to-germany-despite/) and [after](https://qz.com/1319399/angela-merkel-agrees-to-abandon-germanys-open-door-refugee-policy-to-save-her-government/) input from the voters with a significantly changed [popularity](https://www.thelocal.de/20180219/poll-afd-now-the-second-most-popular-party) climate. Across Europe, change only ever came once political careers were threatened, which is when damage is already done.
1.5.13.2.1. Pro: In countries like [Poland](https://www.politico.eu/article/politics-nationalism-and-religion-explain-why-poland-doesnt-want-refugees/) and [Hungary](https://www.dw.com/en/hungarys-orban-tells-germany-you-wanted-the-migrants-we-didnt/a-42065012), the elected politicians understand their nations' integration capacity and possible threats and reacted adequately per their perception. They simply had better suited elected politicians to handle the challenge their people seem to perceive, as evident by the apparent support of the public.
1.5.14. Pro: The oil industry has promoted conflict in the Middle East, exploited it and extracted wealth from these countries, for example in the context of America's wars in Iraq and Afghanistan.
1.5.14.1. Con: Companies like ExxonMobil are [primarily interested in stability](https://www.npr.org/templates/transcript/transcript.php?storyId=151842205) in order to not undermine their operations; they have therefore little interest in promoting conflict.
1.5.14.2. Con: Even if companies had an interest in the wars in the Middle East, it is unlikely that high-income countries profit from that. From 9/11 on, the oil price has been [steadily rising](http://www.macrotrends.net/1369/crude-oil-price-history-chart) for over a decade and never again reached the levels of that time.
1.5.14.3. Con: The oil industry may help extract wealth from the Middle East but claiming that they have promoted conflict there is a baseless accusation contrary to their best business interests.
1.5.15. Pro: If a country profits from war, for example by selling arms or other tools of war, it has a moral obligation to take in refugees.
1.5.15.1. Pro: The [US](http://www.cnbc.com/2017/05/20/us-saudi-arabia-seal-weapons-deal-worth-nearly-110-billion-as-trump-begins-visit.html) and Canada are [major exporters](https://www.theglobeandmail.com/news/politics/canada-now-the-second-biggest-arms-exporter-to-middle-east-data-show/article30459788/) of arms to the Middle East.
1.5.15.2. Pro: -> See 1.3.13.2.3.1.
1.5.15.3. Pro: The current invasion of until then mostly peacful Afrin in Syria by Turkey is conducted with [a lot of weapons from Germany and the US.](https://www1.wdr.de/daserste/monitor/videos/video-operation-olivenzweig-krieg-gegen-die-kurden--made-in-germany-100.html) It seems natural refugees would go to the countries enabling the aggressor.
1.5.15.4. Con: Countries do not sell arms; companies do.
1.5.15.4.1. Pro: Numerous of the world's largest defense contractors are privately owned, for example [Lockheed Martin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lockheed_Martin) and [Northrop Grumman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Northrop_Grumman) in the US, [Rolls-Royce](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rolls-Royce_Holdings) in the UK, [Mitsubishi Heavy Industries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitsubishi_Heavy_Industries) in Japan and [Heckler & Koch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heckler_%26_Koch) in Germany.
1.5.15.4.1.1. Pro: [Seven](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arms_industry#List_of_major_weapon_manufacturers) of the world's ten largest arms producers are private companies without substantial ownership and influence of governments.
1.5.15.4.2. Con: Governments hold partial ownership and exert influence in various large defense contractors, for example the [Thales Group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thales_Group) from France, [Leonardo S.p.A.](http://Leonardo S.p.A.) from Italy and [Airbus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Airbus) with several government minority stakeholders.
1.5.15.4.3. Pro: Therefore, the people who actually make money by selling weapons to war zones should be responsible for supporting refugees.
1.5.15.4.4. Con: Governments do in fact engage in foreign military sales with other nations as a means of strengthening alliances and improving national security.
1.5.15.4.4.1. Pro: According to the [Arms Control Export Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arms_Export_Control_Act), the U.S. can sell defense articles and services to foreign countries and international organizations when the President formally finds that doing so will strengthen U.S. security and promote world peace.
1.5.15.4.4.2. Pro: The UK government supplied [export licenses](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/feb/13/eu-criticises-british-arms-sales-saudi-arabia) to Saudi Arabia for close to £3bn worth of arms from 2015 to 2016.
1.5.15.4.4.2.1. Pro: The fact that these weapons have been used in the Saudi military campaign on Yemen, which a [UN investigation](https://www.theguardian.com/world/2016/jan/27/un-report-into-saudi-led-strikes-in-yemen-raises-questions-over-uk-role) discovered has violated international humanitarian law, only lends credence to the assertion that nations who supply arms should bear a moral responsibility to accept the people whom those arms displace.
1.5.15.4.4.2.1.1. Con: -> See 1.3.13.2.3.1.1.
1.5.15.4.5. Con: The export of military technology, such as [encryption](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Export_of_cryptography_from_the_United_States#U.S._export_rules) for example, is strictly regulated. A private company cannot freely sell their products without government approval.
1.5.15.5. Con: Not all high-income countries do export arms.
1.5.15.5.1. Pro: Five countries - the US, Russia, China, Germany and France - account for 75% of all arms sales worldwide. And two of them don't qualify as high-income countries in the first place \([SIPRI, p. 15](https://sipri.org/sites/default/files/2017-09/yb17-summary-eng.pdf)\).
1.5.15.5.1.1. Pro: China and Russia are [not considered high-income](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/World_Bank_high-income_economy), by the World Bank.
1.5.15.6. Con: Countries' right to self-determination entitles them to decide what moral obligations they wish to take on. By profiting off of war, they show that the economic well-being of their country is more important than the lives of foreign people.
1.5.15.7. Con: Wars would happen with or without high-income countries' provision of arms. The fact that high-income countries sell the arms does not impose any moral responsibility on them.
1.5.15.7.1. Con: Wars would not be as destructive if small rebel groups lacked access to a reliable source of weaponry.
1.5.15.7.2. Pro: The civil wars of recent years that have sparked large-scale refugee movements, for example from Syria, Afghanistan, Iraq, Somalia and Sudan, are fought regardless of weapons sales from high-income countries \(even if they involve these kinds of weapons\). Most of these conflicts were started by rebel forces that do not acquire weapons this way.
1.5.15.7.3. Pro: -> See 1.3.13.2.3.1.1.
1.5.15.7.4. Pro: If high-income countries did not sell arms, the buyers whom they sell to would simply purchase arms from other sources.
1.5.15.7.4.1. Con: If high-income countries did not sell arms, the buyers whom they sell to would likely not have as much access to as high quantity and quality of weapons, significantly limiting their ability to conduct warfare.
1.5.15.7.4.2. Pro: Buyers may even develop domestic military production.
1.5.15.7.4.2.1. Pro: Without the West having control over the supply of their arms, countries may feel emboldened to engage in military adventurism, since their supplies are not dependent on international approval.
1.5.15.7.4.3. Pro: There are [plenty of other sources of weaponry outside high-income countries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arms_industry#World's_largest_arms_exporters). Russia remains a significant producer and China has at least a decade of experience under its belt of creating export-quality arms.
1.5.15.7.4.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.5.15.7.4.2.1.
1.5.15.8. Con: Countries typically continue to sell weapons to immoral actors, like Saudi Arabia, not for financial reasons but geopolitical ones. On net, they may be doing the right thing by selling those arms, and are therefore not blameworthy for doing it.
1.5.15.8.1. Con: The underlying force is the same: weapons are sold out of self-interest, whether it may be financial or geopolitical. This self interest comes at the cost of other interests, for example in peace and stability.
1.5.15.8.1.1. Con: Concerns about geopolitics may be benevolent in nature. For example, the US may believe that a continued alliance with Saudi Arabia is in the interests of the wider Middle East.
1.5.15.8.2. Con: [There is evidence](https://www.vox.com/2017/5/20/15626638/trump-saudi-arabia-arms-deal) that the US continues to sell arms to Saudi Arabia for purely financial reasons.
1.5.16. Pro: The economic activities of high income countries have contributed massively to climate change, which triggers conflict and resulting refugee crises.
1.5.16.1. Con: Those same economic activities have also helped [2 billion people](http://www.un.org/sustainabledevelopment/blog/2015/12/2-billion-move-out-of-extreme-poverty-over-25-years-says-un-report/) escape extreme poverty. The negative effects of a net positive action are not obviously a basis for guilt and redemptive action.
1.5.16.2. Pro: High-income nations owe their wealth in part to their higher per capita carbon footprints, and historically share the most responsibility for climate change.
1.5.16.3. Pro: [Research](https://www.nytimes.com/2015/03/03/science/earth/study-links-syria-conflict-to-drought-caused-by-climate-change.html) shows low-income nations are more susceptible to climate variability impacts such as the extreme Syrian drought between 2006-09, which created the economic and social conditions that triggered the civil war there. Heavy emitters must help climate refugees re-settle into more viable and stable areas within their borders.
1.5.16.4. Pro: Climate change will likely render [large swathes of the world](https://bigthink.com/strange-maps/what-the-world-will-look-like-4degc-warmer) uninhabitable. The people who live in those places need to go somewhere.
1.5.16.5. Pro: Climate change contributes to environmental problems like droughts, which in turn [cause conflict](https://www.scientificamerican.com/article/ominous-story-of-syria-climate-refugees/).
1.5.16.6. Pro: Senior US military officials [claim](https://www.theguardian.com/environment/2016/dec/01/climate-change-trigger-unimaginable-refugee-crisis-senior-military) direct links between climate change and the Arab Spring, the war in Syria, and Boko Haram, all of which have been massive contributors to the current refugee crisis.
1.6. Con: Countries do not have any obligations to people who are not their citizens or residents.
1.6.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.
1.6.2. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.
1.6.3. Con: -> See 1.4.
1.6.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.
1.6.5. Con: While this may have been true in the past, globalization has meant that certain nations disproportionately contribute to global problems \(eg climate change\). Those nations inherit global responsibilities as a result of that harm.
1.6.6. Pro: No country is obligated to do anything that could be detrimental to its own people, as the country only exists at the will of its people, for its people. One can argue specific instances, whether or not the country should take in refugees, but the idea that they have a moral obligation simply because they are rich and because refugees exist is a fallacy.
1.6.6.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.13.3.11.3.
1.6.6.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.
1.6.7. Con: High-income countries have signed international agreements to take in refugees.
1.6.7.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.
1.6.7.2. Pro: All high-income countries are signatories to the [1951 Refugee Convention](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convention_Relating_to_the_Status_of_Refugees). Thus, they have an obligation to take in refugees - moral or not.
1.6.7.2.1. Con: The 1951 Refugee Convention has, for all practical purposes, only symbolic value and little legal relevance as there is no monitoring body, no mechanism for complaints and no legal punishment for violating the convention.
1.6.7.2.1.1. Con: Morally, the absence of monitoring and enforcement mechanisms does not make a treaty any less binding.
1.6.7.2.1.1.1. Con: Treaties are only as binding as the holders are inclined to uphold them. Lack of monitoring and enforcement is evidence of disinclination.
1.6.7.2.1.2. Con: There is a monitoring body: the UN \(who created the convention itself\) has the [UNHRC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Human_Rights_Council) \(Human Rights Council\).
They monitor all aspects of human rights \(including refugees rights\), and specifically use the [Universal Periodic Review](https://www.ohchr.org/en/hrbodies/upr/pages/uprmain.aspx) \(UPR\) to monitor and report on how well each state is doing.
1.6.7.2.1.3. Con: The UN, creators of the 1951 Refugee Convention, have a [complaints procedure](https://www.ohchr.org/EN/HRBodies/HRC/ComplaintProcedure/Pages/HRCComplaintProcedureIndex.aspx) through the [Human Rights Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Nations_Human_Rights_Council).
1.6.7.2.1.4. Pro: The strongest countries \(eg the [P5](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/P5%2B1)\) wield enormous political and economic power. They have their own clouts and it is [difficult to make them toe the line](https://www.quora.com/What-happens-if-a-country-does-not-follow-the-terms-of-a-UN-treaty-it-has-signed/answer/Aditya-Krishna-22).
It often becomes impossible to make a superpower responsible for a treaty it has ratified if it [chooses not to honour it](https://www.kialo.com/the-un-is-a-good-thing-15052/15052.0=15052.1+15052.9+15052.228-15052.229-15052.230-15052.232+15052.233).
1.6.7.2.2. Con: The [refugee convention  of 1951](http://www.unhcr.org/1951-refugee-convention.html) does not oblige states to take in refugees. It allows states to return asylum seekers to [the first safe country](https://www.theguardian.com/commentisfree/libertycentral/2010/sep/21/claim-asylum-uk-legal-position).
1.6.7.2.3. Pro: This is a [legally binding](http://www.unhcr.org/uk/1951-refugee-convention.html) Convention for the 145 countries which ratified it.
1.6.7.2.4. Con: A refugee loses his status as such if any other reasons for specific choice of target nation are present other than safety. Europe in regards to Middle-Eastern and African people is an economic target. Safety for them is more easily achievable elsewhere.
1.6.7.3. Pro: The are only few circumstances under which a refugee can be turned away, for example if they are dangerous or attempting to enter from a safe country.
1.6.7.3.1. Pro: The [Dublin Regulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dublin_Regulation) only require EU-countries to take in refugees if they are the first country into which the refugee first applies to after leaving their own country.
1.6.7.3.1.1. Con: This only applies to EU-Countries, not high-income countries in general.
1.6.7.3.1.2. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.2.3.6.3.
1.6.7.3.2. Pro: Article 32 of the [Refugee Convention](http://www.unhcr.org/3b66c2aa10) lists "grounds of national security or public order" as the only reasons for which refugees with a legitimate claim to asylum can be expelled.
1.6.7.4. Pro: Countries should not break laws they've promised to uphold as doing so lowers the significance of laws. Countries should therefore remake laws which they're unable to uphold into laws which they can abide by.
1.6.7.5. Con: Agreements should be broken when useful.
1.6.7.5.1. Con: Trust that any agreement would be upheld would be non-existent if everyone decides to break agreements whenever it suits them.
1.6.7.5.2. Con: If a state no longer thinks it should abide by the terms of a treaty, they should leave the treaty instead of breaking it.
1.6.7.5.2.1. Pro: This is similar to the concept of Marriage, divorce and Cheating.
A person has the right to sleep with someone else, but because they are married they have to get a divorce before they can exercise that right.
1.6.7.6. Con: It is a country's right as a sovereign nation to decide who it accepts and turns away.
1.6.7.6.1. Pro: [The Westphalian model](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Westphalian_sovereignty) established boundaries and the concept of sovereignty in order to ensure more peaceful relations between countries. Without countries right to sovereign rule being respected, peace between nations wouldn't be sustainable.
1.6.7.6.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.15.
1.6.7.6.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.8.2.
1.6.7.6.3. Con: International legislation obliges countries to always protect certain groups of vulnerable people, such as recognized refugees. Sovereignty can't be called in here to escape this obligation.
1.6.7.6.4. Con: It was also the sovereign nations right to sign up to these treaties, and all nations have the [capacity to conclude treaties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vienna_Convention_on_the_Law_of_Treaties#Content_and_effects) if they no longer believe in them.
Until they decide to leave the treaty, they no longer have the right to act against it.
1.6.7.6.4.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.7.5.2.1.
1.6.7.6.5. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.3.1.6.4.3.
1.6.7.7. Con: -> See 1.6.7.3.1.
1.6.8. Con: When there is public willingness in high-income countries to support disadvantaged people abroad, it produces an obligation for the country to act accordingly.
1.6.8.1. Con: There is a big difference between a 'general public support for improved welfare of populations abroad' and a 'democratic majority supporting a particular policy'. Even where the majority support the same kinds of rights, or actions, people can disagree strongly about the level of spending/ type of policy action that is appropriate for their country to take.
1.6.8.2. Pro: There is generally a willingness to support disadvantaged people overseas.
1.6.8.2.1. Pro: All European countries have a large majority of the population supporting at least some foreign aid spending. The UK is the least supportive, with[17% wanting no overseas aid at all](https://yougov.co.uk/news/2013/11/09/British-amongst-least-generous-overseas-aid/).
1.6.8.2.2. Con: All high income countries who are meeting their foreign aid promises \(Sweden, Norway, Denmark, UK, Finland, France\) have a majority of the public [who think](https://yougov.co.uk/news/2013/11/09/British-amongst-least-generous-overseas-aid/) aid should be decreased, according to a 2013 yougov survey.
1.6.8.2.3.
