Pro: Nearly all high-income countries have accepted the [United Nations target](https://www.oecd.org/dac/stats/the07odagnitarget-ahistory.htm) of spending 0.7%  of their GDP on foreign aid. [Most of them](https://www.theguardian.com/global-development/2017/jan/04/uk-among-six-countries-hit-un-aid-spending-target-oecd) don't yet reach this target, which implies that they see a moral obligation to spend more than they currently do.
1.6.8.2.3.1. Con: At the same time: seven in ten Britons \(excluding those who are undecided\) want to [scrap](http://www.telegraph.co.uk/news/2016/08/22/twice-as-many-british-voters-want-to-scrap-07-gdp-aid-pledge-tha/) Britain’s commitment to spend 0.7 per cent of its GDP on foreign aid.
1.6.8.2.4. Pro: [Many people](http://foreignpolicy.com/2014/01/21/the-case-for-aid/) do feel an obligation to have major foreign aid programs.
1.6.8.3. Con: Citizens being sympathetic to any particular social group does not imply an obligation on their respective governments on immigration issues, unless written into law and voted by citizens.
1.6.9. Con: Countries have ethical obligations to non-citizens. Almost every system of [ethics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Normative_ethics) \(utilitarian, deontological, etc\) values human life equally regardless of nationality.
1.6.10. Con: If a country adopted a competitive attitude of "our country first," it wouldn't guarantee a successful future for its citizens. In the future, the same country might be in chaos or a weaker position. In that case, openness would be more helpful than isolation.
1.6.10.1. Pro: It is in the long-term interests of humanity for high-income countries to host refugees.
1.6.10.1.1. Pro: It is a global good for victims of any periodic, localized crises to have a general expectation of available safe refuge. Thus, every nation, high-income or low-income, has a moral obligation to aid any genuine refugee.
1.6.10.1.1.1. Pro: High-income nations are also subject to periodic crises, and their citizens, if forced to find refuge, would reasonably expect reciprocal treatment.
1.6.10.1.1.2. Con: It is unrealistic to expect that taking in refugees would set a precedent for the future on the basis of which other countries would adopt a similar open door-policy in the future.
1.6.10.1.1.2.1. Con: Most of our extant fairness norms are explained by expectations of future reciprocity in kind. This is described in "soft" moral terms by the [Kant's "Categorical Imperative"](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Categorical_imperative), but modeled in harder economic terms by the optimal "tit-for-tat" strategy in the indefinitely repeated version of [the prisoner's dilemma](https://blogs.cornell.edu/info2040/2011/11/26/cooperation-in-prisoners-dilemma-situations/). An expectation of "open door" policy is based less on hope than on being mutually advantageous.
1.6.10.1.1.2.1.1. Pro: The concept of "war crimes" supports the notion that diametrically-opposed interests may choose to agree on common standards of conduct that benefit both parties.
1.6.10.1.1.2.1.2. Pro: The success of "[mutually assured destruction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutual_assured_destruction)" and the red line in preventing a nuclear war during the Cold War is an example of this equilibrium.
1.6.10.1.1.2.1.3. Pro: The [presumption of innocence](https://www.law.cornell.edu/wex/presumption_of_innocence) is a similar fairness norm that is foundational to modern judicial proceedings. We choose to reduce our chances of punishing criminal behavior in order to minimize our chances of being wrongfully or frivolously imprisoned.
1.6.10.1.1.3. Con: Most skilled/educated people have the option of migrating to other countries without depending of asylum. Most unskilled people would integrate better \(and therefor be happier\) taking up asylum in countries closer to their own. The moral obligation is thus only on the first countries that the refugee enters as they are likely to have the most similar culture.
1.6.10.1.1.3.1. Con: A strict global adherence to the proposed moral obligation to grant asylum to all genuine refugees would also result in most refugees taking asylum in the nearest safe and stable country. Refugees definitionally don't have the luxury to choose where to live. If they did, they would not be seeking refuge in the first place.
1.6.10.1.2. Con: It is not in humanity's long-term interest to migrate refugees into high-income countries. Refugee relocation is equivalent to a hand out and is not sustainable. Refugees should work to resolve their country's issues with the possible assistance from other countries.
1.6.10.1.2.1. Pro: Taking in refugees collides with the moral obligation by refugees to stay in their country and fight for a better life of their societies.
1.6.10.1.2.1.1. Con: There is nothing wrong with abandoning one's home country and starting anew somewhere else, especially if home has little or nothing to offer for the future.
1.6.10.1.2.1.1.1. Con: Abandoning one's home country involves abandoning innocent women and children and is equivalent to committing an act of violence against them.
1.6.10.1.2.1.1.1.1. Con: The data does not back up the idea that women and children are left behind and abandoned in their home countries.
1.6.10.1.2.1.1.1.1.1. Pro: In the case of Germany alone, [about 300.000 people](http://www.spiegel.de/international/germany/hardened-german-rules-leave-refugee-families-split-a-1165162.html), most of them women and children, many from Syria, are currently waiting for permission to travel to Germany in order to join family members there who have already arrived.
1.6.10.1.2.1.1.1.1.2. Pro: Most refugees are women and children; they can hardly be said to have been abandoned as they, too, are on the move.
1.6.10.1.2.1.1.1.1.2.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.1.2.1.
1.6.10.1.2.1.1.1.1.3. Con: As of 2015, of the asylum applicants coming to the EU-28 area from war-torn countries, [most were adult men](http://www.pewglobal.org/2016/08/02/4-asylum-seeker-demography-young-and-male/): 63% of those from Nigeria, 60% of those from Iraq, 53% of those from Syria, 43% of those from Afghanistan.
1.6.10.1.2.1.1.1.1.3.1. Con: These numbers may be misleading: whereas young men are often capable of reaching Europe or sent ahead by their families, female refugees and children often stay back in countries closer to their place of origin.
1.6.10.1.2.1.1.1.1.3.1.1. Pro: In the case of Germany, [about 300.000](http://www.spiegel.de/international/germany/hardened-german-rules-leave-refugee-families-split-a-1165162.html) family members of refugees who were accepted in Germany are currently waiting to follow their relatives, most of them women and children.
1.6.10.1.2.1.1.1.1.4. Pro: According to a [2016 survey](http://www.unhcr.org/news/briefing/2016/2/56cc4b876/unhcr-survey-finds-afghan-syrian-refugees-arriving-greece-fleeing-conflict.html) by the UNHRC in Greece, almost nine out of ten Syrian refugees interviewed there were travelling with family members.
1.6.10.1.2.1.1.1.2. Con: According to a 2015 survey in Afghanistan, among the factors that correlates with the willingness to leave the country is being single, whereas being married correlates with not wanting to leave. The prospect of abandoning loved ones is therefore likely to stifle interest in migration \([Asia Foundation, p. 90](http://asiafoundation.org/resources/pdfs/Afghanistanin2015.pdf)\).
1.6.10.1.2.1.2. Con: There is no absolute moral obligation to fight for one's country.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.1. Pro: This is especially the case if one cannot associate with any side of the conflict. This is probably the case for many in countries like Syria, Afghanistan and Somalia where the choice is between an unaccountable government and radicals.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.2. Pro: The arbitrary act of being born in a certain country should not be considered a justification for forcing people to fight and die in a war, especially if they feel no close association with this country or the purpose of the war.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.3. Pro: Many refugees flee their country precisely to escape the government. Arguing that they should fight in its service therefore makes little sense.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.3.1. Pro: According to a [2015 survey](https://www.adoptrevolution.org/en/pr-survey/) among Syrian refugees in Germany, 70% held the government responsible for the fighting at home and a majority did not want to return home as long as the current government is in power.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.4. Con: If one does not have moral obligation to ensure safety and well being of one's home country, then country has no moral obligation to ensure safety of its citizens, especially non citizens.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5. Con: Citizens have a moral duty to serve their society in return for the various rights and privileges that they are granted by society.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.1. Pro: States ensure that citizens are guaranteed protection under the law and by the police.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.1.1. Con: Citizens cannot meaningfully consent to police protection as it is an artificially created government institution to which there are no feasible alternatives.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.1.1.1. Con: Citizens cannot meaningfully consent to any imposed governmental structure but this does not imply that they are inherently wrong.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.1.2. Pro: Without the state to enforce the law, society would likely descend into anarchy.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.1.2.1. Con: Private police companies could replace state employed police.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.1.2.1.1. Con: Private police forces would be unreliable as they are primarily concerned with profit incentive unlike state police forces whose primary concerns are protecting society.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.1.2.1.2. Con: Private police forces could not be employed by economically disadvantaged communities.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.1.2.1.3. Con: It is harder to hold private police accountable for their actions as no one but the private organization themselves can hold the private police accountable. The laws that exist in a state with a government acts as a way of holding any criminal accountable independently of the police institution.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.1.2.2. Pro: There is no reason for an individual to obey the law solely because it is the law. The only reason people obey is out of fear of punishment. Without state enforcement no one would obey laws.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.1.2.2.1. Con: Individuals create norms of appropriate behavior in order to be able to coexist peacefully in society. It is likely that most people would still abide by these normative codes of ethics without a state as the desire to be able to live together in harmony would still exist.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.1.3. Con: The function of police is to enforce laws that have already been broken, or are in the process of being broken.The [US Supreme Court](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warren_v._District_of_Columbia) has ruled [many](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Town_of_Castle_Rock_v._Gonzales) times that the police are under no obligation to provide "protection".
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.2. Pro: States provide their citizens with the right to due process.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.3. Pro: States provide their citizens with access to many things such as infrastructure and education.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.3.1. Con: The state uses the citizens' own money, collected in the form of taxes, to provide these communal benefits.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.3.1.1. Con: The state is still responsible for collecting, aggregating and spending money on communal goods which individuals could not afford for themselves.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.3.2. Pro: Infrastructure and education are public goods, so even though they are in demand, the free market does not supply them because they are not profitable. The state is therefore the sole provider of these goods.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.4. Pro: States protect their citizens from alien threats.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.4.1. Pro: Given that the military is necessary to provide this ability, it is fair that the state should require their citizens to maintain it.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.4.1.1. Pro: Since it is almost impossible to tell when an attack will occur, the military has to be maintained in equal strength at all times.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.4.1.1.1. Con: Contemporary geopolitical relations gives a fair idea of how likely an attack from a foreign country is. If there's no reason to expect any attack within the foreseeable future, maintaining the military at full strength at all times is unnecessary.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.4.1.1.1.1. Pro: [Conflict trends](http://file.prio.no/publication_files/prio/Gates,%20Nyg%C3%A5rd,%20Strand,%20Urdal%20-%20Trends%20in%20Armed%20Conflict,%20Conflict%20Trends%201-2016.pdf) suggest that both the frequency of wars, and the amount of battle-related deaths, is decreasing over time, making the threat of war increasingly unlikely.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.4.2. Con: Each citizen should be free to choose what means, if any, are appropriate to protect themself from alien threats.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.4.2.1. Con: The nature of these threats renders this impractical. Individual citizens cannot protect themselves against the sort of threats that other states can pose.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.4.2.1.1. Con: That it is impractical does not justify removing this freedom. Citizens may still choose to voluntarily join the military in efforts to protect their state and by extension, themselves.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.4.3. Con: The state is often poor at correctly assessing the nature of alien threats.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.4.3.1. Con: Even if the state occasionally incorrectly assesses the extent of a threat, they are still better placed to make this evaluation than any individual citizen.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.5. Con: Citizens are not morally obligated to put their lives on the line for the state.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.5.1. Con: The state ensures that its citizens are safe for most of their life. It is fair to demand that citizens spend a small portion of their life to help the state protect its citizens and possibly end up putting their lives on the line.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.5.2. Con: If citizens are happy to allow others to put their lives on the line to protect all, it is unclear why they shouldn't also be expected to do so.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.5.2.1. Con: Even if they are unhappy with it, people do not have the right to prevent others to put their lives on the line to protect all. Because people do not have any say in how others spend their lives, it is unfair to act as if they do.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.5.2.2. Con: If people are willing to let other people do something, that does not imply that those people can be expected to do that thing. For example, many people are happy to allow others do donate to charity, but that fact doesn't obligate them to also donate to charity.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6. Con: Debt requires prior consent. Citizens have no ability to choose the state they are born in to, and as such, do not have any obligation to serve the state.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1. Con: This is an argument against all forms of civic duty. However at the point that we accept the existence of other duties, there is no principle reason why service could not also be included.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.1. Con: Other forms of civic duty are not as invasive, dangerous or time consuming as military service.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2. Con: Citizens already contribute to their society by paying taxes and abiding by its laws.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.1. Pro: Beyond legal requirements, citizens also contribute to society through their work.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.1.1. Pro: People who work in manufacturing industries directly contribute to the happiness of citizens by producing goods they consume.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.1.2. Pro: Producers of all forms of art positively impact upon the culture of society which is beneficial for the health of its citizens.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.1.3. Con: Citizens get paid wages as compensation for the work that they do, balancing out their contribution.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.2. Pro: Taxes are paid relative to individual circumstances, rather than equally, as this is a more efficient way of contributing to society. Conscription would not follow this logic of efficiency.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.2.1. Con: This progressive form of taxation is not due to efficiency but instead capacity: it would be impossible for everyone to pay the same tax. The same is not true for service as everyone could contribute equally.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.2.1.1. Con: People vary in physical capabilities and competence.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.2.1.1.1. Pro: Those who are physically weaker cannot contribute as much as those who are in good physical shape.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.2.1.1.2. Pro: Intelligent people and well-educated people are better positioned to be able to contribute more during their service than those who are less intelligent or lack technical skills that are useful for service.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.2.1.1.2.1. Con: There is no reason to believe that those who are more intelligent will automatically do better in an environment such as the military.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.2.1.1.2.1.1. Pro: More intelligent people may be less suited to the pace and style of the military meaning that they in fact do worse than some of their fellow recruits.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.2.1.1.2.1.1.1. Con: More intelligent people could be used in roles that require a sharp intellect, such as overseeing and developing military tactics or administrative assignments.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.2.1.1.3. Pro: Some people have an easier time to learn and execute tasks than others. This means that some will be able to more quickly learn their assignments and will do a better job executing it while serving.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.3. Con: There is a suite of ways in which governments can expect their citizens to contribute. Just because they pay taxes does not mean they should not contribute in other ways as well.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.3.1. Pro: The taxes that citizens pay are spent on improving the lives of the very same citizens. This should not be considered a significant contribution to society.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.3.1.1. Pro: In this way, compulsory service is directly analogous to paying taxes: as a beneficiary of a service from the state, one must directly contribute to the maintenance of that service.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.3.1.1.1. Con: The maintenance of service provided by the state is supposed to be covered through taxes. If current taxes are insufficient then it would be more reasonable to raise taxes than to force people to perform labor on behalf of the state.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.2. Con: Citizens may not choose the state but they do benefit from the state. Given this it is reasonable to exact some form of payment from them.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.3. Con: This logic leads to implausible conclusions. It would justify someone breaking any law they did not agree with as they did not choose their state.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.3.1. Con: There is a difference between positive and negative rights and duties. In this case, it is legitimate to force citizens to inaction \(by stopping them commit crimes\) but not to force them to action \(by requiring service\).
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.3.2. Con: Breaking the law can be justified if the law is immoral.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.4. Con: Citizens do not choose the state they are born to, but they implicitly consent to the rules imposed by the state and the reciprocal obligations imposed by the state by choosing not to leave.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.4.1. Con: Immigration is too expensive for the majority of human beings on the planet to be a real option. As such, most people cannot make the conscious decision to leave the state in which they are born.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.4.2. Con: People have intimate, deep-rooted connections to other people and support systems within their own countries, which make it an unreasonable burden to impose that they leave if they do not respect oppressive laws.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.4.3. Con: Compulsory service acts upon people at the age of 18. In order to avoid the service they would need to make the choice to leave at an age younger than we recognise their ability to consent. This makes it an impossible choice.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.4.4. Con: If people were to leave, they would end up living in another state with similar restrictions.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.5. Pro: Non-adult citizens lack the legal autonomy to choose whether or not they wish to accept the benefits and privileges they receive from the state in return for serving their country.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.6. Con: Then, given that the state has no ability to choose who is born into it, the state has no obligation to its native-born adult population, and state-provided public services, welfare and justice can be denied to those who refuse conscription.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.7. Con: In reality, not all members of society are treated equally under conscription. This 'moral duty' would thus only be served by those who could not escape the draft.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.7.1. Pro: Rich families or politicians could pull strings and have their children assigned to non-combat roles or to relatively peaceful countries or even exempted from service altogether to keep them out of harm's way.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.7.1.1. Pro: [George W. Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Bush_military_service_controversy) used his influence to line up an enlistment in the local National Guard unit. He rarely showed up for duty and effectively sat out the Vietnam War, even though conscription was in place.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.7.1.2. Pro: [Alan Clark](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alan_Clark), whose family was extremely rich and well connected, [found him a way out of serving](http://www.independent.co.uk/news/uk/home-news/dodging-national-service-a-dishonourable-tradition-2029753.html). As a schoolboy, he joined a training regiment a short walk away in Windsor, which he left before he went to college. He later used this experience as a pretext to get himself exempted from National Service.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.7.1.3. Pro: [A survey](http://A survey conducted by the government-run Korea Research Institute for Vocational Education and Training \(KRIVET\) last summer showed that young men from wealthier, better educated families are more likely to avoid military service. Read more at https://asiancorrespondent.com/2014/03/koreans-shun-military-service-as-survey-shows-rich-dodge-draft/#F79EbLf27XUhKEgR.99) conducted by the government-run Korea Research Institute for Vocational Education and Training \(KRIVET\) last summer showed that young men from wealthier, better educated families are more likely to avoid military service.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.7.1.4. Pro: [Donald Trump](https://www.nytimes.com/2016/08/02/us/politics/donald-trump-draft-record.html?_r=1) is suspected to have given a false medical diagnosis of bone spurs in his heels resulting in in a coveted 1-Y medical deferment from the Vietnam War.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.7.2. Con: The problem of exemptions could be dealt with by creating stricter laws that make it harder to escape the draft.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.8. Pro: States provide emergency services and take responsibility for their citizens in the face of disasters, major accidents or economic crises.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.9. Con: Many citizens are oppressed by their states. Ethnic minorities, women, and other groups suffer as a result of ingrained patriarchy, racism, and systems of privilege which exclude them. These citizens do not have a moral duty to serve a state complicit in, or passive towards, their marginalization.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.10. Con: -> See 1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.6.1.2.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.11. Con: This doesn't apply in the case of failed states. Refugees are people fleeing failed states.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.11.1. Con: People are granted some rights and privileges through citizenship, even in most cases of failed states.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.11.1.1. Pro: Even failed states normally have some institutions that allows most people to be a part of that society and live their lives in peace.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.11.2. Con: Citizens of a state that has ended up failing have a moral duty to reclaim and rebuild that state.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.11.3. Pro: The major source countries of refugees are de-facto [failed states](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Fragile_States_Index), for example Syria, Afghanistan, Somalia and South Sudan. It is unlikely that those who flee these countries could make much of a difference under these conditions.
1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.11.4. Con: Failed states have not perpetually been in a state of failure. Their citizens received benefits from them before the state failed, and this is sufficient to generate a duty to aid those states.
1.6.10.1.2.1.3. Con: Most refugees are hardly able to fight in a war.
1.6.10.1.2.1.3.1. Pro: Of the [refugees from South Sudan](http://data.unhcr.org/SouthSudan/regional.php), which is among [the three countries](http://www.unhcr.org/figures-at-a-glance.html) where most refugees come from, merely 16% are adult men; two thirds of refugees are under 18.
1.6.10.1.2.1.3.2. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.1.2.1.
1.6.10.1.2.1.3.3. Con: -> See 1.6.10.1.2.1.1.1.1.3.
1.6.10.1.2.1.4. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.1.
1.6.10.1.2.1.5. Con: Most of the conflicts that have sparked the current refugee crisis are too convoluted and protracted that there is little prospect for any of them being resolved soon. For civilians, there is really no point in participating.
1.6.10.1.2.1.5.1. Pro: The Syrian civil war has been waged since 2011 and [involves](http://www.bbc.com/news/world-middle-east-39528673) pro-regime forces, anti-regime rebels, ISIS terrorists, Kurdish forces, and half a dozen of foreign powers, none of which have been able to make decisive advances.
1.6.10.1.2.1.5.2. Pro: South Sudan, which is the origin of [more refugees](http://www.unhcr.org/figures-at-a-glance.html) than only two other countries, has experienced a [civil war](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/South_Sudanese_Civil_War) since 2013 that involves various groupings, outside forces as well as UN troops, and went through several failed peace agreements.
1.6.10.1.2.1.5.3. Pro: -> See 1.3.6.4.1.3.
1.6.10.1.2.2. Con: -> See 1.6.10.1.2.1.5.
1.6.10.1.2.3. Con: An exception must be made for ongoing, violent conflicts that present an imminent threat to the safety and well-being of refugees. If someone's basic survival needs cannot be met in their home country, they must be able to relocate themselves and their families to safer areas, even if only temporarily.
1.6.10.1.3. Pro: As the native population growth in high-income countries decreases and populations even decline, one solution can be openness to migration for the purpose of expanding the workforce.
1.6.10.1.3.1. Con: In the U.S., [refugees overall are not becoming an integral self-sustained and contributing part of the workforce:](https://refugeeresettlementwatch.wordpress.com/refugee-resettlement-fact-sheets/)"Public Assistance Utilization Among refugees who arrived during the 5 years previous to the survey 57.7% are on government medical assistance such as Medicaid...70.2% are receiving food stamps, 31.6% are in public housing \(an additional percentage is on a public housing waiting list\), and 38.3 % are getting cash assistance such as TANF or SSI."
1.6.10.1.3.2. Con: High-income countries rely on a workforce of highly skilled workers. Refugees will generally not be able to fill these roles.
1.6.10.1.3.3. Con: In Turkey, about [one sixth](https://reliefweb.int/report/turkey/eu-funded-cash-assistance-programme-reaches-half-million-refugees-turkey) of the ~3 million refugees are on cash assistance, and it is [very difficult](https://www.npr.org/2017/08/14/543477446/in-turkey-syrian-workers-struggle-to-obtain-official-employment) for refugees to join the labor force.
1.6.10.1.3.4. Con: Automation can replace workers if there is a workforce shortage.
1.6.10.1.3.5. Con: Unemployment is often a more realistic concern than no available workforce.
1.6.10.1.3.6. Pro: [High-income nations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Developed_country#High_income_UN_members) with [population decline](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_decline#Contemporary_decline_by_country) include Japan, Italy, Croatia, Latvia, and to some extent Russia.
1.6.10.1.3.7. Con: Many European countries already suffer from [unemployment](http://ec.europa.eu/eurostat/documents/2995521/8939932/3-31052018-AP-EN.pdf/a5d2c7ec-01c3-4cf8-ba33-a2ccadd91b93) and, in particular, youth unemployment. Additional workers from abroad are therefore hardly necessary.
1.6.10.1.3.8. Con: European countries not only have low fertility rates but many also have high unemployment. If the locals struggle to find jobs, how would expanding the workforce with immigrants be positive?
1.6.10.1.3.9. Pro: Across almost all industrial nations, the share of the population at working age \(15-64\) is expected to decline significantly in the coming decades \([OECD, p. 14](https://www.oecd.org/eco/outlook/2060%20policy%20paper%20FINAL.pdf)\).
1.6.10.1.3.10. Con: Refugees are not an economic way to expand the workforce as they cost money to resettle.
1.6.10.1.3.10.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.5.
1.6.10.1.4. Pro: High-income nations definitionally have greater discretionary resources, thus it is less onerous for their overall welfare to host refugees, producing a condition with the fairest balance of bearable benefit to beneficiaries \(refugees\) and bearable disadvantage to the disadvantaged class \(hosts\).
1.6.10.1.4.1. Con: High-income countries may also experience circumstances under which they can't spare money, for example economic slumps.
1.6.10.1.4.1.1. Con: A high-income country in a slump still has greater resources than a low income country.
1.6.10.1.4.1.1.1. Pro: Even during the [great recession](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Recession#Effects) \(2007-2009\), US disposable personal income [did not decrease](https://www.statista.com/statistics/710209/us-disposable-income/) \(it just levelled off and didn't grow\).
1.6.10.1.4.1.2. Con: This assumes that other national spending is more important than aid for refugees, [which](https://www.kialo.com/should-high-income-countries-take-in-refugees-2214/2214.0=2214.1-2214.2476-2214.3189) [is](https://www.kialo.com/should-high-income-countries-take-in-refugees-2214/2214.0=2214.1-2214.2476-2214.3189) [debatable](https://www.kialo.com/should-high-income-countries-take-in-refugees-2214/2214.0=2214.1-2214.2476-2214.3189).
If they are considered equal, then a country in a slump would just reduce all public spending - including refugee aid but also military, welfare, and other international aid schemes.
1.6.10.1.4.2. Con: -> See 1.4.6.5.
1.6.10.1.5. Con: Taking refugees in is bad for the environment. Refugees migrating to developed countries increase their carbon footprint manifold and litter their paths. They also cost extra emission in traffic, planes, boats, etc.
1.6.10.1.5.1. Pro: The [carbon dioxide emissions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_carbon_dioxide_emissions_per_capita) per capita in countries like Germany are thirteen times as high as in Afghanistan, five times as high as in Syria, and twice as high as in Iraq.
1.6.10.1.5.1.1. Con: Refugees are likely to have a lower carbon dioxide footprint than other residents of high-income countries as they are unlikely to engage in some of the [expensive activities](https://whatsyourimpact.org/greenhouse-gases/carbon-dioxide-emissions) that produce CO2, for example long-distance flights and driving a car.
1.6.10.1.6. Pro: Several countries such as Australia, the US, and large parts of Europe have relied heavily on refugees for the establishment, survival, and growth of their countries.
1.6.10.1.7. Pro: Some refugees are highly skilled individuals who are very productive in their host countries. There is a net benefit for the host country, and the world as a whole, because these individuals contribute to their economic and scientific growth.
1.6.10.1.7.1. Pro: [Albert Einstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albert_Einstein), who is widely known as one of the most intelligent people in history, was a German theoretical physicist who became a refugee in the US after Hitler rose to power and was unable to return home due to being Jewish.
1.6.10.1.7.2. Con: But how can the host country control these skilled individuals? It would be different if the incoming flow was controlled by the demand for labor. In the uncontrolled option, there is no reassurance or clarification of the positive effect of this incoming flow.
1.6.10.1.7.3. Con: It would be beneficial to the country that these people are leaving, for the higher-skilled individuals to stay and use their talents to improve the conditions of their own community so that the less skilled individuals, no longer need to flee.
1.6.10.1.7.4. Con: Although a tiny fraction might be very skilled, like Einstein was. Many are like the ones in the following video.[m.youtube.com](https://m.youtube.com/watch?v=acXJdWJ5Sjc&feature=youtu.be)
1.6.10.1.8. Pro: High income countries can learn a lot from other cultures, in particular those who are [focused on the well-being of the collective](http://www.eurasianaffairs.net/western-individualism-versus-the-eastern-spirit-of-community/) rather than the individual. Hosting refugees can strengthen cultural identities and develop mutual respect across countries and cultural groups.
1.6.10.1.9. Con: Helping less fortunate people survive is neither in humanity's, nor the planet's, [best interest](https://www.independent.co.uk/environment/overpopulation-is-main-threat-to-planet-521925.html), as the planet is overpopulated. If humanity stopped insisting that we save everyone, there would be fewer of us, and more resources to go around.
1.6.10.1.9.1. Con: If high-income countries do not take in refugees, this does not mean that these individuals vanish from the face of the earth. Most of them merely live dire lives in bad circumstances, for example refugee camps, in that case.
1.6.10.1.9.1.1. Pro: And even if all refugees that are not taken in would suddenly cease to be part of the global population, this would hardly change the course of the world's population prospects. According to the UNHCR, there are currently [25.4 Million refugees](http://www.unhcr.org/figures-at-a-glance.html); that's 0.3% of the world's population.
1.6.10.1.9.2. Con: Overpopulation is a disproven myth on several levels.
1.6.10.1.9.2.1. Pro: [Fertility rates](http://globalfertilitymap.com/#34.30714385628804,-31.289062500000004,2) are inversely correlated to well-being and partly to [wealth per capita.](http://databank.worldbank.org/data/download/site-content/wdi/maps/2017/world-by-income-wdi-2017.pdf) The world's wealth is increasing and in as expected, [fertility rates are decreasing](https://ourworldindata.org/fertility-rate). We are not overpopulated currently and there is no reason to think we will be in the future.
1.6.10.1.9.2.2. Pro: [Malthusian theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malthusianism) establishes the argument of overpopulation, which relies on geometric growth of population and arithmetic growth of resources. This means that in time, population will grow to a point, where resources can't satisfy the resulting demand and starvation will ensue. We have [arithmetic growth](https://data.worldbank.org/indicator/SP.POP.TOTL) of population, which removes the inevitability and allows for relatively small adjustments to balance the supply and demand. This makes the theory's prediction invalid.
1.6.10.1.9.2.3. Pro: Overpopulation and limited resources [theories](https://www.forbes.com/sites/larahoffmans/2011/10/31/7-billion-reasons-malthus-was-wrong/) never include technological progress, as its scale is unpredictable.
1.6.10.1.9.2.4. Pro: Population growth is [inversely correlated](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fTznEIZRkLg) with child survival rate. As less developed countries progress, the latter rate is increased, contributing to diminishing the former rate.
1.6.10.2. Con: The entities who would be able to help that country would not be the refugees currently living in that country, it would be other countries which that country has alliances with. Whether high-income countries should provide humanitarian aid or military aid to their allies is not the question at hand.
1.6.11. Pro: There are far too many citizens who vehemently oppose the high amount of refugees coming into their country, a permanent move that creates unfair competition for their own future generations. It is, fundamentally, the forced redistribution of those peoples land and wealth which their forefathers secured for them through work and warfare.
1.6.12. Con: High-income countries are involved in all conflicts that are going on in low-income countries. We are morally obliged to give them the help and the future that we've stolen from them.
1.6.12.1. Con: High-income countries are not involved in all conflicts going on in low-income countries.
1.6.12.1.1. Pro: Currently, South Sudan is among the [largest sources of refugees](http://www.unhcr.org/uk/figures-at-a-glance.html) worldwide. High-income countries are not involved there to a significant degree.
1.6.12.1.1.1. Pro: South Sudan produced [nearly 10% of all refugees](http://www.unhcr.org/uk/figures-at-a-glance.html) in 2018.
1.6.12.2. Con: The fact that high-income countries should reduce the number of conflicts they become involved in, often due to their desire to reduce or prevent atrocities such as dictators using nerve gas on their own citizens, has no bearing on whether they are obligated to take in refugees from those countries.
1.6.13. Con: -> See 1.5.
1.6.14. Pro: Countries may legitimately make war on one another. If it's ok to actively kill the citizens of another county with the armed services, then it cannot be wrong in and of itself to not take another country's refugees.
1.6.14.1. Con: Legitimate warfare is meant to target the military forces of other countries and to minimize the harm done to civilians. To relate this to the treatment of civilian refugees is therefore not convincing.
1.6.15. Pro: Except in case of natural disasters, refugees are refugees because of the features of their own societies and are therefore to blame for their own troubles.
1.6.15.1. Con: Many of the crises that result in refugee flows are not home-made by the country they occur in but involve a variety of external factors.
1.6.15.1.1. Pro: A [coalition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saudi_Arabian-led_intervention_in_Yemen) of various countries and led by Saudi Arabia has intervened in Yemen since 2015 and contributed to the country's crisis.
1.6.15.1.1.1. Pro: [According to the UN](https://news.un.org/en/story/2017/11/636332-un-agency-chiefs-call-immediate-lifting-humanitarian-blockade-yemen#.Wg5_mBNSxD1), the coalition's blockade of Yemen's land, air and sea ports "is making an already catastrophic situation far worse".
1.6.15.2. Con: Many refugees flee their home countries precisely because they have no power there and have never been in the position to shape these societies.
1.6.15.2.1. Pro: The roughly [700.000 Rohingya](http://The about 700.000 Rohingya refugees who fled Myanmar in 2017 can hardly be blamed for the country's) who fled Myanmar in 2017 are members of a minority that is not recognized by the government, not given citizenship and even excluded from the country's census.
1.6.15.2.2. Pro: Minorities like the Yazidis in Northern Iraq's Kurdish regions become refugees because the are targeted with [genocidal intentions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genocide_of_Yazidis_by_ISIL) and cannot count on much government support.
1.7. Pro: Refugees are desperate, vulnerable, innocent human beings. Given that every human being is of equal dignity and humanity, high-income countries must help them.
1.7.1. Pro: Refugees didn't choose where they were born. Therefore, they didn't choose to be thrust into the circumstances that led them to flee from their homelands for fear of violence, instability, or even just bleak economic opportunities.
1.7.1.1. Con: While refugees do not choose where they are born, they do have a long term choice to attempt to migrate to a wealthier country through legal methods \(work visas etc.\).
1.7.1.1.1. Con: Whilst it is possible for some people to pre-preemptively migrate, certain situations – such as war – can be volatile and unpredictable in nature. It may not be possible for a migrant to foresee such issues.
1.7.1.2. Con: Refugees not choosing their birth place doesn't deprive them of the ability to make choices leading to violent and unstable circumstances.
1.7.1.2.1. Con: Beyond their place of birth, many refugees never were in a position to make significant choices about the circumstances of their lives.
1.7.1.2.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.1.2.1.
1.7.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.7.1.2.1.
1.7.1.4. Con: Refugees who leave their country never do so completely as a result of their environment as there is always some personal choice involved.
1.7.1.5. Con: It is not the poor or disadvantaged but usually middle or better off classes who can afford to pay significant sums to human traffickers.
1.7.2. Con: Many recent refugees to Europe are neither vulnerable nor innocent.
1.7.2.1. Con: -> See 1.4.6.9.1.2.1.
1.7.2.2. Con: About half of all refugees are women. Various UN agencies [stress](http://www.unhcr.org/569f8f419.html) the "grave protection risks" that many of them face; Amnesty International [emphasizes](https://www.amnesty.org/en/latest/news/2016/01/female-refugees-face-physical-assault-exploitation-and-sexual-harassment-on-their-journey-through-europe/) the prevalence of physical assault, exploitation and sexual harassment.
1.7.2.2.1. Con: These numbers do not necessarily represent the situation in high-income countries. In 2015, seven out of ten arrivals [in Sweden](https://www.politico.com/magazine/story/2016/01/europe-refugees-migrant-crisis-men-213500) were male, and even 90% of the unaccompanied minors.
1.7.2.2.1.1. Con: This statistic concerns the number of refugees who reach Europe in the status quo, where there are substantial physical and legal barriers to doing so, not the number of refugees displaced from their home countries globally. Were high-income countries to lift these barriers and take proactive steps to bring refugees to their countries, these statistics would likely equalise.
1.7.2.3. Pro: The mere fact of suffering doesn't make a person innocent. Quite often people suffer the consequences of their own actions. Imagine two militant groups fought, one won, the other had to flee. Some of them may be innocent \(children\), some may have committed crimes.
1.7.2.4. Pro: Many migrants who arrive in high-income countries are economic migrants who come for economic opportunities, not refugees who come out of fear and desperation.
1.7.2.4.1. Con: In the case of Germany, more than two thirds of the refugees who arrived in 2016 were from Syria, Afghanistan or Iraq. 90% had their asylum applications accepted or were at least granted refuge as it was concluded that they cannot be sent back to their home countries \([BAMF, p. 18, 38](https://www.bamf.de/SharedDocs/Anlagen/DE/Publikationen/Broschueren/bundesamt-in-zahlen-2016-asyl.pdf?__blob=publicationFile)\).
1.7.2.4.1.1. Pro: Refugees from certain countries, for example Syria, can hardly be described as economic migrants. Germany in 2016 received 295.040 asylum applications from Syrians, only 0.1% of which were rejected as the applicant was certainly not deserving protection \([BAMF, p. 38](https://www.bamf.de/SharedDocs/Anlagen/DE/Publikationen/Broschueren/bundesamt-in-zahlen-2016-asyl.pdf?__blob=publicationFile)\).
1.7.2.4.2. Pro: In Germany, as of 2015, arrivals from Albania were the largest groups of refugees after Syrians. 80% of them were rejected as their home countries are considered not dangerous enough to warrant general asylum \([BAMF, p. 18, 38](https://www.bamf.de/SharedDocs/Anlagen/DE/Publikationen/Broschueren/bundesamt-in-zahlen-2016-asyl.pdf?__blob=publicationFile)\).
1.7.2.4.3. Con: -> See 1.3.13.1.3.
1.7.2.4.4. Pro: In 2016, across the EU-28 countries, [two out of every five](http://ec.europa.eu/eurostat/statistics-explained/index.php/Asylum_statistics) applications for asylum were rejected and applicants neither accepted as refugees nor granted subsidiary protection status or an authorization to stay for humanitarian reasons.
1.7.2.4.5. Con: -> See 1.1.8.3.1.1.
1.7.2.5. Con: Refugees are, by definition, vulnerable and innocent.
1.7.2.6. Pro: Countries cannot be obligated to accept refugees unconditionally. Some people claim refugee status in bad faith, and show contempt for the laws, customs, and civic principles of their host country. If generalized risk factors can be identified for this behavior, scrutiny is warranted.
1.7.2.7. Pro: Individuals who made it beyond the first safe country accessible to them - which usually excludes high-income countries - can hardly be considered "vulnerable" anymore. After all, they have a chance to stay in a safe place.
1.7.3. Pro: Our reluctance to help refugees is mostly due to our inability to actually conceptualize their situation. When their situation does become clear - as with the death of [Aylan Kurdi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Death_of_Alan_Kurdi) - we begin to realise that we do have obligations to help them.
1.7.3.1. Con: This assumes that people are reluctant, and that reluctance is based on ignorance or lack of information or sympathy.
1.7.3.1.1. Con: The fact that public opinion shifted drastically when the image of Aylan Kurdi became famous is evidence for the statement that people's sympathy depends on the availability of information.
1.7.3.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.6.8.3.
1.7.3.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.6.8.2.
1.7.3.2. Pro: [Research from Austria](http://ftp.iza.org/dp9790.pdf) suggests that exposure to and contact with refugees decreases the political support for right-wing, anti-refugee parties.
1.7.3.2.1. Con: Other [research from Greece](https://www.kcl.ac.uk/sspp/research/economics/People/MatakosGoldenDawn-DMHX.pdf) points in the opposite direction: contact with refugees leads to more support for right-wing parties.
1.7.3.2.1.1. Con: This study was conducted on Aegean islands that saw a massive and sudden influx of refugees, a situation that is hardly representative of what citizens of most high-income countries experience.
1.7.3.3. Pro: [Research suggests](http://knowledge.wharton.upenn.edu/article/to-increase-charitable-donations-appeal-to-the-heart-not-the-head/) that people's empathy only becomes properly triggered when they are confronted with a personal story about someone in need.
1.7.4. Con: It should not be the responsibility of a nation to take care of another nation's citizens. Goodwill should be enacted via NGOs or international governing bodies like the EU \(EU helping refugees from EU member-states\) or the UN \(UN helping refugees from UN member-states\)
1.7.4.1. Pro: This will also reinforce existing cultural and social values for greater and long-term domestic stability. A nation should have the right to self-determinate.
1.7.4.2. Con: Neither NGOs nor international organizations can provide what it takes to help people who are forced to leave their home country: a place to stay. The support that these organizations can provide for refugees without reliance on nation states is therefore very limited and of little immediate use.
1.7.4.3. Con: Most high-income countries are part of the EU and/or the UN. Only with the goodwill of each nation can these organizations make the refugee crisis a priority and put in place a real action plan. Without an international action plan, goodwill acts are nothing but anecdotal opinion calming efforts. Still, as international bodies they can provide guidelines and organize joint operations abroad or at borders. All else \(shelter,food, work permit\) is to the moral and ethical discretion of each nation.
1.7.5. Con: -> See 1.1.8.3.1.
1.7.6. Con: Claims like these would need to be universalized across all scales. The small scale version of this is that those who make more money have a moral obligation to take in homeless people into their homes.
1.7.6.1. Con: There is a clear difference of scale here: the discussion applies only to the obligations of countries as a whole, not to that of other organizations or even individuals.
1.7.6.2. Con: The situation of a homeless person is different than the situation of a refugee: a refugee is both homeless and unable to safely stay in their country of origin.
1.7.6.3. Con: Wealthy individuals who have the means should indeed take in and shelter homeless people.
1.7.6.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.7.1.2.
1.7.7. Pro: Basic humanitarian principles, such as those found in [international humanitarian law](https://ec.europa.eu/echo/who/humanitarian-aid-and-civil-protection/humanitarian-principles_en) demand that we give them a safe place to live and protect them.
1.7.7.1. Pro: According to basic humanitarian principles, those who can should help those in need.
1.7.7.2. Con: It is by no means obvious what counts as a humanitarian principle and what this means in practice. Persistent disputes about issues like abortion and euthanasia illustrate this.
1.7.7.2.1. Con: Abortion and euthanasia are ambiguous and difficult because they are borderline cases, situated at the boundary between life and death. No such ambiguity exists in the application of humanitarian principles to the refugee crisis.
1.7.7.3. Con: Even [basic humanitarian principles](https://ec.europa.eu/echo/who/humanitarian-aid-and-civil-protection/humanitarian-principles_en) as defined by the Geneva Convention can come into dispute with each other.
1.7.7.3.1. Pro: Showing humanity to one side can be seen as violating neutrality towards another side, as - for example - contentious decisions about granting asylum illustrate \(e.g. in the case of [Turkish officers](http://www.aljazeera.com/news/2017/03/turkey-norway-officers-asylum-170322221445983.html) accused of plotting a coup\).
1.7.7.4. Pro: The Geneva Convention lists as one humanitarian principle that "human suffering must be addressed wherever it is found, with particular attention to the most vulnerable." \([European Commission](https://ec.europa.eu/echo/who/humanitarian-aid-and-civil-protection/humanitarian-principles_en)\)
1.7.7.4.1. Con: "Wherever it is found” means the suffering country \(eg. war zone in Syria, civil war in South Sudan\), not Europe. Hence, if the GC was to be adhered to, it would require us to improve the conditions in the countries of origin of refugees, not to take them in.
1.7.7.4.1.1. Con: Since it is primarily about addressing human suffering, it should be focusing on how to do that as quickly as possible. Fixing the conditions of war-torn countries will take long time while providing safety, food and shelter for the refugees can be done right away by taking them in.
1.7.7.5. Con: -> See 1.6.7.2.2.
1.7.7.6. Con: Not every country operates under humanitarian principles. Many countries partake in activities like actively causing harm in the form of state sponsored terrorism, or not providing basic healthcare and education for the needy which contradicts humanitarian principles.
1.7.7.6.1. Pro: Under the leadership of Richard Nixon and Henry Kissinger in the 1960s and '70s, the US adopted [Realpolitik](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Realpolitik) as their primary policy for international diplomacy. Since it is a policy based on pragmatic considerations rather than moral ones, it led to many political decisions that could be viewed as non-humanitarian.
1.7.7.6.1.1. Pro: Realizing that the Vietnam War was a lost cause, Nixon and Kissinger [applied Realpolitik](https://https://www.theatlantic.com/magazine/archive/2005/06/the-craft-and-craftiness-of-henry-kissinger/304011/) in order to secure an exit that would preserve the credibility of American power. This meant negotiating with Vietnamese communists under the threat of violent retaliation should they not compromise. Ultimately, this policy continued the conflict until 1973; and although the US managed to preserve its image, it came at the cost of thousands of lives.
1.7.7.6.1.2. Pro: Fearing the spread of communism in South America in the 1970s, the US initiated a [bloody coup](https://www.gilderlehrman.org/history-by-era/seventies/essays/henry-kissinger-and-american-foreign-policy) against Salvador Allende, the democratically elected socialist leader of Chile. Under American support, the government that replaced him started a program of organized terror to imprison and murder all opposition -- even those who were non-communist.
1.7.7.6.1.3. Pro: From 1965-1973 the [bombing campaign](https://www.jacobinmag.com/2015/04/khmer-rouge-cambodian-genocide-united-states/) that the US led against Cambodia in an effort to eliminate North Vietnamese strongholds cost an untold number of civilian lives and laid the groundwork for the Khmer Rouge to secure power, leading to the Cambodian genocide of 1975-79 and the infamous "killing fields".
1.7.7.6.1.4. Pro: Under Realpolitik, the US supported and expedited the [Indonesian invasion of East Timor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Indonesian_invasion_of_East_Timor#US_involvement) \(which cost upwards of 100,000 lives\) as well as the [Bangladesh genocide](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/1971_Bangladesh_genocide) \(which cost upwards of 300,000 lives\) because these were considered to be in America's best national interest.
1.7.7.6.1.5. Con: Nixon and Kissinger's tenure in terms of foreign policy is [typically seen](https://www.newyorker.com/news/news-desk/does-henry-kissinger-have-a-conscience) as grossly immoral and an abhorrent way for a state to behave.
1.7.7.6.1.5.1. Con: Although many people feel this way, there are also many people who consider Kissinger to be one of the greatest [political masterminds](https://www.politico.com/magazine/story/2015/10/henry-kissinger-history-legacy-213237) in recent time.
1.7.7.6.2. Pro: The US post WW2 [brought over](http://www.smithsonianmag.com/smart-news/why-us-government-brought-nazi-scientists-america-after-world-war-ii-180961110/) scientists from Nazi Germany and Imperial Japan who had partaken in crimes against humanity for the purpose of facilitating their own scientific progress.
1.7.7.6.3. Pro: Many states [actively endorse](https://www.state.gov/j/ct/rls/crt/2003/31644.htm) state sponsored terrorism which involves actions contrary to humanitarian principles for the purpose of furthering their own political and economic goals.
1.7.7.6.4. Pro: American forces committed [abhorrent acts](http://www.motherjones.com/politics/2013/03/rape-wartime-vietnam/) during the Vietnam War. These were either covered up or soldiers were [given minimal or no punishments at all](http://www.history.com/topics/vietnam-war/my-lai-massacre) so that the American military would not suffer humiliation.
1.7.7.6.4.1. Con: It is widely recognized that war crimes are abhorrent, and that it's unacceptable behavior for a state not to strictly persecute them.
1.7.7.6.5. Con: It is irrelevant whether a state actually does or does not operate under humanitarian principles. States ought to operate under humanitarian principles either way; moral demands are independent of how people actually behave.
1.7.7.6.5.1. Con: Moral demands are based on current norms. If it is unconventional for someone to donate the entirety of their finances to charity before they die then someone who doesn't do that isn't morally wrong. Similarly, if it is the norm that countries do not act in a humanitarian fashion then countries who follow that norm are not morally complacent.
1.7.7.6.5.1.1. Con: Moral demands operate independently of conventional norms. The conventionality of slavery did not make it morally acceptable.
1.7.8. Con: High income countries owe a greater duty to their own citizens living in terrible conditions before taking care of those from other countries.
1.7.8.1. Con: You can both help refugees and citizens in distress. Doing the former does not hold a country back from doing the latter.
1.7.8.2. Pro: Across the EU-28 countries, [about 120 Million](http://ec.europa.eu/eurostat/statistics-explained/index.php/File:People_at_risk_of_poverty_or_social_exclusion,_EU-27_and_EU-28,_2005-2015.JPG) people are at the risk of poverty or social exclusion and [about 40 Million](http://ec.europa.eu/eurostat/statistics-explained/index.php/File:Sub-indicators_of_people_at_risk_of_poverty_or_social_exclusion_EU-27_and_EU-28_2005-2015.JPG) are severely materially deprived. This dwarfs the numbers of refugees who arrive in these countries.
1.7.8.2.1. Con: This does not support the fact that there is any fundamental reason to give preference to your fellow citizens. If you consider yourself as close to any other human being as to your fellow citizen, then you shouldn't let nationalities define your priorities for helping someone in distress.
1.7.8.3. Pro: The homeless population within America is [huge at around half a million people](https://www.forbes.com/sites/niallmccarthy/2016/11/25/the-u-s-cities-where-the-largest-homeless-pop-infographic/#22d0d53d4dde). They are also desperate, vulnerable people and their government owes a bigger duty to them than to refugees.
1.7.8.4. Con: No one in high income countries is at a high risk of being thrown into a war zone or drowning in the Mediterranean. The magnitude of harms faced by refugees and people in high income countries is not comparable.
1.7.8.4.1. Con: It is difficult to quantify one harm as being greater than another. In such a scenario, it is morally wrong for countries to forsake their own citizens for foreign ones especially when they can divert the same resources to help their own citizens.
1.7.8.4.1.1. Pro: High income countries face numerous domestic problems like [healthcare](http://www.treatmentadvocacycenter.org/fixing-the-system/features-and-news/2596-how-many-people-with-serious-mental-illness-are-homeless), [literacy](https://www.dosomething.org/us/facts/11-facts-about-literacy-america) and [crime](https://www.rainn.org/statistics/victims-sexual-violence). Diverting the resources used to help refugees to their own citizens could help tackle those problems better.
1.7.8.5. Con: The problems causing homelessness - mental illness, for example - are often very difficult to fix. By comparison, simply letting in refugees is easy.
1.7.8.5.1. Con: Accommodating refugees is not so simple as merely letting them in: there need to be resources in place to provide them with food, shelter, employment, and other basic needs. Otherwise they will end up on the street right beside the neglected homeless.
1.7.8.5.1.1. Pro: Beyond the basic services provided to homeless people, accommodating refugees often requires language services, security vetting, identification, and a variety of other costly services.
1.7.9. Pro: The alternative for refugees is often to [return to war-torn areas](https://www.theguardian.com/global-development/2016/sep/15/fear-forces-refugees-dadaab-kenya-worlds-largest-camp-return-conflict-zones-human-rights-watch-warns) where their lives are at severe risk. Forcing people to return to a war zone rather than grant them asylum is unconscionable.
1.7.9.1. Con: Most refugees who do not make it into high-income countries do not return to their home countries; they are merely stuck in other countries that took them in, [for example](http://www.unhcr.org/figures-at-a-glance.html) Turkey, Pakistan and Lebanon.
1.7.9.1.1. Con: -> See 1.3.2.8.1.1.1.
1.7.9.2. Con: This is presented as a binary option when other alternatives could exist.
1.7.10. Con: There are many vulnerable people in the world, such as those who are impoverished, whom high income countries could save by contributing more to foreign aid. But high income countries clearly don't have a moral duty to help all of these people merely because they are vulnerable.
1.7.10.1. Pro: The fact that we do not feel a strong moral obligation to have massive foreign aid programs indicates that we are justified in prioritising the well-being of our own citizens.
1.7.10.1.1. Con: Our feelings about moral obligations do not solely dictate our actual moral obligations: a narcissistic/selfish person may not feel the moral obligation to help a disabled person drowning in front of them, but that has little bearing on whether the obligation exists.
1.7.10.1.2. Con: -> See 1.6.8.2.
1.7.10.2. Con: High-income countries have a moral responsibility to help all vulnerable people. Using the fact they are not helping one group is a bad excuse to not help any group at all.
1.7.10.3. Con: The fact that refugees are explicitly seeking refuge in high income countries makes them different from faraway strangers in poverty: it makes the moral demand they place more proximate.
1.7.10.3.1. Pro: If somebody walks past a child drowning in a pond, the fact that they give lots of money to charity does not absolve them of a moral responsibility to save the child. This is because the nature of the moral obligation that the child generates is quite different.
1.7.10.3.1.1. Con: This analogy is flawed as the moral obligation in this case would be of a less far-reaching nature than in the case of taking in refugees. The child in question, after being saved, would be returned home - and not take up permanent residence with their rescuer.
1.7.10.3.2. Con: If fulfilling their moral obligations to refugees reduces a person's ability to fulfill their moral obligations to faraway strangers in poverty, then perhaps we should prioritise the faraway strangers.
1.7.10.3.2.1. Con: A given amount spent on refugees produces a greater and more certain benefit than the same amount sent to developing countries at the other end of the world in the hope of effecting long-term change.
1.7.10.3.2.2. Pro: The fiscal costs of settling refugees amounted to [1.35%](http://voxeu.org/article/fiscal-cost-refugees-europe) of Sweden's GDP, which is [about the same amount](https://www.weforum.org/agenda/2016/08/foreign-aid-these-countries-are-the-most-generous/) as the country spends on foreign aid.
1.7.11. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.7.1.2.
1.7.12. Con: By definition, refugees are seeking escape from oppression and danger. Safety can be found in many places in the world. If the wealth of a host country plays a part in a person's consideration to flee there, that country has no moral obligation towards the person as they conflate refugee status with economic migration.
1.7.12.1. Con: For precisely this reason, high-income countries are not taking in people who claim to be refugees but cannot prove that they are actually seeking refuge from a threat. Economic migrants are usually sent back to their home countries.
1.7.12.1.1. Pro: -> See 1.7.2.4.2.
1.7.12.1.2. Pro: -> See 1.7.2.4.4.
1.7.12.2. Con: Typically countries with higher incomes tend to be [safer](http://www.independent.co.uk/life-style/safest-countries-to-live-in-the-world-a7415221.html) than those that are underdeveloped or still developing. As such, it is inaccurate to claim that any refugee who would prefer asylum in a higher-income country is economically-driven and thus not a "true" refugee.
1.7.12.3. Con: This is a false dichotomy. As a refugee, you are already torn from your home. It would only make sense to seek both safety and opportunity. There is no reason to assume someone isn't a "true" refugee based on economic factors.
1.7.12.3.1. Con: One can be both a refugee and economic migrant, but being one doesn't mean somebody is both. Looking for economic opportunity specifically makes makes a refugee the same as any economic migrant.
1.7.12.4. Pro: It must be justified why a poorer nation couldn't have equally provided the freedom and safety that caused a refugee to escape their home, and how poorer nations are less morally obligated to do so.
1.7.12.4.1. Con: By this logic, every country should reject all refugees as there are usually plenty of alternative countries available for them to seek refuge in.
1.8. Pro: -> See 1.6.7.
1.9. Con: Taking in refugees creates security problems for high-income countries.
1.9.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.2.1.1.
1.9.2. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.3.1.1.7.
1.9.3. Pro: Most refugees arriving in high-income countries are Muslims; their cultural and religious backgrounds have led to many conflicts in the past. Admitting them to high-income countries risks spreading this propensity for conflict.
1.9.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.10.3.1.4.4.3.1.
1.9.3.2. Pro: Of the ten countries with the largest Muslim populations \(and two thirds of the world's Muslims\), every single one experienced some form of significant international conflict at some point since 2005 \([Gleditsch/Rudolfsen, p.7](http://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/2053168016646392)\).
1.9.3.2.1. Pro: Furthermore, six of these ten countries went through a period of outright civil war within the last two decades \([Gleditsch/Rudolfsen, p.7](http://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/2053168016646392)\).
1.9.3.2.2. Con: This might be explained with other factors that these countries have in common. For example, most of them [are relatively poor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_countries_by_GDP_\(nominal\)_per_capita#cite_note-5). Only two of them rank among the 100 wealthiest countries in terms of GDP per capita and none among the world's top-50.
1.9.3.3. Pro: Muslim countries have accounted for a steadily increasing share of the deaths from civil wars during the last decades. Between 2010 and 2014, about 94% of the estimated deaths in civil wars around the world occurred in countries with a mostly Muslim populace \([Gleditsch/Rudolfsen, p. 5](http://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/2053168016646392)\).
1.9.3.3.1. Con: The fact that this share has been rising indicates that other factors play a role. During the decade after the Second World War, Muslim countries accounted for next to none of the deaths that occurred in civil wars \([Gleditsch/Rudolfsen, p. 5](http://journals.sagepub.com/doi/pdf/10.1177/2053168016646392)\).
1.9.3.4. Con: Studies indicate that the Muslim nature of countries does not influence the likelihood of conflict to occur. Factors such as a lack of economic growth, a demographic youth bulge, political repression and ethnic dominance have far more impact in this context \([Sørli et al, p. 141, 155](https://www.jstor.org/stable/30045102?seq=1#page_scan_tab_contents); [Karakaya, p. 533](http://www.tandfonline.com/doi/abs/10.1080/03050629.2015.1016158)\).
1.9.3.4.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.15.2.1.3.3.4.4.1.
1.9.3.5. Con: Some of the conflicts among and within Muslim countries can hardly be blamed on the role presence of Islam. Other forces, such as America's invasion of Iraq in 2003, also play a role.
1.9.3.6. Con: Some of the conflicts among and within Muslim countries can hardly be blamed on Islam; they are the result of colonial legacies, for example the drawing of borders by colonial powers without regard to the realities on the ground.
1.9.3.6.1. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.15.2.1.3.3.4.
1.9.4. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.6.
1.9.5. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.7.
1.9.6. Con: It shouldn't cause any security problems if host countries use strong selection processes and organize the process of taking in refugees properly.
1.9.7. Con: A community that feels welcomed and included in the larger culture is more likely to report on individuals with criminal intent than one that feels like unwanted interlopers. Welcoming refugees creates  grateful allies against violence.
1.9.8. Con: People have a tendency to overestimate security risks.
1.9.8.1. Pro: The things citizens of high-income countries are afraid of [do not align](https://qz.com/898207/the-psychology-of-why-americans-are-more-scared-of-terrorism-than-guns-though-guns-are-3210-times-likelier-to-kill-them/) with the threats they actually face in their lives.
1.10. Pro: -> See 1.6.10.1.
1.11. Con: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.
1.12. Pro: Due to their wealth, high-income countries have the ability and therefore a special obligation to take in refugees.
1.12.1. Con: A country's wealth is irrelevant to whether it should take in refugees.
1.12.1.1. Pro: Refugee policy should be dictated by geographic distance and ethnic and cultural similarities, not wealth.
1.12.1.1.1. Con: High-income countries are much more capable of taking in refugees, providing them with appropriate shelter and giving them a chance to start anew in case a return home is out of question.
1.12.1.1.2. Pro: High-income countries act as an incentive to settle, not to return.
1.12.1.1.2.1. Pro: Because high-income countries provide a safer and more prosperous socio-economic environment, refugees have more reason to want to settle into high-income countries than to return to their home countries even if the environment there stabilizes.
1.12.1.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.1.6.5.1.4.1.1.
1.12.1.1.4. Pro: The member countries of the Gulf Cooperation Council, such as Saudi Arabia, Qatar and Kuwait, are close to the countries where many refugees originate from, capable of taking them in, and of cultural and religious proximity.
1.12.1.1.4.1. Con: While wealthier nations in the region may be better suited to the relocation of refugees, those countries' inaction or refusal to accept refugees in no way detracts from the moral obligation of high-income nations which are more geographically remote to offer reprieve.
1.12.1.1.5. Pro: It is difficult to distinguish between a refugee and a migrant who just pretends to be one. If a person has no official documents with them, that are verifiable and not fake, it is hard to test whether they are lying or not. This problem is avoidable by helping refugees in the area where they live.
1.12.1.1.6. Pro: -> See 1.3.4.
1.12.1.1.7. Pro: Prosperous countries should only welcome refugees if they share the country's values and are willing to play by its rules.
1.12.1.1.7.1. Con: It is impossible to make decisions on a case-by-case basis about admitting individual refugees based on their beliefs as it is not possible to determine these beliefs in a reliable manner.
1.12.1.1.7.1.1. Con: [Experts who have actually done the vetting](https://www.washingtonpost.com/posteverything/wp/2017/02/01/refugees-are-already-vigorously-vetted-i-know-because-i-vetted-them/) on refugees disagree. "those who have been working in immigration for years from opposite ends of the political spectrum are appalled" by policies that assume that determining motivations is an impossible task.
1.12.1.1.8. Con: Many countries close to the places where large numbers of refugees originate from have neither the resources nor the capacity to take them in.
1.12.1.1.8.1. Pro: A huge number of Syrian refugees have fled to neighbouring Lebanon, which, while similar in culture and ethnic population, does not have the capacity or resources to sustain such a large refugee population.
1.12.1.1.8.2. Con: With the exception of Afghanistan, not a single country in the region is classified [by the World Bank](https://blogs.worldbank.org/opendata/new-country-classifications-income-level-2017-2018) as "low income". In fact, numerous are ranked as "high income" \(e.g. Saudi Arabia, Oman and the UAE\) or as "upper middle income" \(e.g. Iran, Iraq and Turkey\). The region isn't poor.
1.12.1.1.9. Pro: Transporting refugees through countries is dangerous, in most cases, illegal and sometimes impossible.
1.12.1.1.10. Pro: Refugee policy has specific conditions regarding safety. Selectively seeking safety across a farther distance is not a valid reason and immediately removes legal obligation of any country to accept such people on the basis that their motive is different from the refugee status framework.
1.12.1.2. Pro: The decisive factor for taking in refugees should be what is best for refugees, not the wealth of any given country.
1.12.1.2.1. Con: Some countries have more resources than others, and correspondingly have a greater capacity to house and help refugees.
1.12.1.2.2. Con: The impacts on the economy of the refugee-accepting country are non-zero and need to be taken into consideration.
1.12.1.2.3. Con: "What is best" for refugees comes at a high cost for the incumbent countries. Their special status is agreed upon on a humanitarian basis, ensuring living conditions and safety. Suggesting that refugees' interests should be further defended beyond the current consensus would not be agreed upon as it goes against the principle of a country's primary duty, ensuring first and foremost its own citizens' well being.
1.12.1.3. Pro: Lower income countries can ask for financial support if they can't afford to take in refugees.
1.12.1.4. Con: Wealth and resources are a part of any moral calculation. If you don't have enough for yourself then your moral obligation is reduced. If you have great resources you can help with minimal impact on yourself.
1.12.1.4.1. Con: Countries that are taking in refugees and illegal immigrants frequently have a high rate of people in absolute poverty: [in Italy for instance](https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2017-07-14/poverty-in-italy-is-soaring) and in Greece more than 15% [of Greeks are in extreme poverty](http://greece.greekreporter.com/2016/06/09/study-fifteen-percent-of-greeks-live-in-extreme-poverty/).
1.12.1.5. Pro: Arguing that wealthier countries should take responsibility for citizens of poorer countries is akin to arguments in support of colonialism.
1.12.1.5.1. Pro: The logic inherent to this claim resembles that of "[The White Man's Burden](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_White_Man%27s_Burden)" and the idea that the mostly white citizens of high-income countries have, as a result of their universal superiority, the obligation to civilize and rescue the peoples in other parts of the world.
1.12.1.6. Pro: High-income countries should have equal responsibilities as low and middle income countries. Few exceptions should be considered, e.g., if a country democratically decides to accept or refuse; If the specific country influenced the situation that is forcing migrants to leave their country \(e.g. military intervention on refugee's origin country\). Considering that refugees motivations are not economical, economical situation of the country should not have any influence.
1.12.2. Pro: High income countries generally have more than enough resources and competence to be considered capable of taking in refugees.
1.12.2.1. Pro: The European Union could easily take in the refugees coming from the Mediterrenean routes, given that a proper distribution between member countries is agreed. In fact, from the beginning of the migrants crisis \(2015\) until the first months of 2016, the total number of refugees and migrants to the EU was [1.2 millions](http://www.unhcr.org/europe.html). This number represents only [0,2%](https://www.statista.com/statistics/253372/total-population-of-the-european-union-eu/) of total EU population.
1.12.3. Pro: -> See 1.6.10.1.4.
1.12.4. Pro: -> See 1.5.
1.13. Con: Accepting refugees encourages more people to attempt dangerous journeys to reach high-income countries, which results in more harm on net.
1.13.1. Pro: If accepting refugees causes more people to flee their home countries, this is harmful to the people left in those home countries.
1.13.1.1. Pro: Refugees, usually young people, are a necessary element to build their own countries. Taking refugees as a moral obligation would mean less support in the long run for their original countries to stabilize, develop and prosper.
1.13.1.1.1. Con: Because of infrastructure, labour in developed countries is significantly more productive - real GDP per capita in Syria is ~[15 times](https://www.cia.gov/library/publications/the-world-factbook/rankorder/2004rank.html) smaller than in the UK. Longer-term, refugees could pay for their own living costs and then continue producing more wealth than they would otherwise.
1.13.1.1.1.1. Con: In that logic, all underdeveloped countries should be emptied of people. Higher aggregate productivity per se does not make them break even in total. Furthermore, it is not coincidence why these people have not managed to develop productive infrastructure in the first place. Unless refugees significantly occupied jobs of higher productivity or became entrepreneurs net taxpayers, their existence would be economic loss for original taxpayers.
1.13.1.1.2. Con: Refugees are, by definition, at risk of persecution, often by their own country. It is insensible to expect these people to stay and build up their home country.
1.13.1.1.3. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.1.2.1.
1.13.1.1.4. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.1.2.1.1.
1.13.1.1.5. Pro: -> See 1.4.6.9.1.2.1.3.
1.13.1.1.6. Con: Young children are likely not able to participate in the process of nation-building by voting, fighting, working, etc. If they are desperate enough to seek refuge elsewhere, that demonstrates their inability to contribute meaningfully to their own country.
1.13.1.2. Pro: Taking in refugees from poorer countries causes brain drain and depletes the potential work force of the refugees' source nation. This is not to say those countries were on track to improving; however, if everyone leaves for greener pastures, the possibility of improving the source country is removed regardless.
1.13.1.2.1. Con: Oftentimes, the refugees will send aid to their home country or return to bring the benefits of their new nation back to their origin nation. Brain drain is not an inevitable result, and accepting refugees sometimes has the opposite effect on the origin nation.
1.13.1.2.1.1. Con: Sending financial aid is not as effective as developing the country and helping it to rebuild itself up as a stable and developing nation.
1.13.1.2.1.1.1. Pro: This is why Angela Merkel [said](https://www.reuters.com/article/us-europe-migrants-germany-refugees/germanys-merkel-says-refugees-must-return-home-once-war-is-over-idUSKCN0V80IH) that refugees must return, and noted that in the 1990s, 70% of refugees from Yugoslavia returned to their home countries after the war.
1.13.1.2.2. Pro: The sending money money back home is a [source of damage](http://www.europarl.europa.eu/RegData/etudes/etudes/join/2014/433786/EXPO-DEVE_ET\(2014\)433786_EN.pdf). When you send money to a country, you are not sending investigative journalism and you are not sending education. Instead, you are sending a source of corruption and discouraging professionalism and work. It is damaging two economies at once.
1.13.1.3. Con: The harm to refugees' home countries is hypothetical and would occur in the future. This should not be a reason to reject people who face present and assured harms.
1.13.1.3.1. Pro: -> See 1.6.10.1.2.1.2.5.11.3.
1.13.1.4. Con: Many refugees flee their home countries because they are oppressed, persecuted, discriminated against or outright driven out. It is unlikely that any of them would be in a position to contribute much to these countries.
1.13.1.4.1. Pro: Groups like the Rohingya in Myanmar, hundreds of thousands of which [have fled](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rohingya_people#Rakhine_State_conflicts_and_refugees_.282012.E2.80.93present.29) to neighboring Bangladesh and other countries, are oppressed and persecuted to the extent that they are [not even recognized as citizens](https://www.cfr.org/backgrounder/rohingya-migrant-crisis) in Myanmar.
1.13.1.4.2. Pro: -> See 1.13.1.1.2.
1.13.1.4.3. Con: In these instances the high-income countries should invest in refugee programs in surrounding countires \(e.g. Jordan for Syrian citizens\) so the oppressed will still feal connected with their culture and have an interest/ability to return when they can contribute. So there is a posibility for their counties future.
